ful information for every sufferer from the following com-

Decay, Bashfulness, Excessive Heart Palpitation, Des-
pondency, Impure Blood, Liver and Kidney Complaints,
Contagious Diseases, and various other symptoms experi-
enced by Nervous Sufferers.
This Pamphlet is sent Free on Application,
and all in any way suffering from the above would derive
benefit from the perusal of this work.

in need of treatment, he should lose no time in writing
me a letter detailing in full his symptoms, on receipt of
which I will at once reply, explaining the nature of his
complaint, and if I can effect a cure, the time it will take,

treatment of Nervous Diseases has made me thoroughly
conversant with all their peculiarities. I am prepared,

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted-*
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
lommunieations will be regarded as strictly confidential.

'he address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling —which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, JULY 11, 1891.
Payment of Members and the Labour
Party.
Among the many causes that have contributed
to the success of the Labour-party in the recent
New South Wales elections there are two which
tower high above the others. The Payment of
Members’ Act is one—the other, the lesson
taught by the late strike. Among the enemies
to the Act alluded to, who would gladly repeal
the measure that brought the poor but thought-
ful wage-earner to the front, there has been a
slight simmer of hope, now practically dissi-
pated, that, by the ingenuity or the chicanery
of lawyers, certain words in it might be so
warped as to destroy its intention and restrict
its action in the late Parliament. In construing
or interpreting an Act, the judges, of course,
must be guided by the words before them. If
through intention, or inability, or negligence,
the Legislature chooses to use words ambigu-
ously, that is not the fault of the judges. But
suppose the worst—suppose an authoritative
opinion to be obtained that the Act to remune-
rate members began and ended with the last
Parliament, the capitalistic triumph would be
but short. Another measure, whose provisions
could not be misunderstood, would be at once
passed. Delays might follow. The Upper
House—the very name is droll considering the
persons who squat on its seats—might pause,
dawdle, reject. The Governor even might be
E revailed upon to refuse the creation of a fresh
atch of nominees intended to pass the new
measure. Should he do so he would have to
seek other advisers, and, as they could not be
found, his choice, in the famous words of Gam-
betta towards MacMahon, would lie between
submitting and resigning.
• # •
In Victoria the passing of the Act for re-
munerating members has never yet had the effect
produced by the similar measure in New South
Wales. It has never yet succeeded in creating
a solid labour phalanx with a clearly-defined
policy ; never resulted in returning a block vote
of one-fourth of the representatives pledged to
the workers’ cause, or, in other words, commis-
sioned to make or unmake Governments which
would not lend a willing ear and an active
response to the toilers’ demands. This was
partly by reason of the backward state of prole-
tarian political thought in the southern pro-
vince twenty years ago, and partly, perhaps
mainly, because of the Berrys and other
smell-feasts who gabbled in Melbourne by-
streets, so that they might ultimately grovel in
London boudoirs. In a word, Victorian
Democracy was sold in order that a few of
those flatulent ones who climbed to place and
power over its shoulders might secure ginger-
bread titles and play the congenial part of
harlequins at Courts, where their greasy presence
gave rise to a flutter of fans or a raising of eye-
glasses. It is different with New South Wales.
The uses of adversity are sweet, and in the
late strike which prima-facie ended so badly for
the workman, there was brought home to him
this suggestive fact—so far as his claims were
concerned, freetraders and protectionists were
“much of a muchness.” The so-called pro-
tectionist journals that should have stood by
him, deserted him in his need. Thus the hard-
ship and suffering caused by the strike will n
have been in vain, should they but teach t
artisan and the labourer never again to trus
the individual or the journal that turned up
them in their adversity. Perhaps the drolle
feature of all is that the very broadsheets whic
a year ago lent their little pule in favour o:
Nordenfeldt guns for the suppression of popul
dissatisfaction are the precise ones in this, t
hour of labour’s triumph, to tender unsolicitec
advice as to how the party should condu
itself. Impudence is good and serviceable
its way, but it can be overdone.
# * #
That the principle of remunerating Parli
mentary delegates is not alone theoretical
sound, but practically unassailable, was we
shown during the late N.S.W. politica
struggle, and will become more obvious still
a few weeks when the session begins. The non
producers with either head or hand—the socia
spawn whose office is to eat and digest wha
others grow —are violently disturbed. T 1
idea of seeing a man legislating to-day who, bu
yesterday, was plastering rooms or wheelin
bricks, is to them by no means a pleasant on
Strangely enough, foremost among the mos
violent denouncers of the Labour-party in tl
recent wrangle, are those who have mendec
kettles, pulled beer-engines, and carried hods
days gone by. It cannot be said of them tha
they mended or pulled or carried better tha
their fellows. On the contrary, they wer
generally behindhand in all the physical virtue
involved in pulling or hauling or humping
But they possessed the one inestimable gift o;
parsimony—the gift that, in society as at pre
sent constituted (in spite of the teachings o:
Christ), is more valuable to the possessor tha
all the cardinal virtues welded into one
Strange that, to a person whose early life wa
given to house-painting and decorating, th
fact of another painter becoming a legislate
should be so offensive. By thirty or forty year
of what a few call thrift and the majority stin
the former acquires “a stake in the country
and has no necessity to work for the remainde
of his days. This remainder he would fai
devote to public affairs, having devoted so muc
to himself, and is consequently surprised whe
his ungrateful country says—-“ We’ll have non
of you. If you could not give us the raornin
of your days, when your feelings were as arden
as your cold nature would allow, we decline t
accept your dotage when individual selfishnes
has become a second nature and your onl
object in serving us is a hankering for th
glamour, such as it is, that the position wi
throw round your long life of paltriness. ”
* * *
To politics as to any other profession mei
should take when young. The Tory sneer of
‘ ‘ professional politician ” applies with equa
force to a “professional” actor, musician, sur
geon, lawyer, parson, Avriter, artist, scientist
carpenter, shoemaker, scavenger, or hangman
For that matter, what would have been the New
South Wales politicians for the last thirty year
or more but “professionals,” except that a
their best they never rose beyond clumsy ama
teurism ? Ever since the advent of the quasi
independent system under which Australia
lives our representatives have largely consistec
of men who deprecated payment to member
while privately enriching themselves at the
country’s expense, or of servile sycophants who
pompously assumed that their fair round bellies
and unstinted broadcloth would make their
unkempt English respectable. The very reflec
tion that the men who now deplore the uprising
of the “lower classes ” Avere to be seen in the
House during the last and previous Congresses
spluttering like drunken helots, or gibbering in
the tongue of Mrs. Gamp, is a lesson in
effrontery. Of all persons least calculated to
make beneficent laavs for a community commenc
us to those who by half a century or less ol
successful selfishness have become financially
independent. There is no independence about
them in the best meaning of the word. This
Avhimsical world of ours is alive with shams
but the most egregious sham of all is the tacit
assumption that the person who has been most
successful in hoarding money by means of the
labours of others is the best person to entrust
with public affairs. In the vast majority of
cases, when taken out of the little huckstering
circle in which he sat as spider in the middle
and gobbled the foolish flies that alighted on the
circumference or on the numerous and sticky
radii, he Avill be found a dunce of the purest
Avatsr. The art of governing, of law-making,
has no affinity Avith commercialism, the petty
huckstering faculty.
# * •
Concerning representative Government there
are some things that should never be forgotten,
especially by the New South Wales Avage-
earners who now, for the first time, have made
their power felt. The system of representation
has been a class institution down to this year of
grace, 1891. Men now alive and vigorous saw'
a House of Commons avliosc entire constituents
consisted of 15,000 voters in a population of
30,000,000 —a House more than half of whose
members were nominated by the Peers while
the fractional remainder were sometimes re-
turned by electorates in Avhich the candidate
was either the sole voter or one out of two or
three. Even at the present moment millions of
Englishmen have no more voice in the govern-
ance of their country than the Russian political
convicts have in the affairs of Siberia. There
are four or five labour-candidates in the Com-
mons, it is true, but these in a House of nearly
700 have about as much influence as the Italians
of 1814-15 had regarding the dismemberment of
their country at the Congress of Vienna. And
yet the Sydney journals blab in a flaccid,
sprawling way about the possibility of “class”
legislation in the newly-elected Parliament !
Why, the Commons, which Sir Henry Parkes
extols as the Avorld’s model in the Avay of a
representative institution, is the very home of
class legislation, and Avill continue so till the
British masses are not alone enfranchised, but
politically educated up to the point Avhen a
proletarian majority wdll take over charge of
the army and navy, and when the “stately
homes of England ” will be levelled with the
dust, rather than millions should starve while
thousands of square miles of the choicest land
are given over to deer and grouse. Talk of
class legislation, with such an example as the
British Lower House held up to you for admira-
tion ! Surely the working-men of Australia are
no longer to be choused by such transparent
tomfoolery !
* # •
By the man who earns his living by hand or
head, the portentous fact should never be lost
sight of that, so long as a majority of a legisla-
ture is composed of idlers, drones, fribbles, or
even men in easy circumstances, advance in the
proper direction will be blocked. The rich have
ever had legislatures in their power since the
dawn of constitutional government under the
Plantagenet Henry 111. The results are to
be seen on every hand. Over against a
sybaritism, unknown since marauding pro-
consuls entered Rome with the spoils of a
province in their train, is to be seen abjectness,
to which that of the Jews under Pharoah was
luxury. Yet the English professor, Thorold
Rogers, who devoted the best part of a life
to the subject, assures the world that, in the
14th and 15th centuries, there was a fowl in
every Briton’s pot, not on Sundays alone, but
all the week through. What extracted the
fowl and turned the husbandman out to starve on
the road-side, to be sold into slavery, branded
and whipped for his first attempt to escape, and
hanged for his second or third ? Nothing but
the class legislation of Tudor and Stuart
Parliaments, a legislation which, in a somewhat
modified form, is carried on in Britain to this
day. And yet, forsooth, the frowsy Sydney
press takes upon itself to lecture the Australian
labour representative on how he should com-
port himself ! It is time the poor got not only
a fourth but three-fourths of the legislative
power. It is no more than their numbers will
eventually give them should they not be weak-
kneed enough to be cajoled by what a W est Sydney
member not inappropriately called the “ pot-
hatted ” section of the community. That they
would use such an accession of power with more
temperance and justice than was ever meted to
them in the days of their weakness and dis-
organisation is as certain as that their regime,
would herald an era of true prosperity hitherto
unknown. There would be an occasional temp-
tation, no doubt, to order out the artillery and
troopers to prevent men in frock-coats from
crowding at Circular Quay, but it is improbable
that the cavalry would be permitted to draw
swords or the gunners supplied with anything
beyond blank ammunition. Indeed, no special
object would be attained by firing ball-cartridge.
For the Ministers who are sufficiently thick-
skinned to retain office in the present state
of parties could afford to laugh at an ordinary
sabre-cut or rifle bullet.
* * *
In view of the near approach of Federation
and the later advent of an Australian Republic,
one and indivisible, the Labour-party did well
to banish the fiscal question from its platform.
A split on the subject would have been inevit-
able, and considering the questions of equal or
greater moment pressing for an immediate solu-
tion, such a split at the outset would have been
deplorable. Besides, when provincial amalga-
mation arrives, Protection will not be far
behind. The latter, indeed, is a corollary of
the former. Again, while from our standpoint
free foreign-trade is as suicidal for an un-
federated New South Wales as it would be for
a federated Australia, we have never challenged
the fact that a country may bo so situated
as to make unrestricted commerce a necessity.
What we have denied strenuously is that the
commercial doctrines of the Manchester school
were as inexpugnable as a geometrical axiom—
were applicable to all countries, however geo-
graphically placed or commercially developed.
But without wilfully shutting one’s eyes, it is
idle to deny that penury of the same kind, if
not to the same degree, may exist in countries
whose fiscal systems differ widely. If the
squalor of protected Paris or New York is not
so widespread or abject as that of partially-
protected London (the world contains no really
Freetrade State), still it exists. This shows
that the causes of poverty lie at depths rarely
reached by foreign-trade or by protective plum-
mets. So far as Australasia is concerned, our
contention has always been, not that Protection
was a specific for social ills, but that it was a
palliative, or a move in the right direction. For
the Labour-party, then, to divide at the outset
and thus disfranchise itself over a minor affair
while momentous abuses are crying for redress
would be merely following in the footsteps
of the Irish chiefs Avho broke each other’s
heads for the love of Christ while the Nor-
man or Anglo-Saxon cut-throats were behind
the hill waiting to finish the job which the
foolish Celts had begun. One thing is certain—
nothing would be sweeter in the nostrils of
Sydney politicians and editors than the scent
of a free-light amongst the working-men’s
delegates. Thucydides ! as Mr. Daniel
O’Connor would say, how gaily the intelligent
persons on both sides of the Speaker, as well as
the f riends of the working-man at the heads of
the Sydney dailies, would sidle up with bottles
and sponges and press the combatants to fight
to a finish !
• * *
From a variety of causes then, the conflict
through which New South Wales has just
passed has been the first of its kind in the
Australasian colonies. It is probable that it
would not have occur red at all but for the provision
for remunerating members, wisely made by the
last Parliament. From all men of liberal minds,
men who see in Democracy one of the chief
methods by which the scandalous inequalities of
existing society may ultimately be assuaged or
destroyed, the late triumph has received a
hearty welcome. Not so from those whose lives
and thoughts are but a standing protest against
God for making such a mistake as to disturb
chaos by creating the world. Such persons
believe in a society of infinite gradations with
them on top rosy-gilled and daintily clad. They
believe, or affect to believe, that a person has a
perfect right to do as he likes with what is
called “ his own,” whether the latter consist of
houses or lands, of mountain, river, or sea-
coast. By the fair name of ‘ ‘ freedom of con-
tract ” they dignify the gaunt necessity that
ends in the sweating-room and the pauper’s
coffin. To creatures who see in the miseries
of the poor nothing but the working of an
inexorable law the result of the elections has
come as a shock. Still, it is but a gentle
lullaby compared with what will follow.
When education was left to the caprice of
parents, or letter-carrying to the whims of in-
dividuals the community increased in ignorance
while the chances of letters reaching their des-
tination decreased. It will be for the new
delegates to show that State interference must
be immensely extended in various directions
until gradually men come to see that they are
members of one family, that the adventitious
distinctions sought to be set up between them
for ages are no real distinctions at all, and that
the only true measures of a personal worth are
character and knowledge.
A Programme for the Labour Party.
Foe. many years—indeed, for many centuries—
the perpetual watchword of the prudent poli-
tician has been to “Go slow.” There has been
much drivel about the insertion of the “ thin
end of the ivedge; ” much solemn bosh con-
cerning the necessity for weighing all measures
of reform carefully in the balance; much
painful bogwash about the desirability of avoid-
ing velocity lest it should savour of revolution.
The only thing that the average politician does
suddenly is to suddenly remain where he was
before, and not move. When a great evil is
discovered—or rather when an evil that was
discovered in the days of the Roman Uesars
becomes more than usually pressing—the
orthodox proceeding of the orthodox kind of
Liberal statesman is to acknowledge that the
evil is one that urgently demands a remedy, and
to beseech the country to lie low lest it should
give way to “revolutionary legislation.” Then
he hangs fire for 25 years while still admitting
the extreme urgency of the case, and mean-
while his opponents complain of his indecent
rapidity, and the country is so used to this sort
of thing that it really mistakes it for haste.
By and bye a small, feeble, half-hearted measure
of reform comes before a Legislature, for no
orthodox statesman ever brings in the whole
reform at once ; and it is bounced out by the
Opposition which—under the grand old principle
of Government by Contradiction—is bound to
object even to its own theories when the
Ministry supports them. The country can’t
complain of this, for it hires the Opposition for
this purpose, and no other. Finally, after
weary years of contradiction, the scheme
escapes from the Opposition, and then it is
thrown out by the Upper House instead. The
country can’t reasonably complain of this either,
for it keeps the Upper House going for no other
purpose. The whole scheme has only one
merit —it makes things go slow—so hideously
slow, that the Legislature is even yet just
beginning to think that some day, when it is
quite rested, it may possibly get up and tinker
with measures that were found to be essential
away back in the Middle Ages. And the only
difficulty that stands in the way is the fear of
revolutionary haste—the fear that things have
not yet gone slow enough, and that some
more profound consideration is still required.
» * *
In this respect more than any other, the Aus-
tralian Labour Party differs from all the parties
that have gone before it. It means to go fast.
Apart from this fact, there is really little differ-
ence between it and the other parties in Aus-
tralian politics. The Orthodox “moderate”
politician—the individual who is vaguely de-
scribed as a “right-minded person”.— accepts
the labour programme in a vague, theoretical
way. but he wants to be so unspeakably gradual
in his movements that 10,000 years would not see
it much nearer accomplishment. The ortho-
dox Conservative politician also looks forward
to the ultimate realisation of the same projects,
yet he wants to be a good deal more gradual
still. But the working class has grown tired of
this old, prehistoric drivel. It is weary of
wanting a house and receiving a post-dated
promise of a brick. It recognises that Time
travels fast, and that nations do not last long, and
that any good that is to be got out of this world
had better be got now, else the globe may burst
up, and the solar system collapse, and all things
come to an end, before progress has got fairly
started. It realises that, so long as it travels
in the right direction, it can’t go too rapidly,
for the object of all progress is to get some-
where, and the only known way to get there is
to keep moving. It has lived for years on that
tough, unsatisfactory thing known as the thin
end of the wedge, and it has found the thin end
of the wedge to be an unsubstantial article of
diet, and very much devoid of gravy. And
whereas, in the past, it has supported such poli-
ticians as Parkes, and Griffith and Robert
Stout, because they promised to do something
for it and never did anything, it now proposes
to do something for itself instead, and conse-
quently Parkes and Griffith are as super-
fluous as the leaves of last autumn, and the
party of Labour declines to act as their adver-
tisement any longer. So far, it may be, this
new force hardly knows where it is tending, or
how far it is likely to go, but its path is maxked
out for it, and it must follow it.
* * #
In the first place, the Labour party is on the
road to Independence. Willingly or unwillingly,
it must raise the standard of the Australian
Republic. The “Federation under the Crown”
will be stillborn after all, and the chief use of
the Federal Parliament, as planned out by the
Convention—if that Federal Parliament ever
exists at all—will be to furnish a convenient
central battlefield on which the question ni
Monarchy versus Commonwealth can be settled.
It will be a suitable place for the old loyalism
to die in, and a handy sepulchre ready for its
interment. And sooner or later the new party
must declare definitely for Protection against
the outer world, for the sufficient reason that
Labour cannot live by unloading nigger-manned
ships at Circular Quay, or by constructing un-
profitable public works with borrowed money,
or by competing with the cheap Chinaman or
the low-priced Hindoo on a system whereby
only the cheapest survives. The Labour-party
has already committed itself to a certain
measure of Protection—it has demanded that
Australia should be protected against the
Chinaman, and the inexorable logic of necessity
will keep it moving onward in the same direo-


It does take nine tailors to make a man :
one to measure him, one to make the clothes, seven
to collect the bill.
Concerning the ever-recurring farce of
testing, in the witness-box, ability to understand
the nature of an oath in the case of an infant, or
a blackfellow, or a Chinaman, or a J.P., Dis-
trict Court Judge Paul, of Queensland, the other
day remarked that it would be far better to in-
struct and to catechise witnesses about statutory
punishments than about hell-fire and other ini-
aginai y things. The average blackfellow's answer
as to the consequences of his telling a lie on oath
is —“ Mine go to Hell! ” with a big accent on the
last word. “ Where’s hell?” the Court asks —just
as if the Court itself knew! —and Jackey promptly
replies, “ Longa plully lock-up, my word ! ”
Ition. Presently it will also demand Protection
against the cheap human scum from the
Mediterranean and the paupers of all nations—
against every form of labour and the produce of
labour, in fact, that is imported by capital for
ithe purpose of reducing wages. In short, it
will demand the largest, widest, and most com-
prehensive form of Protection on record. If it
■does not, themthere will presently be no Labour-
party at all,' and the movement will die of
internal atrophy. And next it will legislate for
the protection of Australia from the absentee
capitalist, who is, if possible, a shade more
costly and more injurious than the imported
blackleg, inasmuch as his connection with this
country consists chiefly in buying land for an
invisible trifle per acre, in blocking settlement
while he holds it, and contributing nothing
whatever to the revenue, in getting it improved
out of loan-moneys, and then in selling it back
to the nation at an enormous price and taking
away the proceeds.
# * *
Now that Labour has become a genuine
power in politics the much-promised, much-
postponed One-Man-One-Vote principle is at
fast in sight. Above all others, this is the sort
of measure concerning which the standard
politician loves to go slow. He loves to use it
as a war-whoop in a long series of elections,
and to keep in power for years on the strength
of it, but he does not love to see it become law,
for then the war-whoop is used up, and he can-
not grind his axe upon the old shibboleth any
longer. But the Labour-party cares not one
straw though all the old hereditary barnacles of
politics are stranded for want of a war-cry, and
never get afloat any more ; and the capitalistic
plural vote is doomed. And—whether the
party has fully recognised the fact or not —the
old male monopoly of the suffrage is doomed
also, and political power must be extended to
both sexes upon equal terms. The one ruinous
blot upon the whole system of Government by
Capital is that Capital has never cared for any-
thing except its own interests ; the blot upon
Government by manhood suffrage is that the
male democracy has never attended seriously to
anything beyond its own direct interests either.
But if the Labour-party does not remedy this old
injustice it will presently find that the old Con-
servative faction will take up the question, and
will thereby be in a position to taunt the demo-
cracy itself for its conservatism, and to rebuild
some, at least, of its lost influence by passing
itself off as the new party of progress and en-
lightenment.
s * #
The enforcement by law of the eight-hour
system is naturally an essential feature of the
Labour programme, and should that prove in-
sufficient to meet the requirements of the
nation, a seven-hours’ system must be enforced
in its place ; failing even that, a six-hours’
system must do instead. For it is a self-evident
axiom that there should be work enough in the
community for all—that one million consumers
should furnish sufficient employment for one
million producers; and the existence of an
“ unemployed ” population is sufficient evidence
that some section of the nation is doing more
than its share of work, and thus leaving another
section with no occupation. Consequently the
hours of labour can only find their level
when the unemployed difficulty is extinct,
This is the first step towards the abolition
of poverty ; the next is the nationalisation of
land and the abolition of landlordism, with all
its evil concomitants of booms and syndicates,
foreign speculators, absentee proprietorship and
public ruin ; the establishment of a national
system of compulsory insurance ; the abolition
of public borrowing, which is merely absentee
proprietorship in an extended and aggravated
form ; the creation of a State Bank of issue
which would supersede to a great extent the
existing institutions that are so largely run for
the benefit of British stockholders ; and the en-
couragement of Co-operation as a substitute for
the individual employer who has been tried for
so many years and has proved so bad a failure.
* # *
Unfortunately, the present system of Parlia-
mentary chaos makes any serious reform almost
impracticable. The policy of Government by
Contradiction must go, for the arrangement
whereby the country hires one set of men to
carry out its wishes and another set to prevent
them doing it is too cumbersome and too super-
latively idiotic to be of much use. The Upper
House must go, for it is absurd to maintain the
theory of representative institutions when the
will of the people can be overruled by an
irresponsible gang of aged capitalists most of
whom were nominated by other capitalists for
reasons which will not bear inspection. The
gubernatorial veto must go also, for there is no
hope or possibility of reform when the fixed
resolve of the whole nation can be set at naught
by a foreign nonentity, in whose eyes the policy
of the Labour party is one-half blasphemy and
one-half revolution. The present system of
election needs to be formed on the French model,
so that the members returned must represent
an absolute majority of the electors, instead of
representing only the dishonest advantage
gained by a judicious splitting of the enemy’s
vote through the agency of bogus candidates.
And lastly, all questions of serious importance
must be settled by a direct vote of the people
after the manner of the Swiss Referendum ,
instead of being wi’ecked amid the -chaos of a
general when twenty political ques-
tions are mixed up together in inextricable con-
fusion, and not one of them is answered.
« # *
These are the things which Labour mostly
wants, or which it will want when it becomes
an organised force in all the Legislatures of
Australia. It demands a Federal Republic,
Protection against all the world outside this
continent and immediately adjacent islands,
the nationalisation of land, and the aboli-
tion of landlordism in all its branches, and
the government of the people by the people and
for the people in its widest sense, by the estab-
lishment of a system wherein every adult in-
habitant will have an equal voice in public
affairs. It wants to wipe out the great art and
swindle of politics, by taking the direct vote of
the whole people on all important questions ;
it wants no imported Governors nor Borrowed
generals nor hollow military pomp, no foreign
titles nor foreign capitalists, and no more foreign
loans, no cheap labour nor low-priced and
diseased emigrants from the slums of Europe
and Asia, no more religious feuds, no National
Anthem nor similar doggerel, no Upper House,
and no more party Government. It wants co-
operation in place of capitalism, national in-
surance instead of relief works and benevolent
asylums, a universal eight hours’ system, and
various other things of greater or less im-
portance, which will probably be heard of later
on. This, no doubt, is a long and numerous
programme, but it has been accumulating for
5000 years or more, and there are a good many
arrears to get up in consequence. They have
been gathering through all the ages, while pro-
fessional politicians were getting in the thin
end of that wedge which never seemed to get
in any further or any faster, and while pro-
fessional reformers were wailing about the
necessity for preserving the ancient landmarks
of the constitution ; and they have been accu-
mulating ever since the first old, useless public
fossil involuntarily voided the great idea of
going slow, and avoiding revolutionary tenden-
cies. And as that was a very, very long time
ago, when the world was still young, and the
Father of Lies was yet in his infancy, the
accumulated bill which Labour has to present
for settlement has grown somewhat formidable
in the interval.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Move On.
The accused was discharged upon his promise to leave
at once for Sydney.— Item from the Melbourne Age.
The prisoner had undergone two previous sentences for
vagrancy, bub on promising to quit the colony imme-
diately he was released with a caution.— Evening News
Item.
Prisoner was an old offender, but upon his undertaking
to quit Brisbane at once for New South Wales he was
allowed to go .—Item from Brisbane Courier.
The law of imprisonment, regarded as a cure
for crime, is admittedly a failure. It doesn’t
reform the criminal, for the same old batch of
offenders have a perpetual lease of the prisons,
and keep on inhabiting them till they die, and
another batch grows up to take their place.
And it doesn’t extirpate the criminal, for life is
tolerably healthy inside a gaol, and the food is
good, and the air salubrious, and consequently
the old offenders are a long-lived race, and last
till they reach a bad old age. The only logical
alternatives in the case are either to cure the
convict, if his moral disease is curable, or to
extirpate him, when his disease has proved
itself incurable ; but the law doesn’t know how
to do the one, and it lacks the courage or the
consistency to do the other. Consequently the
whole system, so far as the hardened criminal
is concerned, is a makeshift that answers no
visible purpose whatever. When the ancient
and hopeless drunk is “sent up” for the hun-
dredth time or so, nobody expects that he will
be improved by the experience—he is merely
sent up to get him out of the road for the time
being. He isn’t lodged in gaol as a remedy,
for his case is obviously past remedy ; neither
is he deposited there as a punishment, for,
as a rule, he rather likes being there—he is
simply despatched to prison because it is the
correct and orthodox thing to do, and the law
doesn’t know how to do anything else. But
now and then it appears that the old-established
“drunk” has grown tired of one particular
gaol, and has become weary of appearing
before the same old “beak,” and fitting his
chin into the spikes at the same old dock, and
then he generally promises to go and be a
nuisance in some other province for a change,
and, as a rule, he is released on the strength of
that assurance. Possibly it makes the Bench
feel better, and affords it a little pleasant
variety when it is enabled thus to swap drunks
with the Bench in the next capital, for even a
magistrate must feel weary of always sending
up the same band of frowsy wrecks year after
year, and would gladly get a few new outcasts
for a change. Possibly there is a faint hope in
the legal mind that if the vagrant is thus moved
on from Adelaide to Melbourne, and from Mel-
bourne to Sydney, and from Sydney to Brisbane,
and from Brisbane away up into the Far North,
and then is moved right back again till he
brings up once more in lus old haunts, he may
ultimately wear out on the road—that he may
be walked to death, or may founder at sea, or
be otherwise disposed of. Again, it may
be that the Law entertains a joyous delu-
sion that the fresh air, and the scenery
and the laughing-jackasses and other works
of Nature, may move the soul of the
sneak-thief, and strike a sympathetic chord in
the individual who has no visible means of sup-
port ; and that they may burst into tears and
become reformed characters. Or—more pro-
bable still—each P.M. wants to clear his own
particular city of its bad characters and doesn’t
care a single cent, for the feelings of the people
in the next city ; in other words, he throws his
garbage into the next man’s garden, and the
only reason why he never gets to the end of the
process is that the next man is shovelling gar-
bage just as violently as he is, and with exactly
the same end in view. Or—and this is the most
reasonable solution of all—he does it for no
particular reason at all, only he knows that he
is a failure, and the Law is a failure, and the
prisoner is the worst failure of the lot, and
therefore his one desire is to shift the responsi-
bility, and pass on the blame, and let the whole
thing slide.
A New Way to Make Railways.
Here are a few Bulletin ideas for railway
construction in the country districts. First of
all, begin by estimating the increased value
that will be given to lands in the vicinity, and
charge the proprietors, say, three-fourths of the
amount —that will pay for all the land wanted
and leave a good deal over. Don’t fence the
line—if any darned fool gets on it and the
engine walks over him it is his own look-out,
and if anybody’s cows or pigs insist on sleeping
on the line let them be squashed by the cow-
catcher and don’t pay compensation—the owner
ought to have fenced them in. Don’t have any
wayside stations ; simply put up a board with
the name of the place on it; and have no plat-
forms—any reasonably athletic person can shin
up into a carriage without one, and the cripples
can get a leg up from some good Samaritan in
the train. Have no station-master nor porter nor
ticket-seller—the guard who travels with the
train can do all that; and dispense with gate-
keepers in the country districts —the man who
can’t get across the line without being dry-
nursed by a gate-keeper might as well be dead,
anyhow. Have all the rails, locomotives, and
the like made in the country, and have all the
formation of the line done by day-labour ; and
pay for them both in notes issued by a State
Bank. Then, when the railway is finished, the
chances are that there will be some cash left on
hand, for only about half the amount extracted
from the local proprietors on the betterment
principle will have been used to purchase land,
and the other half will be left. This amount
could be used to reduce the public debt. The
notes issued to pay for construction would be
redeemed gradually out of profits—as soon as
there were any profits. Any locality that
began to pine after a railway-station could have
one—the amount to be paid out of the local
rates. Have only one class of carriages—the
trams manage to pull through on that basis,
and the trains can do the same. Then raise
Cain generally and go right ahead.
V The “Deep-toned Aye.”
Dear Bulletin, The present New South
Wales Parliament was elected because Parkes
wanted the country to give a “deep-toned
Aye ” on the subject of Federation, and accord-
ing to the broken-down fool of a constitution,
the only way that the country could do it was
to hold a general election. The result is rather
amusing when you figure it out carefully. Part
of the new House consists of Parkes’ Freetrade
party, which professes to support Freetrade and
Federation at the same time, regardless of the
fact that one of these principles must kill the
other, and that the party might as well try to
chew boiling ice and travel north-east by south.
Another section supports Freetrade without
Federation, and each of these claims to be the
only original and genuine Freetrade party, and
declares the other to be a degraded imitation.
Part of the direct Opposition wants Protection
with Federation, and part of it wants Protection
without Federation, and each of these declares
that it is the only genuine and all-wool Opposi-
tion, and that the other alleged Opposition is
shoddy and a delusion. Then, as to the Labour
party, part of it wants Freetrade and
the other part wants Protection ; also some of
it wants Freetrade with Federation, and some
more of it wants Freetrade without Federation;
likewise one sub-division wants Federation and
Protection, and the other sub-division wants
Protection and no Federation. To sum the
situation up in a general way, one-third of the
country has given its decision on the Labour
question because it considered it to be the most
important, and has virtually let the tariff and
the Federation problem go. A good deal less
than another third has given its vote on Federa-
tion and has let the tariff and the Labour ques-
tion drop altogether. And rather more than a
third has decided on the tariff question, and has
left Labour and Federation to slide igno-
miniously. The general result of all this may
be arrived at pretty well by adding one mile
three furlongs to £2 7s. 6U1., and then dividing
the result by 3cwt. 2qrs. I7lbs. The country
can’t give a decision by the electoral process on
more than one question at a time, and when
there are at least three burning questions to be
considered at once they get so badly mixed up
that there is no definite result about any of
them. The only way to find out whether the
nation wants Federation or not is to take a
popular vote on that subject alone; by the
present method the Federal question is about
eighteen times more hazy than it was when it
first started.—Yours as usual, Titus Salt.
Who Are “The People?”
Both the Sydney morning dailies, recognised
organs of capital, and slaves bound to the
chariot-wheels of their demigod, Parkes, are
undercutting each other in the bid for the
Parliamentary Labour-party’s support. Violent
loves, when suddenly conceived, are usually
vicious, and these greasy monopolistic advances
to Labour deserve no sentiment but the con-
queror’s scorn. The S.M. Herald (which
prints and issues from under its roof an evening-
paper that loses no opportunity of insinuating
that the Labour-members are unwashed ruffians
and unscrupulous loafers who live on the
ignorance and credulity of their brother men)
even in its abject whining, cannot divest itself
of the errors due to its training and class-
arrogating traditions;—
And the members of the Labour-party are the more
bound to recognise this general responsibility since it is
well known that they received a great deal of the support
which placed them where they are from outside tho
ranks of unionism and even of labour. They have re-
ceived generous and sympathetic assistance from the
people, and theymust be aware that after the first sense
of surprise was overpassed, they have received general
and cordial greeting upon their success. They are not
looked upon as anarchists and revolutionists, but as men
who come to Parliament to give direct representation to
a section of the community which has not been satisfied
with its representation in the past.
What a climb-down ! But yesterday, and the
Labour leaders were pilloried in the morning
press (as they are still being pilloried in the
Herald’s Echo) as “ anarchists and revolu-
tionists ” —as dangerous agitators to be dra-
gooned and gaoled ; now they are highly
desirable parliamentarians. Labour might have
grovelled and begged to monopoly for years
without making any impression upon the brass
bosom of its unsleeping enemy. Its fierce
revolt and its subsequent ballot-box victory
have wrung acknowledgments from the throats
of cowed foes, who would never have been
generous enough to grant justice for justice’s
sake. Yet, even in this piteous appeal to the
triumphant cause, the S.M.H. has the gall to
tell Labour that it has * ‘ received generous and
sympathetic assistance from Ihe people.''' Who,
let us ask, are “the people” in the Herald’s
estimation? The handful of capitalists? The
few dozens of squatters ? Tho city ring of
importers? The awkward squad of money-
changers and brokers? Bah ! Labour is “ the
people.” It has “received generous and sym-
pathetic assistance from ” —itself !
Munro and the Women.
Munro has repented of his scheme for granting
the suffrage to female ratepayers only, and has
started afresli on a more democratic basis. He
has drawn up a Bill of eleven short clauses —
the whole thing being about the size of one
sheet of notepaper—in which he sets up three
propositions in single file for the consideration
of the Victorian Parliament. The first asserts
briefly that every man shall have one vote and
one only ; the second, that every woman shall
have the same; the third, that any man or
woman attempting to vote more than once at
any one election shall get into trouble. The
whole affair is short, business-like, and ener-
getic, and for that very reason The Bulletin
is driven to the conclusion that Munro isn’t a
politician. Parkes is. If Parkes had such a
scheme on hand, and had absolutely nothing
else to travel on, lie would have got through
two elections on the strength of it—he would
have stumped the country on it, and dragged
his lame leg through the length and breadth of
New South Wales, and slopped over into the
adjacent provinces—he would have told eyery-
body that he was old and sorrowful, and wanted
to die, but that he would perish standing up
with the pump-handle of the State clutched in
his frozen grasp rather than let this great
reform slide—he would have looked, in fact,
like a giant who was breaking his back to hold
up a pin, and tho country would have taken it
all in. Munro has thrown away a big adver-
tisement this time ; if he had even hired a poet
and a brass band, and laid in a barrel of fire-
works it would have been better than nothing.
Also, he has made an ass of himself on a small
side issue, by giving women a vote at elections
for the Legislative Assembly only, instead of
going the whole hog at once. ' The old super-
stition about getting in “thethin end of the
wedge ” couldn’t be shaken off. It has become
such a fixed conviction that political sagacity
consists in removing one-half of an evil and
leaving the other haft standing that the average
statesman only washes one ear at a time, lest
washing them both should be revolutionary,
and should overset some great landmark of the
Constitution.
—— •» —-
A Snag Ahead.
The snag ahead for the New South Wales
Labour-party is the Upper House, and it will
require much vigorous navigation to get round
that obstacle. For several years past the
Assembly lias been growing more and more
liberal in its tendencies, and the old bigoted
fossils who were thrown out at each successive
election have been translated to the nominee
mummy chamber, so that the progressive ten-
dencies of the nation have made things in some
respects a trifle worse than they were before.
Through the amazing bull-headedness of the
cornstalk Constitution it has come to pass that,
when a legislator is too dense or too narrow-
minded or too infamously selfish to be endurable
even for three years, the country gets that same
legislator for the term of his natural life instead;
and this method of improving things by making
them worse is going to create trouble. The
Upper House has made very little noise in the
past, because the Assembly has never trodden
seriously upon its old hereditary corns, but
now things are changed—there are 16 planks in
the Labour platform, and each one of them
will fall heavily on the Council’s bunions,
and rouse it to furious opposition. There-
fore, even though the Labour-party should
secure the assistance of one of the other
parties in the Assembly, and rush every
separate item of its programme through
in triumph, it will be no further advanced than
it is now—the Labour-party in Maoriland
passed about six progressive measures last
session, and the Council dropped them all softly
into the dust-bin, and the same experience will
happen over again in Sydney. The one alterna-
tive is to send to the Council a batch of demo-
cratic nominees sufficient to carry through the
popular programme; and then promptly pass a
measure, with their assistance, either abolishing
the nominee House or turning it into an elec-
tive body. It is necessary to get the new batch
of nominees in early, for until they are in it is
almost hopeless to waste time on the One-Man-
One-Vote Bill, or any other such measure—the
present Council would simply sell the document
for waste paper, and all the enthusiasm of the
Assembly would be so much lost material.
And, having got the new nominees in, it is
advisable to get a Reform Bill through with all
speed, for even an advanced democrat cannot
be trusted long with irresponsible power, and
in less than twelve months the new members
would probably have fallen in love with the
system which gave them a life-long billet apiece,
and would be disinclined for a change. There-
fore the Labour-party would do well to look to
this matter, and if any Premier or prospective
Premier is willing to swallow the Labour pro-
gramme whole in return for the support of the
democratic members, they can test his sincerity
by demanding the appointment of a body of
democrats to the Upper House, sufficient to
prevent the hopeless wreck of their projects in
that legislative sepulchre. And if Parkes or
Dibbs, or- the other individual, whoever he may
be, declines this condition, the party may
safely conclude that he means to get his name
up as a reformer by booming every imaginable
reform in the Assembly, and then to whoop
round as a patriotic martyr when the Council
blocks him by pre-arrangement, and leaves
things just as they were before.

Vice-regal day at the sheep-show, at the uncomeatable
Sydney Agricultural Ground, on 3rd inst., was attended

enthusiasts who would rush blindly into Tophet if they

never will. But only the noble Earl and Mr. Goschen in a
red Tom Thumb tie turned up, and as they, though as good

nothing for it but to criticise the baa-baas. In the-
pavilion the Tasmanian sheep had the thickest and

shearer’s point of view, of all the exhibits, but I preferred
the artistic, if less accredited Southdovvns with their
spindle-spidery legs and their pretty though shoddy
fleece. However, on my explaining my purely aesthetic
preference to a large and raging squatter I was told (in.
effect) that I didn’t know enough about sheep to eat a
mutton-chop intelligently, so I gave up looking “ knowing’’
round the pens after that. The sheep-dog trials were
rather interesting ; one irate Scotsman from Yass, with a
aiher disappointing tyke, quite lost his temper when a.

any black sheep at the show—at least on four legs.
The N.S.W. United Fanciers’ dog, cat and men show at
the Exhibition, on 2nd ult., although opened by Governor
Jersey, was rather a dismal experience—the attendance
being a mere handful of queer doggy-looking men, and
two or three ladies headed by the eldest vice-regal
daughter in putty-coloured jacket and brown hat trimmed
with brown ribbons ; Mrs. W. P. Manning (mayoress) in
lavender-grey with floral vest and border bonnet en suite-,
who brought her younger daughter in dark-blue. But
the exhibits were interesting, and of all I liked the bow-
Wows best altho’ they did keep up an incessant yapping
And snarling (what ail ideal dog-fight there would be U
they all got loose some of these times !) which set the
cocks crowing until you couldn’t hear yourself think.
A dear little fluffy sky-terrier ungraciously met my
advances by nipping my sleeve with a set of rat-trap
teeth, but I found the big dogs far more sociable —a deadly k
looking creature-, with a face like a Chinese war god, re
ceiving my attentions in a manner quite contrary to the
fixed exppression of bis blood-shot eye. That ancient
aphorism about “ appearances,” &c., turns up, you see,
even at a dog-show. And, speaking of dogs, have you
ever remarked the likeness between the prize Toby at such
shows, and the master who hangs lovingly over him haP
the time ?
The evening I sat giggling at u Our Flat,” when the

shriek, christened him “ Jersey !” while even the d.c. was
Seen to give way to surreptitious smiles behind its feather

take her place when she was too bored to Show at the

where any little difference between the two Wouldn’t he
remarked. If “M. B. Jersey ” dolled her faC-sihiile up in
oneof her gowns and set her down beside Lord J., I’d bet a
Bernhardt treasury to a pair of gloves that notone in a

Talking about Lady J., reminds the thftt what the
Countess calls manners and what local people have beeh
taught to regard as manners, don’t come from the same
tome of etiquette; No doubt Lady J. is right (Australian
deportment hasn’t much to boast about, anyway), but,
still, it does seeni strange to See a Governor’s wife rising
to shake hands With any man or woman who claims her
acquaintance. The old rule —whacked Into the girls of a
dozen generations, 1 believe—was to rise for age and for
superior rank, but to remain seated before your own
compeers and gentlemen—but now that N.S.W.’s leading
lady has commenced popping up for all sorts of people, I
suppose society will hare to emulate her condescension-
At which most women will heave a regretful sigh. It was
a heavenly joy to many of them to watch some embarrassed
young man floundering over a waste of carpet to greet
them, as they sat, unmoved; according to what we have
all understood as etiquette.
The moral one deduces from that riotous farce “ Our
Flat,” at the Garrick, is that, unless there is a rich pa in
the immediate background, the girl who connubializes
with a literary character, who can’t sell his brains, doesn’t
know enough to come in out of the wet. As the feckless
female who follows such a blind matrimonial lead, Mary
Kingsley—barring her soft-soapy way of verbalizing, was
a square peg, and dressed herself very well—ditto her
atage stster, Marie Brooks, as “ Lucy Macallum. ’ In the
first act Margery wears terra-cotta silk with the front of
fancy tartan, trimmed with thick fringe on the one side ;
“ Lucy,” in a sweetly summery garment in lavender with
yoke, puffed sleeves and foot frill of cream mull, floral
hat; Act 2—“ Lucy,” all in white silk and lace—a charm-
ing get-up, but hardly the sort of garment a real lady
would slip into before luncheon. In a realistic sense,
curl-papers and a pink flannel wrapper would have been
miles nearer the target. But, what are frocks beside Mr,
Royce’s unctuous coughs and sudden sprawlings among
the furniture? or Polly Emery’s padded ankles and
delirious trippings over the tail of that tea-gown ? “ Our
Flat” is more funny than frocky.
The last performance but two of “ Lyons’ Mail,” at Her
Majesty’s, drew such a good house that it seems odd they
don’t keep it on at the Royal. A medical theatre-party
in the vice-regal box, all the ladies of which carried
sprays of flowers, lit up the circle, Mrs. (Ur.) Mackellar
wearing a handsome frock of black wafer net over maize-
satin, trimmed with black chiffon and gold-tinsel ; another
doctor’s wife had donned a well-mingled black and mauve
toilet; cream moire and tinsel braid (very fashionably
made) was sported by a distinctly plain woman in the
d.c.—l assure you this isn’t feminine spite, for it never
does to praise a woman and her dress, too. But Bessie
Bignold fainted rather crudely ; if stage-draperies won’t
keep down couldn’t they be loaded with shot like the
riding-skirts? Also, I couldn’t see quite why the far-
sighted management saw fit to put a knock-kneed lady
into knickerbockers.
The question that is agitating the Sydney theatre tram-
travelling public just at present is whether a woman
covered with diamonds, furs, and satins can negotiate a
journey with both feet up on the seat in front of her and
still expect the rest of the compartment to mistake her
for a duchess ? It’s rather a new experience nowadays to
see a woman put her feet tip—the great, throbbing
feminine tendency seems to be to put them down— hard.
Madame Weigal’s “Fashion Journal” for July is about
again, and in looking over its pages one feels soothed and
pleased to see that the frock, made in merry, merry May,
is just as stylish as ever. Rather a novelty for ball-room
wear and a saving clause to the woman with a neck like a
New York sheep-skin, is a “ fraise or ruff of tulle” worn
round the neck with low cut evening bodices. It is a
thickly niched or pleated band of tulle to match the gown
tied at the back of the neck with long bows and hems
Teaching to the hem of the garment. This interesting
authority also declares that badge and grenadine are
going to have a run—two wiry gauzes which put one’s
teeth on an end to touch them.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
On Saturday, June 13, the pupils of Miss Palmer,
assisted by several ladies and gentlemen, professional and
■amateur, gave a morning concert, which was opened with
the overture from “ Zampa,” played on two pianos by
eight bands ; this was followed by selections from Verdi,
Beethoven, Chopin, Liszt, and other eminent composers.
Miss Portus recited “ The Old Actor's Story,” and as an
■encore gave “Coming thro’ the Barley,” and Miss Palmer
played a rondo by Chopin, and in duos with Mrs. Teece
and Miss Grace Palmer. All the numbers were excellently
rendered, the time and phrasing throughout were perfect,
and, judging from the enthusiasm that prevailed in the
auditorium, Miss Palmer has every reason for self-gratu-
lation.

apparently in support of an imputation con-
cerning the existence of improper relations
between the two, that a man had habitu-
ally called his niece “Gertie,” instead
of Miss Blank, or whatever her surname may
have been. Now, is it usual for a man to address
his niece by anything but her Christian name ?
Does not the uncle of Mary Smith always call lief-
“ Mary,” and not “ Miss ” Smith ?
Rev. Conroy, an Irish priest, has, from a
distance of 12,000 miles', entered a libel action,
claiming £5OO damages, against the Launceston
Telegraph (Tas.), the alleged libel being a state-
ment that he had prayed Goi to strengthen the
arm that killed Sub-Inspector Martin, of the Irish
Constabulary.
Australia does not enjoy full English copy-
right privileges. Under existing conditions, if
yon write a book in Australia and print and copy-
right it in London, it is copyright in Great
Britain and in Australia ; but if you print and
copyright your book in Australia, it is not copy-
right in (treat Britain—any English publisher
may steal it.
The Bedfords bought Covent Carden and
Long Acre 300 years ago for £l7O. Since then
they have themselves spent on the property cer-
tainly not more than £50,000, and its value is in-
calculable. It was put down then as worth
£6 6s. Bd. per year, and now it brings in from
£50,000 to £lOO,OOO a year. What have the Bed-
fords done for this money? They have sat still
and enjoyed themselves.
A just-discovered letter of the painter,
Rubens, in London National Gallery, says that
“a great land-nay, a continent,” lias been re-
cently discovered by the Dutch traders to the
South-east of Java and Sumatra, but that the
chartered company was keeping the matter dark
for fear of the rivalry of the other European
countries. It reads oddly now, this intelligence
of Australia in 1620, when the betting was 100 to
I in favour of it becoming a Dutch settlement.
Writes one of the female idiots who went
from Australia to China some months ago as
missionaries—they having been surrounded by
riotous Chinamen at some inland town
Just try and imagine our position. . . . No English-
man within miles and miles. Shut up in a rickety old
house with a mob of raging Chinamen outside
We had absolutely no one on earth to look to, and so we
ooked to Heaven.
How these godly people do give themselves away !
“Not an Englishman within miles,” you see.
Consequently they had to fall hack on Heaven.
But any fool can guess which rescuing agent those
twenty-four parson-deluded females would have
preferred had they owned a choice.
Concerning the unfortunate gun explosion
aboard H.M.S. Cordelia, by which six people were
killed outright and many wounded, the Sydney
Telegraph reporter states that “ the topgallant
Lulu-ark (!) was broken and battered in various
places.” Likewise the duff halyards were
shivered, the fore-peak of the main-crosstrees was
unrove, the keelson sheet parted at the blocks,
the cook’s galley was blown out of its bolt-ropes,
the fo’c’stle stern anchor main-haul snapped like
a carrot, and, in a few words —
The royal-truck chains were all unshored,
The scuppers did unstow ;
It chucked the captain overboard
And let the painter go.
The Glorious Fourth was marked in Sydney
by an official reception at the office of the IT.S.
Vice-Consul, to whom many citizens paid their
respects, and later in the day General Howell and
Mes-rs. Donald Manson and Livingston Hopkins
took with them down the harbour, per launch, to
Pearl Bay, about 40 friends, whom they had in-
vited, as has been their custom for some years past,
to celebrate the occasion. Mr. Hopkins pre-
sided at a generous luncheon, whereat champagne
flowed freely, and fully sustained Ids reputation
as an unapproachably humorous chairman.
Among the many speeches made,'the most notable
were those of Mr. Edmund Barton and Mr. G. R.
Dibbs—the latter of whom, in all innocence, tried
to carve the wooden ham with conventional trim-
mings, entrusted to his attention by “ Hop.” and
his fellow-conspirators. Mr. Dibbs, who (like
Premier Parkes) is ah expert with the lathe, has
promised to turn a wooden ham for the next
celebration of the Republic’s birthday.

It is probable that the Bernhardt season
is being run at Sydney Her Majesty’s as much in
the interests of the serpentine Sara as in those of
the public. The dressing-rooms at the Royal ate
scarcely tit for the grooming of donkeys, much
less the toilette bf the human form divine, and
the extra tier at the Pitt-street house interposes
another gradation in price between the lordly ten
shillings of circle and reserved stalls and the wel-
come, if humble florin of the gallery. It has been
said that the £Bl5, taken at Sara’s opening night
in Melbourne, was the largest sum ever taken at
any one theatrical representation in Australia. It
may have been the most drawn by a dramatic
performance, but certain Sydney old-timers re-
member that Catherine Hayes on one occasion
filled the treasury of theancient VictoriaTheatre to
the tune of £l3OO by one concert performance. It
may also interest those who grumble, at the Bern-
hardt season’s prices to know that the Catherine
Hayes’ charges were, for circle, 30s. ; stalls, 21s. ;
upper circle, 155.; pit, 10s. Od. ; galleiy, ss. ; all
round * considerably over three times as much as
the prices prevailing at ttel Majesty’s for the Beth-
hardt-Abbey-Williamson season. The auction sale
bf circle and stall seats, not taken for the season,
held in the theatre and presided over by Auctioneer
Gorman was not an unmixed success. For this
there were several reasons. In the first place,
nearly all the best seats had been secured by
season-ticket holders, then, many of the people
who might have cared to give fancy prices had
seen Sara in Melbourne, finally, the first bloom of
the Bernhardt boom has been dissipated by the
rumours of long waits ahd capricious behaviour,
that have filtered over from the malodorous city.
Therefore, after the first and best seat had fallen
to the nod of “Falk” for £2 Bs., prices at once
dropped to 305., and thence, by easy stages, to 12s.
od., when proceedings were abruptly terminated.

for yet another 20 nights to payable business. r l he

There is no get away from the fact that
“Evangeline” is still doing S.R.O. business at
Sydney Criterion, though the circle audiences are
not so” stylish as they were wont to be. As a
consequence, the season has been extended till
July 31st, after which date “ Dr. Bill,” supported
by the original Sydney cast, will hang out his
brass-plate and commence to purge away melan-
choly with allopathic doi.es of laughter. One
consolatory feature about “ Vangy,” as it is
affectionately called “ behind,” is its good orclu s-
tratibn, and the ingenious conducting of Composer
Rice, whereby every point is accentuated, and the
singers are not drowned by, but are buoyed up
on, the accompaniment. Virginia Earl has made
herself a big favourite, chiefly by means of her
curls, white teeth, fetching smile, and winning
American way, but perhaps, also because of her
Henry Irving stride, which fascinates by reason
of its incongruous unexpectedness. Singing “I
like it,” the other night, she attributed Greer’s
defeat to his attack on the barmaids, whereupon
“Joey,” who was present, seized 1 1 is nail-can and
bolted to escape the titters of the sitters in his
vicinity.

Our prediction concerning “Our Flat”
seems likely to be realised. A change is necessary,
and if “something”—pending the arrival of
James Cassius, it is not known what—can be got
ready in time, the time-payment furniture will be
removed for the last time in Friday evening next.
Little Albion Whyte—peculiar combination of
names, suggestive of Emerald Green and Schwartz
Noir and Co.—deserves a line for the easy, grace-
ful manner in which she plays the inquisitive,
destructive child of the prying busybody of a
neighbour. Otherwise there is nothing to be said,
for “Teddy ” Royee and Polly Emery continue to
bear the burthen of the show and annex the
laughter and applause.

So far the Halles have succeeded in filling
Sydney Town Hall to its utmost on several occa-
sions, and the invariably crowded condition of the
cheaper seats effectually disposes of the hitherto
much promulgated theory that Sydney people
don’t care for high-class music and attend concerts
of that description for fashion’s sake only. Friday
and Saturday nights are announced as positively
the last.

Alf, Wyburd made a big splash at Bondi
Aquarium on the “ Glorious Fourth,” a day yearly
gaming more recognition from the republic-loving
Australians. On Sunday last, when Amy Blackie,
Harry Lester, Fred and other well-known
artistes performed, the attendance was pheno-
menal. The management has some novelty in
course of preparation; we don't exactly know
what, but believe it is a four legged fowl that eats
coals and speaks several languages, and emits
electric sparks when its tail is agitated.
»* * *
Gaiety Burlesque is booming at Melbourne
Princess’. A fresh mob of low-browed, long-
ohinned chappies seem to have suddenly burst into
bloom at the button-hole, and the air of the dress-
circle boozery is heavy with the scent of acres of
carnations, as these nauseating noodles exchange
inanities concerning certain tall, showy young
women, who figure at the fag-end of the present
company in the intellectual capacity of legs. The
chappie persons are alleged to be'squatters, and
the fine, fleshy damsels are reported to like squatters
better than oyster-stew, therefore a good deal of
social interest attaches to the course of events up
yonder. Nevertheless there are portentous signs
that the old, simple faith in Gaiety burlesque is
severely shaken. A sceptical spirit "of enquiry is
abroad, Tha Sadducees of Melbourne are want-
ing to know why the lines of several songs are so
hideously stupid, also why the artists appointed
to warble this drivel don’t compose something
better out of their own heads as they go along. A
second hearing of ditties like “You wink the
other eye,” “What’s your price?” or “It’s
really most extraordinary, isn’t it ? ” sets rational
citizens wondering what they would think of a pri-
vateindi vidual who inflicted such cruelties upon his
friends in cold blood. They would think he was
what Fred. Leslie terms “a rotter.” However,
there are many other shows which cannot bear to
be looked at from this point of view, though it is
a quite legitimate way of considering items that
are hauled in by the heels and have no earthly
connection with the rest of the entertainment.
“ Ruv Bias and the Blase Roue ” has been pruned
and trimmed considerably since the opening night,
so that audiences can now withdraw in deadly
silence at a convenient hour. The apathy with
which they beat a retreat on Saturday night was
not a favourable symptom. It is the misfortune
of “Ruy Bias ” that, after the front scene of the
second act (and there are three acts altogether) it
begins to decline and fall, like the Roman Empire.
From simple softening of the brain it degenerates
into gibbering lunacy. Happily the management
saw fit to cut out, as soon as possible, an un-
pleasant chunk of “wholesome fun,” in which
Irving was represented as an aged ballet-girl.
Now, Irving is the last man who should be held up
to ridicule in a Gaiety burlesque, for he makes, or
did make, a practice of paying supernumerary girls
double what they could get for the same work
from most other managers, the extra money being
intended to encourage respectability, social purity,
or whatever Mr. Varley calls it. But no matter,
Nellie Barren seems to grow on the Australian
taste, when she isn’t singing, although the delicate
humour of a London gamin accent will never be
hugely admired outside New Babylon. Her
strong grip of the burlesque art is most evident
in a love-scene with Grace Pedley. Leslie lays
himself out to vary his clever business in small
details, and Fred Storey’s limb-throwing is
certainly quicker and “cleaner” than last time.
Danby counts for very little in this show, how-
ever, whilst the dancing of Sylvia Grey and
Florence Levey (both of whom have now settled
down into an elegant stride) arouses no furore.
The local article, as purveyed by the Royal
ballerinas, is much better than it used to be,
whereas the Gaiety tripping has, perhaps, deterio-
rated in quality and undoubtedly lost its glamour.
As for the lovely amazons who stand around and
ogle, they are pathetic illustrations of the differ-
ence between mind and matter. Admitting all
these things, the fact remains that “ Ruy Bias ”
is bright in its way, especially at the outset, and
a handsome spectacle all the time.
** * *
“Run Wild,” the sad story of a ’appy
ome broken up by the introduction of a terrier
dawg, has been doing only moderately at the
Opera House. Sometimes the terrier gets a bone
in his throat and coughs promiscuously, but
generally speaking, he is a gloomy failure. On
Saturday next Jennie Lee retires in favour of
“The County Fair,” with its mechanical race-
scene and other features recently sampled by
Sydneyites.
** » »
Revivals of old friends are following one
another in weekly parts at the Bijou, the only
Melbourne theatre where common-sense is enjoy-
ing a look in just now. On Saturday they put up
“ Caste ” with cheerful results, this neatest of
Robertson’s comedies being popular alike with the
“gods” and the society people who dye young.
The latter love to hear Captain Hawtrey and the
Marquise hold forth upon the immutable laws of
caste, without understanding that the author is
poking borak at blue-blood and sticking pins into
parvenus. The former see in Ecoles a true type
of the philanthropist who never robs his brother
of a day’s work, but is quite open to annex his
beer. They have, after all, no occasion to wince
at the miserable old ruin, because his sym-
pathies are with capital, not with labour,
and his one notion of independence is unlimited
credit at the little church round the corner.
The excellence of the “Caste” performance, in
which all"the B. and B. originals appear again, is
an oft-told tale. The Broughs, Mr. and Mrs., are
always at their best as Esther Eccles and Sam
Gerridge— a London mechanic to the life. Miss
Romer and Percy Lyndal take high honours, and
if Anson’s fine conception of Eccles is in danger
of being oyercoloured, as much may be insinuated
against Titheradge and Pattie Browne, both of
whom seem greater favourites than ever in their
old parts. Pattie’s pert self-consciousness is be-
coming very marked. There will be a “Caste”
matinee this Friday (10th), and on the following
night “Diplomacy ” comes to stay for a week.
** * *
At the Alexandra they have had about
enough of Wild West wind, which doesn’t whistle
like it did. Therefore “The Trapper” is fixing
up his traps, including cowboys and Indians, and
the interesting savage who languishes for love of
Lily Dampier will have to be quick and drown
himself in the tank before Carver can bundle him
back to his wigwam. “The Trapper” retires
forthwith, the Dampier setting of “His Natural
Life ” being due on Saturday.

Various halls are in active eruption, more
so than usual. Frank Clark contrives to keep
matters cheery at the Gaiety ; the immortal Billin
iscooin’ away on his managerial perch at the
Vie.; and a Novelty Company are struggling with
fate at the St. George’s, The novelty appears to
consist of nigger. Miss Ada Fitzroy has earned
a good name for herself at the Waxworks. The
supporters of that old-established institution roll
up steadily to laugh at her shadowgraphs, or turn
pale at the thought of a fragile young lady being
able to carry 30 names with their respective
numbers in her classical little head. Ada’s
skill as a shadowgraphist sets one smiling
at Fred Leslie’s one crude attempt in the same
direction, which quite knocks them up at the
Princess’. The Battle of Waterloo Cyclorama re-
mains at two shillings a head, although the Siege
of Paris is on the spot, rolled up in readiness for
the completion of the Bourke-street building. Mr.
Jude is gathering honey at his Musical Evenings
in the Melbourne district, and the perennial
Lynch Family Bellringers, who have come out
again quite as good as new, are doing ditto. They
ravage the suburbs five nights of the week, and
return triumphant to the Athenaeum every Satur-
day.
* * -* *
In one respect Sara Bernhardt is a dis-
appointment—when she writes a letter on the
stage there is the same awful want of probability
about the document as of old, for she writes it in
just the same unsatisfying way as any other
artiste. In other words, she gets hold of the pro-
perty ink-bottle with a reckless disregard of con-
sequences that ought to make the ink fly up in her
eye, and which would do so if there were ink in it.
Then she grasps the pen and plunges it into the pot
with much vigour, and next shebounds and gambols
for about three seconds on top of a large sheet of
paper, and the document is finished. Then she
addresses it by rushing at the epistle a second
time, and administering one wild wallop on the
envelope; and when her correspondent opens it,
and solemnly reads two columns of literary matter,
and goes mad on account of the statements which
Sara has inserted, the audience doesn’t seem at
all surprised. This sort of thing is irritating,
but unhappily it is unavoidable; if Sara
were true to historical fact, and wrote that letter
just as Cleopatra would have written it, it is three
chances to one that the audience would break into
riot and wreck the theatre. For when Cleopatra
wanted to write to Antony, it is most likely that
she spread herself all over the table, and nearly
brained Charmian and Iras with her elbows as
she stuck them out in all directions, and wasted
four hours and eight sheets of papyrus, and bobbed
her head laboriously as she made her up-strokes,
and jerked her tongue out painfully to keep time
with the down-strokes, and not only inked herself
up to the eyes, but scattered huge blots over most
of Antony’s legions as well, and nearly wore her-
self out hunting for the big words in the Egyptian
dictionary of the period. And when the literary
effort was finished she was probably led away by
her attendants to get the ink washed off her,
while the document was despatched to its destina-
tion by a eunuch mounted on an elephant. That
sort of thing might be true to nature and history,
but the audience wouldn’t stand it.

The Sara Bernhardt speculation of six
nights’ and three matinees’ tenancy of the Ade-
laide Royal has proved very successful to those
who undertook the risk, though Wybert Reeve
didn’t profit much over the affair. The repertoire
included “Camille,” “FrouFrou,” “LaTosca,”
“Adrienne Leoouvreur,” “Jeanne D’Arc,” and
“Fedora,” and the attendances were large, and
the applause, on the whole, satisfactory, consider-
ing that but few in the audience really understood
what was going on. The scenery used in the pro-
ductions has been seen in the Adelaide theatre for
a number of years, and the company, after the
French star, were locally pronounced second-rate.
Tommy Hudson is doing wonders with his Min-
strel Company at Garner’s Rooms, and A. Middle-
ton, representing the MacMahons, has made great
preparations for a successful season with the
phonograph at thejTown Hall. Capt. De Burgh
explains the invention.
