What crank paid for the insertion in Sydney E. News of
a-third of a column trying to prove that it was wicked to
call Sara “Divine”?
Let Sydney beware. Nothing in her Australian experi-
ences has more irritated the “divine” Sara than the
senseless rounds of applause, dragged in without rhyme
or reason at the slightest pause, which have spoilt some
of her best scenes. The born idiots who wish people to
believe that the'' understand French are solely respon-
sible for this, and should be watched.
Honour to whom honour is due. The financial risk of
the Bernhardt engagement rests entirely with Abbey and
Grau, Williamson and Garner merely finding the theatres.
Even the expense of decorating Madame’s dressing-room,
at Melbourne Princess’, was borne by Henry Abbey. But
the “ firm ” took the credit all the same, w’hereat Abbey
waxed wroth.
“Laura Villiers, who is to appear at the re-opening of
Melbourne Koyal, is a daughter of London Pavilion
Villiers. She is slight and rather short, with fairish hair,
a sweet voice, not too powerful, and is very near-sighted.
In England her appearances have been mostly in the
provinces. The lady has money.
Bernhardt and Gaiety successes notwithstanding, the
prospect of Irving and Terry, or the Kendalls coming to
Australia is more than doubtful. Bernhardt has the rest-
less desire for travel and is never long in Paris. The
Gaiety management have two companies of nearly equal
merit and can always spare one abroad, whilst the other
keeps alight the sacred lamp at the London Gaiety.
Toole, with about the smallest theatre in London, can
only draw there for about four months in the year, and
has to get somew’here for the rest of the time. But
Irving and Terry can jjlay in London all the year round,
and the great Henry will never, like some other stars, act
with cheap scenery, or properties, or a cheap company.
James Brown Potter is described by one who knows him
well as one of the handsomest men alive, with a beautiful
voice. And yet she preferred Bellew.
When Manager A. and Manager B. visit England and
America they are entertained and made much of by
Managers 0. and D. But when Manager C. and bis wife,
and the wife of Manager D. visit Australia, Managers A.
and B. are either unable or unwilling to return the com-
pliment. A mistake, as a little time will show. Apart
from this, is not Australia wanting in some respects in its
treatment of members of the profession ? Where else
would a pimply-faced publican tell people willing to pay
liberally for accommodation that “we do not take pro-
fessionals” as at Adelaide ?
The scene in Bernhardt’s “ Tosoa,” where Ploria first
sees and then clutches tne knife to kill Scarpia, is a
psychological study of the finest kind, and worth all the
rest of the play put together.
In “Gamille,” the opening piece in Adelaide, Sara
Bernhardt suffered so much from cold in the theatre
that she was practically unable to do more than walk the
part. But the daily critics got there all the same,,
although they cannot quite make up their minds about
Mrs. Brown Potter. “We understood her, jou know.”
Lady Hopetoun says that she made eight unsuccessful
tries to obtain seats for “The Dancing Girl,” at London
Haymaket. Yet when they produce Jones’ play at
Melbourne Royal, her ladyship will be able to book the
vice-regal box at the first attempt. Which shows that
the difference between a Governor’s wife “at home” and
abroad is very, very considerable, but by no means goes
to prove that a play which has a modern British dock for
its hero, will carry this country by storm. Laura Villiers
is said to have paid a lot of money for the Australian
rights, nevertheless-.
Emma Eames, a new operatic prima donna, is about to
join herself in matrimony with Julian Story, the well-
known portrait-painter. Alas ! the case of Ellen Terry
and G. E. Watts was a solemn warning to all who contem-
plate forming a stage and studio guild of this description.
They seem all right in theory—husband paints himself,
and wife paints herself—but in practice the idea generally
turns out a failure. Husband and wife have a hard brush
and try to paint one another as often as not.
Abbey says the Bernhardt exchequer is most satis-
factory, and if Sydney and Brisbane come up to expecta-
tions the tour will be a great success financially.
Some consternation was caused amongst the occupants
of the reserved stalls at Bernhardt’s “La Tosca,” in
Adelaide. Just on eight o’clock, w'hen the reserved seats
were nearly all filled with social cream in evening dress,
in walked a Celestial in moleskin pants and slouch hat.
He had booked his little seat, so they had to take him.
He sat out the play with stolid indifference.
Mrs. Henry Abbey (Miss Florence Girard) was some
time leading lady at the London Haymarket, under the
Bancrofts, and was considered the most beautiful woman
then on the stage.
How far will superstition extend? One of Augustus
Harris’ companies was to open the Northampton Theatre,
rebuilt after a fire. At the last moment the little man
almost cancelled the engagement because he discovered
that peacock-feathers had been painted on the act-drop.
But Janet Achuroh carried a (Colombo) fan of the same
feathers in “Forget-Me-Not,” everywhere. Mrs. Ban-
croft always scattered a packet of pins in front of the
green-room pier-glass on first nights for luck, and every
actress that ever was carries about with her a select assort-
ment of old horse-shoes.
Tearle is not the only actor who could be altogether up-
set by any display of indifference on the part of an
audience. Sothern w 7 as just as bad. One night, exas-
perated by a man who w r as ostentatiously leaving a
private box during the second act, he called out to him,
“Do you know that there is another act, sir?” “Yes,”
was the very audible reply, “that’s exactly why lam
going.”

fire scene, and made a feature of the realistic effect. But
the realism was carried too far when they turned the hose
with real water on the “eminent” one, w’hose language
was painful in the extreme.

On the night the Melbourne Royal opens, the Alexandra

Bernhardt’s Co., all told, are 38. They went from Mel-
bourne to Adelaide by steamer, not rail, and cut it very
fine, so as to jeopardise the opening, unless the Oroya got
in strictly to time. The Oroya has a peculiarly cosy music-

Most ludicrous are the London notices of Barry Sullivan.
For instance, a first-class paper is before us, with an
elaborate critique written by a man who evidently newer
saw him. The writer refers to his imposing presence and
big sonorous voice, which he was so proud of using, and
which filled the biggest theatre. Why, these are the very
two things he was most deficient in. The notices written
are of G. Y. Brooke, not Sullivan at all. Says one—“He
had none of the subtlety of Irving ’’—not Irving’s, but his
Richard was equally subtle. A critic who has seen him
thinks his best part was Faulconbridge in “King John.”
The only actor who never craved for original parts, and,
indeed, didn’t like them. Good old leg !

Perhaps so, though we don’t believe it.
Coppin got to work at once gutting the Melbourne
Royal. Shows himself fully abreast of the time, for, when
W. and G. and everybody else failed to tender up to
mark by the end of 1890, Coppin and Co. at once got their
architect’s plans for a virtually new 7 theatre. The place is
reduced to the mere skeleton, naught but the walls,
floors and pillars of the tiers, so that all will be brand-
new, like the Princess’ or Bijou.
Madame Simonsen has engaged Signor Maffezzoli as
opera conductor. The old-time Australian conductors
were Lavenu, Linley Norman, and Loder, and Lyster
brought Reiff, since whom there has been a long succes-
sion of batonists: Siedc, Marzorati, Oiorza, Van Ghele
Hughes, Harrison, Cellier, and so on, Maffezzoli is a very
ardent young man, and ought to succeed, like the ener-
getic Hazon.

A Broken Hill (N.S.W.) P.M. has decided, in the case
Mahoney v. Ridge, a suit for £l4 10s. 6d., wages due, that
a “ performer ” does not come under the definition of a
servant as furnished by the Masters and Servants Act
Consequently, though Ridge admitted owing the bulk of
the money, the case was dismissed. Barbarism !'

does Lena Salinger, engaged at Frisco Tivoli. For the

Imano, who came here two years ago with Johnny
Sheridan, has since played the principal baritone parts n
“Dorothy,” “lolanthe,” and Poo-bah in the “ Mikado ’*
(he played it for 15 months in England) with Williamson
and Garner’s opera company throughout Australasia, He
has a well-trained, round, sweet and forceful baritone of
great range.

Barry Sullivan was as great an.autocrat before as behind
the curtain. “Order” was Sullivan’s first law. No
matter in what part of the house, no matter who was con-
cerned, order had to be kept. One night a party ou
military men, with a couple of well-known ladies (one ofi
them still, after a stormy career, much to the fore in,
Sydney all-wool society), occupied the o.p. box, and were
extremely noisy. The partv was so rowdy that Sullivan—
who was not engaged on the stage—walked round th®
circle with a stately stride, entered the box, and with a
most courtly bow requested the whole lot to clear out—
which they did, amidst cheers from all parts of the house
These people were causing a public scandal about this
time, the press designating the gentlemen, “ male
poodles.”
Fitzgerald Bros., of circus fame, are resting at Bendigo,
for the purpose of training horses, &c. They are brethren,
of the senior member for West Sydney.
The new Melbourne Cyclorama, Siege of Paris, will;
soon open; all the fixings have arrived from ’Frisco.
With Waterloo, Eureka Stockade and Siege running at
once, cyclorama business will probably be overdone in,
Melbourne, but other folks were nibbling after the dirt-
cheap site, and the directors thought it better to invest
their surplus than allow competition from outside.

Louis Lohr writes to say that he has never seen things
in general so lively in Maoriland. The Patey business has
been splendid everywhere ; returns from 27 concertsabout
£5OOO, and speculators in several towns actually bought
the seats in advance and auctioned them at a profit.
There are only one or two inferior companies in the
country who have been stranded there, and Maoriland
seems more capable of supporting good shows during the
coming season than at any previous period. The amiable
Lohr is now making things smooth in Tasmania.
We have just seen a private letter (in pencil) from Harry
Edwards, dated New York, May 21. In this communica-
tion he speaks of himself as “ a broken-down man,” and
says that he had been suffering since February with
“ grippe,” rheumatism and pneumonia. Altogether the
letter is a hopeless one, and it looks very much as if
there were some truth in the rumour concerning the
death of the brave and gentle actor and entomologist.

Dear Bulletin,— ln reading your last issue I was par-
ticularly struck with a paragraph under the heading of
Poverty Point. The joke in question ran as follows :
“The real reason of Elton’s re-engagement. The ‘Firm’
wanted George Walton to take the former’s place, but,
leaving the offer too late, found that the latter had
arranged to take out a company of his own and would not
renew. Which means an extra ten pounds per week to
the great E.” Now the little funnyment is not a bad gag
as it stands, but it is not complete. I propose to supply
the other half, which runs thusly:—ln the first place,
Elton is not re-engaged; in the second, the great E. does
not receive ten pounds per week extra; and finally,
Walton was not offered a re-engagement of any sort what-
ever. Of that there is no possible doubt, no probable
possible shadow of doubt, no possible doubt whatever.—
Yours, &c., Wm. Elton.

Brougham said : “ Commonwealth means
Republic.”
“ Freetrade is gaining ground daily?”
In what country ?
No deposit is required from parliamentary
candidates in South Australia.
The voice of the Australian majority has
emphatically declared for Protection.
Now that many of the lawyers have been
driven from N.S.W. Parliament, law-reform is
ahead.
In Maoriland, 48 people own lands worth
£9,788,450; and 1116 possess holdings worth
£34. 848, 612.
T he Melbourne dailies are fiercely whipping
up the Conservative voters in view of the political
labour-storm ahead.
Fifty Labour members in the next Vic-
torian Parliament, after the example of the unpre-
pared party in N.S. Wales.
Queensland has been borrowing money in
London at the rate of 2| millions sterling every
year since 1884—Griffith’s ten-millions loan year.
The Bill pro - forma in the Queensland
Parliament this year was one “to Amend the
Law relating to Noxious Animals.” Dog Towzer?
In 1889, out of 20 millions of acres of
land alienated from the Crown in N.Z., 17,987,507
acres were owned by 1615 persons. Of these,
1140 were permanent absentees.
A Melbourne scribe sums up the crowd
that gathered at the opening of Parliament as “ a
congregation of people of no consequence, some of
them cockneys in cocked hats.”
The new Australian Parliamentary Labour-
party may be compared to a rapid stream running
between two old crumbling party-lands and
gradually encroaching on their banks.
After the first rough statement at Wagga
of the Murrumbidgee polling, Arthur Rae, one of
the members-elect, called for and got three
thundering “cheers for the Republic !”
Parkes proposes as an obvious sop to the
N.S.W. Democratic party, to create a Minister of
Industry. He has amongst his political following
many chevaliers of industry to select from.
The Victorian Assembly has rejected on
the voices Sir Bryen O’Loghlen’s motion refusing
to sanction Federation —save on a One-man one-
vote basis. The Southern Labour-party will see
about this matter —after next general election.
Treasurer Ballance, of Maoriland, pro-
poses to abolish the property-tax and substitute a
progressive land and income tax. The effect of
this would be to relieve small farmers of direct
taxation, and throw the burden on the mono-
polists.
Never a reference lias been made this
session by Premier Munro, in the Victorian Par-
liament, to the recent death, in harness, of his
late colleague, Langridge. Probably, Munro owes
him a grudge for dying and letting in Labour-
leader Hancock.
Let no one make a mistake. One Man
One Vote may be “in sight ” but it is not coming
without a political death-grapple between the
Conservative and Democratic parties. The drums
of the allotment-mongers are now rolling from
Cape York to Cape Otway.
In re Canadian Lady Macdonald’s cabled
“elevation” to the peerage: In 1791 Fox quar-
relled with Burke over Pitt’s scheme of Canadian
peerages, and, said Fox, “ Are those red and blue
ribands, which have lost their lustre in the Old
World, to shine fresh again in the New ?”
Thirty members of the new N.S.W. Par-
liament are said to be pledged to the programme
of the never-touch-its. Once more The Bulletin
asks Boss Templar Clark (who is bald —this doesn’t
look well) what the prohibitionists intend to do
about the Australian wine-industry ? Are the
vineyards to be rooted up?
A Primrose-Leaguer was endeavouring to
persuade an audience that, Home Rule granted,
agricultural England would swarm with Irish
labourers, and that this would cut down the wages
of the Englishmen, “Ah,” rapped out an old
lady, “you don’t want any assistance in that—
you’d ’a’ had ’em over here afore now if they’d ’a’
done that.”
A Bulletin correspondent advocates the
abolition of the poll-tax on Chinese and the sub-
stitution of total prohibition, with free passages
to China for all who choose to return; a yearly
tax of £lO per head on those who remain ; a tax
on all those who employ Chinese labour and the
public declaration by each employer of a Chinaman
of the fact.
During an election campaign a parson
called on an old lady to solicit her husband’s vote
for the conservative candidate. “No, sir,” said
she, “he’ll vote for the labour candidate.” “But
what has the labour candidate done for you?”
“ He has done a great deal, sir,” was the reply—
“ I’ve been living here for2o years and he’s at last
brought you into my house.”
One of the items which should have the
unceasing attention of the Australian “ Woman’s
Rights ” party is the law of inheritance. A man
at marriage “endows” his wife “with his goods;”
yet he is at perfect liberty to cut oft her and her
children in his will with fourpence each and leave
all his wealth to his mistresses and their illegiti-
mate children—or to do any other thing, sane or
mad, that he pleases.
In a late Leicestershire election the fol-
lowing quotation from the Times was placarded
in the Liberal interest:—
The landlord’s only notion of a peasant is as the payer
of rent, his only notion of Government as the squanderer
of grants ! When the cottiers can pay no rent he turns
them out to die, and when Ministers will give no more
money he turns round and blackguards them.
The quotation is 37 years old.
Adye Douglas—the Parkes of Tasmania,
who some years ago posed as a violent Republican
and in that capacity absolutely shocked his fellow
members of the Federal Council —finds that the
Federation Bill is not Conservative enough!
Douglas wants the Senate to amend money bills,
and he opposed a Federal Court of Appeal- on the
ground that a foreign Privy Council is the best
for a free Commonwealth.
Mr. Frank Cotton, of the N.S.W. Parlia-
mentary Labour-party, appears to be rather too
thick with Premier Parkes, and to accept far too
many little civilities at his hands, for their mutual
political independence to long continue. Either
Cotton will convert Parkes or Parkes will convert
Cotton—who, it is stated, dined with the old man
the other night. “ I will talk with you, walk
with you, but I will not eat with you, drink with
you nor pray with you.”

Tire tale of the Queensland Coalition
Government. Premier Griffith, a few years back,
swore that he would be no party to any further
alienation of the people’s lands. Jumeau
MTI wraith vowed that he would ne’er consent to
let Queensland pay subsidy to the British Naval
Tribute. Three short years and—the Crown
lands are to be sold wholesale to find Treasurer-
Mac. money, and the Naval Tribute dividend is
to be paid as a sop to Griffith’s loyalty.
Munro now proposes to enfranchise all
women. The Bulletin hopes to see women
enfranchised, but it trusts that the Victorian
electors will allow no scheme for female suffrage
to endanger what is Democracy’s most urgent
need —the thorough enfranchisement of the men.
It is necessary first to obtain One-Man-Otie-Vqte,
in order that the great Radical reforms which
hinge thereon may be effectuated, and secured
from the influence of the inevitable Tory reaction,
consequent on the ascendancy which Democracy’s
enemies, the clergy at large, will temporarily
obtain at the hands of the female voters.
Queensland Government has cleared its
first Parliamentary fence, the Address-in-Reply
being adopted without a division. This means
that Donaldson & Co., and the Adullamites ready
to cross-support any new party, are not anxious
to take over a tangled, semi-insolvent concern just
yet. “Uncle” Jessop’s resignation as Chair-
man of Committees—“ Uncle ” was always a
Mcllwraithian when the term included Morehead
& Co., but never a Coalitionist —and the appoint-
ment in his stead of Arthur Morgan, the Warwick
newspaper-man and prominent Darling-Downs
“ buncher,” is ominous of a personal rifting of the
present Ministerial conglomerate.
In Brisbane has been formed a “Patriotic
League,” whose avowed objects are :
1. To ensure to every man the peaceable enjoyment of
his earnings and savings and his personal liberty.
2. To uphold law and order, and to oppose organised
communism and socialism.
3. To protect the interests and credit of the colony.
No. 1 is evidently incomplete ; it should read
“ earnings, savings and stealings.” The Patriotic
League may be left to dine itself to death. And
so may the bogus “National” League, a Tory
institution just started in Melbourne, no list of
the objects whereof is necessary after you have
read the names of its promoters.
According to a Sydney Sunday paper there
are in the eleven principal electorates of Sydney
and suburbs no less than 22,341 non-residential or
plural votes out of 109,581 — i.e., rather more than
one-fifth of the total number. These are thus
divided :—Balmain, 9789 voters—non-residential,
2172; Canterbury, 18,704-4008 ; Central Cumber-
land, 9914—1875; Glebe, 4752—450; Newtown,
8163 —1342 ; Paddington, 10,390 —1578 ; Redfern,
9900—907 ; St. Leonards, 7379—1701; East Syd-
ney, 10,041 —4708: South Sydney, 9862—1148;
West Sydney, 10,821—2440. Under such cir-
cumstances it is wonderful that any Labour mem-
bers were elected at all.
B. D. Morehead has been fare welled in
London at a banquet, and is coming mail-haste “ to
rescue Queensland from her present straits.”
Morehead is aghast at the result of the N.S.W.
elections, and. thinks he would be failing in his
duty to Queensland if he absented himself longer.
“ The Labour-members,” says he, “are agitators
who are ready to set class against class, and never
cared for the welfare of the labourer.” Morehead
did—in 1884 ! In that year he tried to hoodwink
the very democratic constituency of Fortitude
Valley by opposing the late F. Beattie, but some
of the latter’s friends, on polling-day, just loaded
a lorry up with stage kanakas and Chinese, all
sandwiched with placards—“ Vote for my welly
good friend, Missee Morehead,” and the working-
man saw through all the cabs and beer, and placed
Morehead a good last. Then, on the death of
sturdy old Crimean-shirted Jacob Low, he sneaked
to the back-block squatting electorate of the
Balonne (pronounced locally “Balloon ”), and got
returned for that. He has been in the balloon
ever since, but ho may soon fall out of it. More-
head has brilliant talents, and an almost intuitive
knowledge of party-politics, but his sympathies—
as squatters’ agent, banker, importer, forward and
supply agent, &c. —are invariably against the
wage-earner. He loves labour only at election-
times, and never at all so long as he’s safe in his-
balloon.
In a Lightning- Bath.
The world has now partially discovered that mere*
medicaments cannot be used as probably remedial
for all diseases. In almost every case of illness:
excepting those of violent accidents and judicial,
hangings, the knife or the detergent are used withi
a free hand, and frequently with unfavourable.*
results. But, widely speaking, we,are gradually-
coming out of this darkness of blind trust in ai
nasty herb, or a sharp knife, and recognising thatt
in almost every case of disease the cause is the;
absence in the suffering system of electricity, the;
primal principle of all life. Celsus, an ancient
gentleman who was quite an authority ora
the merits and demerits of drugs and laxatives
in his time used to say, “ the best of all
medicines is none,” and gave expression to an
unswerving faith in medical electricity, which
was repeated centuries later by Hoffman in these
words : “ Electricity is the fundamental cause of
all vital motion. Medicine will never progress
until we apply to it the laws of mechanics and
hydraulics.” The temporary cure for cancer to-
day is the knife, which merely prunes the poisonous
roots and makes them grow more rapidly. Who
shall say that cancers will not yet be destroyed,
by plunging into them an electric needle, charged;
with a current of local electricity, sufficiently,
strong to blast the growth of the horrible disease,,
and yet not strong enough to inflict harm,
on the patient. It may be remarked that no-
mechanism could be invented delicate enough,,
to register the varying susceptibilities of;
various subjects, but a recognised in-
stitution of Melbourne, the Bethel Electric:
Medical Baths, 293 Lonsdale-street, gives the;
answer to all that. Patients of extremely various;
strengths, from the child suffering from infantile:
paralysis to the aged Midas who has been mar-
tyred by gout, are treated daily from the great"
batteries, and yet delicate mechanism controlling;
the batteries gives the supplies of life according to>
the strength of each subject. If ever required—
and that contingency will arise from experimen-
tary reasons only—the waters of the baths couldl
be turned into seas of fire; as it is, there is merely
a gentle flow without any perceptible “shook..”’
The method of application is just this : There ar©
three baths, to each of which electricity is supplied
by the mechanism just alluded to. The baths are
made of pure copper, and filled with warm water,
through which a double current of electricity is
circulated. The patient has no contact with any
conductor. He merely reclines on a porous
wooden stretcher with cane supports for the head
and shoulders, and is kept in the bath for from
20 to 45 minutes, just as his fancy or necessity
decide. There are trained attendants of both
sexes for the male and female divisions of the
baths, and the Medical Superintendent, Dr. 11. B.
Wilson, personally regulates the electric charges.
The proprietary do not claim the ability to cure
one disease —they have the baths and know that
the system has cured scores of digestive, circu-
latory, nervous and cutaneous ailments. No
one knows how it is that the combinations
of electrical currents produce such results,
but there are the results—solid facts which
all the talk in creation cannot controvert.
One might say that the Batteries said to the .
Bath’s Co. shareholders “You build the institu-.
tion; give us a little sodium occasionally, and get;
your subjects—we’ll do the rest.” Three years: ;
back a Sandhurst man was found to be suffering
from paralysis of the optic nerve, resulting in M
almost complete blindness. He had 21 baths, andq
is now able to read small print and look at a ,
natural horizon where before his vision was.
bounded by a false horizon formed by the motion-.
less nerve. A 47 years martyr to gout was carried i
to the baths howling with pain—he had 42 bathsj
and can now walk as briskly as a young man..
Another man had been suffering for over-
-30 years with gout. The last two years of i
that period he passid in bed perfectly helpless:.
A course of 36 baths compassed a perfect cum.
A young lady who had been a cripple fronn
rheumatism for six years entered the baths with
one knee three times its normal size through gouty
deposit there. She was under treatment for two
months, and shortly afterwards walked a distance
of six miles. A case of disease of the kidneys
resulted in a cure, after several renal calculi
(stones) had been dislodged and pa c sed, when only
two baths had been taken. The oases of acute
sciatica, lumbago, neuralgia, and ot ovarian and
uterine diseases successfully treated by this cura-
tive are legion, and in one instance of paralysis,.
Where both sphincter muscles were totally power-
less, perfect control was almost immediately
restored. Owing to the fact that the Bethel
Baths, at Melbourne, named after their inventor
and patentee, are the second and last institution,
of their kind in the world, suffering Queens-
landers, Tasmanians, and even New Zea-.
landers come to the Marvellous for treatment.
Already the system of medical electricity
has proved beyond doubt its sterling curative ,
virtues where success depends on the dissolution .
of an internal growth, so will the same force,
under greater tension, destroy the cancerous-;
affections that have killed so many. If neuralgia ,
is the prayer of the nerves for healthy blood, andl
paralysis the protest of overworked muscles, those;
electro motors that are so carelessly impoverished'*
and these two are curable by electricity —if elec-
tricity can give movement to a dead thing, surely
it can arrest the growth of a cancer, or any other
form of parasitic ulcer. The work of electro-
medication, and, above all, that systematised in
the regime of the Bethel Baths, has been wonder-
fully productive of success at the present, and its
certain future position is that of the moit effective
of any of the slayers of disease.

Personal Items.
Not Tozer. Towzer.
Sir Alfred Stephen will enter his 90th year
on 2nd proximo.
The latest Sydney hotel sign is “ The Sir
George Grey,” Yet Grey is a teetotaller.
A bad newspaper calls Wales “ His Royal
Lowness.” “ His Royal Ovveness,” it means,
Dibbs has 14 daughters. Parkes, in pro-
posing Woman Suffrage, is deliberately playing
into his hands.
While Lady Hopetoun wears her arm in a
sling she needn’t shake hands with anybody.
Clever woman !
The Rothschilds, it transpires, are heavily
interested in some of the large Maoriland meat-
freezing companies.
Capt. Verney’s father is living, aged 90,
so “Verney the Vampire,” vide Pendragon, may
be a baronet ere his sentence expires.
June 24th was the 20th anniversary of the
suicide of the poet Adam Lindsay Gordon, who
lies buried in Brighton Cemetery, near Mel-
bourne.
Six years ago, John Hancock, M.L.A. for
Collingwood, was a comp, on the Home News,
which now congratulates him on his demagogic
elevation.
The mining Archduke, John of Austria,
supposed to have been lost on the voyage to South
America, is said, on good authority, to be at pre-
sent in Adelaide.
The best portrait of Bernhardt is Bastien
Lepage’s, painted 1879. Lepage was the artist
whom Marie Bashkirtseff loved. They both lay
dying in one room—of phthisis.
The b ;st Parisian photo, of Melba, in Mel-
bourne, is with her little boy standing up behind
her, on a chair, with his arms clasped round her
neck—a proper little Armstrong.
Attached to a public-house in Sussex-
f treet, Sydney, and used as a sitting-room, is the
cabin, complete, of the ship in which E. H.
Hargreaves, the gold-discoverer, first reached
Australia.
Walter Scott, the London publisher, is a
remarkable man. He is an iron-master, a big
contractor, a colliery-owner and a soap-maker.
He started as a working mason, and his father
was a XNevvcastie snoemaKer.
Rather odd that Melbourne City Mission
should entrust their defence, in the Barber case, to
a Jew, Mr. Leon. He handled the case admirably,
and indeed stands next to Isaacs amid the
H brews at the Victorian Bar.
Charlie Huddle, 70 years of age, who has
been over 40 years in Australia and has never
before recorded a vote, walked eight miles into
Wagga, through mud and water, to record a
plumper for the Labour candidate.
At a recent Dubbo public meeting, when
W. P. Crick referred passingly to Parkes’ vanity
and ambition, a noisy elector interjected—“ What
is your ambition ? ” “To sit still and behave
myself,” replied Crick imperturbably.
Parkes boasts that as his train left Castle
maine (Vic.) the local girls kissed their hands to
him. Pooh ! We once saw forty women kiss
their claws to the late Brigham Young as he
steamed out of Salt Lake depot going East.
In the new N.S.W. Legislative Assembly
there are two Abbotts, two Browns, three Clarkes,
two Cullens (brothers), two Fitzgeralds, two
Hutchinsons, two M‘Kinnons, two Parkes
(father and son), two Smiths, and two Wilkinsons.
Sampson’s eloquent little sermon on
Cardinal Newman, which appeared in the Referee
after Newman’s death, is now accounted for by
the fact that the writer belonged to the R.C.
church. Few people knew it until he came to be
buried.
Shires, the snake-man, they say, is making
his will in Bairnsdale, Gippsland. Australia still
remembers how Mr. Drummond, P.M., suicided
with one of Shires’ snakes, and how Underwood,
owner of the rival antidote to Shires, was killed by
one of his own snakes, in Collingwood,
A crank named Guesdon has just died at
Clapham, London, leaving to charities in England,
Tasmania, and Victoria, £300,000. He once had
an office in Melbourne, and was known to tho
world as a most overbearing potentate; but no
one appeared to know the nature of his occupa-
tion.
“ Pendragon ” Sampson became sole pro*
prietor of the Referee on the death of Ashton
Dilke. He was editor of Chatto and Windus’
“Slang Dictionary,” and kept a dozen hunters,
which he rode regularly. He was twice married
and leaves a daughter by his first wife and a
widow.
' Cunninghame Graham recently visited
Morocco in quest of health, and was well received
until it was found that he was prominently iden-
tified with the English eight-hours’ movement.
As they only work two and a-half hours daily in
Morocco, Graham was looked on as a noxious
reactionary.
Diamond Beaney’s funeral was about tho
biggest since Fawkner’s. They say he left
Cromwell House to his housekeeper. If so, ’tis a
mighty big thing, for it is valued at £BO,OOO, and
must have cost him a good deal more, furnishing
and all, even apart from the diamonds that may
be lying about perifiiskus.
Lady Hopetoun, at Bendigo, was delighted
to see the hovel in which Lord Salisbury, then
Lord Robert Cecil, lived in 1853. Graham Berry
unearthed the historical fact—though it had been
duly recorded in Therry’s Reminiscences, pub-
lished years before—while hobnobbing with Salis-
bury in a garden-party at Hatfield House.
W. T. Stead takes a flying trip to Australia
shortly, about an Imperial Federation idea,
strictly original and patent. Meanwhile the Re
view of Reviews falls off, and is sadly reminiscent
of the new broom. The idea is that Stead will
leave it, after all, and go for an active political
career, p rhaps aiming at a Radical Premiership.
Some years ago Quong Tart, Sydney’s boss
Chinese, was tendered a banquet locally. Reply-
ing to the toast of his health, lie said : “Some
people tink ’cos a man born in China he a heathen
and savage—might just a well say ’cos Jesa Cly
born in a stable he ahorse.” The soul of Dean
Swift has apparently been reincarnated in Quong.
A daughter of George IV. has been ad-
mitted, ill and destitute, to Camberwell (Eng.)
workhouse. George was about the only King
whose illegitimates were not all ennobled and pro-
vided with big estates. This lady, born four
years before George’s accession, claims, however,
to have been born in wedlock of a German mother.
The second Murrumbidgee member, Rae,
will be one of the shortest, if not the shortest,
man in the N.S.W. Parliament, with the shortest
name. He is a Maorilander, of Scottish descent,
and has not been resident in Australia quite three
years. Curiously enough, his colleague, G. R.
Dibbs, is the biggest if not the tallest man in the
Assembly.
Extract from speech by that blithering
idiot, Albert Victor, at a London hospital dinner :
In 1881 ray father presided at this festival dinner, on
whioh occasion an important sura was raised for the funds
of the hospital. In conclusion, I beg to state that my
Royal Majesty her mother, I mean her Royal Majesty my
mother, has given an extra donation of £5O. We will now
drink the institution.
Buchanan styles Labouchere “ the Scapin
of politics Stevenson is “a hide-bound genius in
posse;” Archer, “a saturnine, severe young gent, in
a cheap literai'y suit; ” Andrew Lang, “ a typical
Cockney;” Ibsen, “the dustman of a suburb;”
Zola, “the scavenger of a city.” Buchanan, ac-
cording to his own showing, is the literary
vivisectionist of the day.
THE SAME THING.
S HE: “Who is that?”
H E: “That’s the lover.”
SHE: “I wonder if he’s the real or the stage one!”
The motion for the expulsion of Captain
Verney from the Commons was moved almost
without words. It was seconded by a mere lifting
of the seconder’s hat, and carried without a re-
sponsive “aye.” The House was assisting at the
burial of a reputation. We admire this display
of good taste and frankly admit that no Australian
legislature is yet equal to it.
The late H. M. Murphy, of Melbourne,
was once officially waited on by an ignoramus
who wanted to vote, but couldn’t sign his name,
as required. • “Good heavens, my dear man,”
says Murphy, “did you ever know anybody who
could read the signature of any head of depart-
ment in the Civil Service? Put down any
Egyptian hieroglyphs you like.”
The latest popular expression of the creed
of the party of progress, remarks Francis Adams
in a chatty letter to The Bulletin, is so neat as
to threaten to become historical. It is—“a better
adjustment of the world’s treasure, leisure and
pleasure.” Massingham, late editor of the Star
and present editor of Michael Davitt’s Labour
World, is said to be the author.
Gladstone ill, says our cable. All the
Gladstone family fortune came from the slave-
trade, of which Liverpool was head centre, and in
the time of Gladstone’s father, Sir John, Cooke,
the tragedian, was vinous on the stage one night,
whereupon the Liverpudlians hissed. Cooke ad-
vanced to the footlights and exclaimed, “ Men of
Liverpool! Every brick in your accursed city is
cemented with the blood of a slave. ”
Parkes (thinking to “draw ” H.M. for an
autograph letter) caused a volume of the Federa-
tion Convention proceedings to be arrayed in very
elaborate binding at N.S.W. Government Print-
ing Office, and had it posted in time to reach
Queen Victoria as a birthday present on 24th May.
The Bulletin is not authorised to -predict that
Victoria’s acknowledgement will- be published on
an early date in the daily papers, but it makes the
announcement all the same.
Fitzjanies Stephen leaves behind him, on
the English bench, the reputation of the last (it is
to be hoped the last) of the old style of judge who
swore there was but one criminal Gospel, that of
the Gallows, and the lash was its prophet. Not
to put too fine a point on it, he was a cruel brute,
who delighted in making a fine personal perform-
ance of his trials, playing with the prisoner, like
a cat with a mouse, and then suddenly crushing
him by shouting out an appalling sentence.
Mrs. Lynn Linton is 80 years old. When
she was twenty she ministered to Walter Savage
Landor, and was a Republican. At thirty, she
was Radical. At fifty, a Liberal. At sixty, a
Conservative, At seventy, a Tory, and now she
is a shrieking old reactionary, whose chief object
in life is to vilify her own sex. If she’d died at
forty, when she wrote “Joshua Davidson,” her
name would have been reverenced as that of a
dauntless pioneer. It’s a big mistake to outlive
yourself.
An Australian clergyman nastily and
sneakingly hints in the Guardian —the Anglican
Church organ—that one of the reasons why the
Australian Bishops objected to Canon Barlow’s
consecration to the N.Q. see, was that the vetoers
“had unstated reasons” for a “painful duty.”
This is just the creepy, cowardly, snuffling kind
of meanness that blossoms from narrow sectarian-
ism, and makes a healthy man want to kick the
ordinary parson. Barlow’s life has been an open
book that lias never till now been dogs’-eared by
slander.
Newnes, M.P., is, it is cabled, trying to
float his two paste-and-scissors publications,
London Titbits and the Strand Magazine, into
a limited company, capital £400,000. For chilled-
steel cheek, Newnes is peerless. The profits of
Titbits could be equally shared within two years
by any really smart journalist with one-twentieth
of £400,000 at his command, and a clever advertis-
ing manager to run the business; as for the
Strand Magazine, it dates from but yesterday
and may, for all the world knows, die to-morrow.
Mr. “Jim” Morgan, returned as a labour-
protectionist for the Bogan (N.S.W.), and who is
the sort of man to be returned again, is a native of
Tasmania, and will be remembered by thousands
of Sydneyites as the pleasant-mannered blonde
who, for many years, handled all the money at the
the S.M. Herald advertising-counter. Mr. Mor-
gan was manly enough to throw up a good
position for what he regarded as a principle, and
to go to work with pick and shovel. The Bulletin
is not surprised that the same sterling qualities
which popularised him with the Sydney commer-
cial community have had their effect upon the
Bogan electors.
Many years have elapsed since W. S.
Lyster showed the Melbourne Board of Health
round Melbourne Theatre Royal. Said Mr.
Bulger, of the white waistcoat, “I hear com-
plaints of fleas in this theatre.” “What?”
shrieked the impressario, characteristically tug-
ging his beard, “Gentlemen, I have offered a
reward of five pounds for the first flea caught in
this theatre, and it has not been claimed yet
though I have one hundred employes.” Tha\
settled it, though the Board’s palm itched
as it rested on the red upholstery of the upper-
circle. Meanwhile an employe, hard by, was
stuffing a bandana into his mouth.
Sir Henry (then MrQ Parkes was an eai'ly
insurer with the A.M.P. Society. On Feb. 18,
1852, Henry Parkes, of Sydney, newspaper pro-
prietor, insured for £lOOO. Some of the conditions
of the policy (now obsolete with the A.M.P.)
strike one as funny :
If the said Henry Parkes shall he killed in battle, or
shall go on the sea, unless passing to and fro the colonies
of N.S.W , Victoria, South Australia and Van Diemen’s
Land, in vessels exceeding 50 tons burden, or if the said
Henry Parkes shall die by his own hands, or by duelling,
or by the hands of justice, or from casualty by collision
with the aboriginal natives of any part of New Holland,
or in consequence of injuries sustained in any such colli-
sion . . . then this policy shall be void.
According to a Mr. Capper (who heard the
story from the late Isaac Fletcher, an intimate of
Admiral Smyth who was a friend of Capt. Hardy’s
—pretty roundabout, this) Nelson never spoke a
word after he was struck by the fatal bullet. All
the touching little stories about his sayings
before death are purely apocryphal, so it
is now averred. Such, for instance, as:
“Take care of poor, dear Lady Hamilton,
Hardy ; take care of poor Lady Hamilton. Kiss
me, Hardy,” said he. Hardy knelt down and
kissed his cheek, when Nelson said, “Now I am
satisfied. Thank God, I have done my duty,”
And this: “He said to the chaplain, ‘ Doctor, I
have not been a great sinner; ’ and after a short
pause, ‘ Remember that 1 leave Lady Hamilton,
and my daughter Horatia, as a legacy to my
country.’” Hardy, speaking to Smyth, who re-
peated the conversation to Capper, declared these
all to have been the deliberate inventions of Lady
Hamilton.
A curious error crept into an article which
appeared in our issue of last week descriptive of
the establishment of the Richmond Tobacco Co. at
Newcastle, N.S.W. A portrait of Dr. Harris, who
isgenerallyrecognised as theleading shareholder in
the concern, was, by some means, given as a pre-
sentment of the energetic young manager of the
company, Mr. H. Stoddart. Newcastle folk would
smile when they saw that the latter gentleman
(who is a regular Jacob in this respect) had sud-
denly developed a beard like that of the popular
medico. Apologies all round.

and farmers at wholesale prices.

Society
Australia has lost one of its best-advertised
And most remarkable medical men, Mr. J. G.
Beaney, M.L.C., who died at Melbourne last
week, leaving what is understood to be a substan-
tial fortune to be distributed in English and Aus-
tralian charities. He had, so far as is known, no
near relatives, but he owned a large number of
expensive friends and a fine crop of healthy pro-
fessional enemies. He was a man of good medical
instincts, but it was his unbounded push and keen
knowledge of the world (that is to say, his deep
appreciation of the littleness and credulity of
mankind in general) which gave him the enormous
success that he undeniably attained as a money-
making doctor. He began as a chemist’s assistant
in Brighton, England, and (though by that time
legally qualified) followed the same calling when
he first arrived in Victoria; he wound up his
active medical career (it may bo said to have been
terminating gradually for a considerable while
owing to his ill-health) as the doctor who made
more money than any other in Australia barring
one—the Napoleonic-looking and brilliant operat-
ing surgeon J. N. Fitzgerald. Beaney’s practice
was mainly in the way of the Parisian Ricord,
whose barouche, with the well-known piebald
ponies, never stopped before a patient’s door, and
whose coachman was for ever moved on, the Papal
Nuncio once laying a complaint of a stoppage in
front of his mansion.
# * •
Beaney was a showy man, essentially
vulgar—or, let us say, Oriental —in his tastes—a
man of champagne, diamonds and ruby-velvet
dressing-gowns. With him the motto, all through,
was “Show, show, advertise, advertise.” If a
book appeared under his name, you might bet
your life that someone else had written it. He
boasted that he had, 100 times without a failure,
performed the master-operation for hernia—
which, if correct, entitled him to a big reputation
as a clever and lucky surgeon. But he too often
acted as if be were following a trade instead of a
profession. If he performed a lithotomy opera-
tion, the ghastly results were put in the public
window at the medical bookseller’s, and labelled
“Extracted by Dr. Beaney,” whose grateful
patient, cruel to relate, had gone to the cold and
silent tomb. For example, a gigantic calculus,
one of the biggest known, taken from the body of
one Robert Berth, at Melbourne Hospital, was
thus displayed in the window of friend Bailliere,
duly ticketed in the Beaney interest, and it was
in reference to this that what was practically a
charge of manslaughter came to be formulated
against the man of fizz and glitter, whose
enemies alleged that, instead of crushing
the stone—which he might, they urged, have
done after making the incision he, in his
anxiety to secure a prize advertising - speci-
men, deliberately hauled out the big pebble
with the forceps, hopelessly tearing the surgical
wound. At the inquest, Beaney"s end of the log
was brilliantly upheld by J. L. Purves, whose
cross-examination of the opposing doctors was the
most masterly legal effort in the present writer’s
journalistic experience, and it was at the time
favourably compared with Aspinall’s able defence
of Beaney when the latter was tried for
attempting to procure abortion on a St. Kilda
barmaid years previously. On both occasions
Beaney was acquitted.
• « *
However, it was not on account of the
alleged irregularity of much of Beaney’s practice
that his brother Sawbones so bitterly opposed
him. It was on account of his professional money
success and the factors therein —his immense
brass plate, his “ghosted” books, his Imperial
collection of diamonds —auctioned for £3o,ooo—his
unscrupulous and well-creased “ hangers-on ” (the
disclosures in the suit of Bailliere v. Beaney
showed him in his advertising nakedness to the
world), the enormous sums he used to spend when-
ever necessary in securing his election as “ senior
surgeon”— i.e., the surgeon returned at the head
of the poll—to Melbourne Hospital.
• # •
The Doctor, whose various titles were nok
exhausted by twenty letters in the obituary, will
probably not cut up very rich. He was an
extremely generous man, and spent almost as
freely as he received. He had not long inhabited
his expensive Cromwell House, built on dear land
at the Collins and Russell-streets corner, and
some of the current depreciations have told on his
investments. In appearance he was a little
squat, dandy, podgy, Dailey-figured man, who
parted his hair in the middle ; like Dailey, it was
his pride and joy to wear a velvet coat and an
enormous camellia in his buttonhole; his manner
was always that of perfect sang-froid. He is
spoken of by his intimate friends as a man to be
loved for his own sake. Some of the Melbourne
daily papers never lost a chance of flaying him;
but then most of the anti-Beaney diatribes were
written in blood with scalpels, and not by ordi-
nary journalists with office-ink. Beaney’s pro-
fessional kindliness did even more to establish
him in the good graces of the reporters, with
whom he was, at any rate years ago, all powerful,
than his magnums of champagne.
It is said that Dr. Beaney predicted the
time and manner of his death to his housekeeper,
the well-known ‘'Susan” (Miss Treacy), whose
name, by the way, not Susan. Which recalls
how curious Dr. Tracey, Beaney’s now long-
defunct next-door neighbour, was before his death
to know the nature of the vast cancerous mass
found within his body. Also that Cuvier, dying,
called attention to the muscles of his own mouth
as illustrating his favourite theory,
# * *
Dr. Beaney died partly of the Watson-
Kitz case and partly of the Victorian Law Depart-
ment —two diseases and both of them incurable.
He died of the Law Department because it
provides a cold, damp corridor for witnesses to
stand about in, among miscellaneous draughts and
cold breezes and hunks of damp that settle on
their lungs; and he died of the WatsomKitz case
because it was that case which left him standing
among all these malign influences. Truly the evil
that men do lives after them. Old Watson made
money, which was bad ; and then he appointed
trustees to take care of it; and then these trustees,
who wouldn’t have existed if Watson hadn’t made
money, objected to Kitz ; and finally they
summoned Beaney as a witness and left him in a
damp spot as if he had been a frog, and he died as a
consequence,
* # *
Says a cynical correspondent : —“ Cham-
pagne ” Beaney, the “ diamond doctor,” of
Melbourne, has given his last dinner. His
belief in the life-sustaining properties of gold-top
was not warranted by results, or it may be that
his average absorption of the elixir, which he
fixed at two quarts per diem, was somewhat ex-
cessive for a man who took little delight in
muscular exertion. Anyway, he died before his
time, and on Wednesday (Ist) the medical pro-
fesh mustered around Doctor Beaney’s grave in
Melbourne General Cemetery. Much of the exag-
gerated eulogy bestowed upon Beaney by a section
of the local press may be ascribed to his liberality
and fizz. His hand was as free with his money as
with the operating-knife, and his many friends
were outnumbered, until sickness arrested the
flow of income and hospitality, by the host of
toadies, journalistic and otherwise, who advertised
his name. Time was when one couldn’t pick up a
Melbourne paper without discovering some para-
graph or other wherein Beaney figured as a social
or surgical hero. His appetite for notoriety was
insatiable, and as the press, becoming less paro-
chial, gradually dropped the custom of puffing
doctors in general, and Beaney in particular, life
AN ILLUSTRATED FAR ABOUT AN ANXIOUS PA.
ANXIOUS BUT THRIFTY FATHER : “ Doctor, if you can cure him for less than the funeral
expenses, go ahead; but if you can’t, sonny will have to take his chances.”
h
seemed to lose ft good deal of its savour
for this falling star. That Beaney had solid
claims to be considered an “eminent ” surgeon, in
the Lancet sense of the term, is undeniable, for
he could probably have chopped up a dead bullock
with greater rapidity and precision than most of
his Australasian rivals. But it is to be feared
that, like most of the craft, he unnecessarily
sacrificed scores of limbs to his passion for a
“ splendid operation,” whilst the monstrous
charges he exacted from sufferers who were not
rich, or from their relatives who could ill afford
the fee, made it impossible to regard Beaney as
anything of a philanthropist. A surgeon who
wants a gaudy house with a tower to it, unlimited
champagne, diamonds and flatterers, is an even
more mitigated blessing to the community than a
physician of similar tastes. There is little
virtue in robbing honest Peter of a hundred
guineas and his leg, in order that loafer Paul may
be able to borrow a fiver and load himself up with
liquor at his patron’s expense. However, Beaney
was a real good fellow as the world goes, nor any
more of an over-rated humbug than a verdant
doctor-ridden community insisted on making him
in those halcyon days when he was the high-
priest of the butchery trade.
* • •
On his first trial for his life Dr. Beaney
was so poor that he had to mortgage all he had in
the world including his furniture, instruments,
&c., to House, the corn-merchant, of Elizabeth-
street for £lOOO to pay the expenses of his defence.
He was at first defended by Dawson, when the
jury disagreed. At the second hearing Aspinall
appeared for him, and acquittal was the result.
* * *
A parson who ’ started a big religions
revival was discovered Gardinerising some of the
lambs of his fold while the latter were under the
hysterical influence of penitence. The parson was
expelled, and duly went under for some time.
Two or three years later, however, he reappeared
locally as a bright and shining light and a famous
preacher. An acquaintance, who saw his name
advertised in the sermon-columns, went to hear
him preach, and after the service sought him out
and said : “ Well, yon have the most altitudinous
gall I ever noticed. To think that you dare
preach here again I” “My dear brother,
answered the shepherd, quietly, “ the Lord has
forgiven me all my sins, and I should like to pray
with you for your conversion.”
# # *
The wife of a late N.Z. Cabinet Minister
recently called on another Maoriland dame of less
exalted social status to solicit a bazaar-subscrip-
tion. The visitor was very kind and condescend-
ing in her way, and the lady of the house was
properly respectful and gave the fair cadger a
liberal contribution. After pocketing the money
the Ministerial lady became less affable, and on
taking her leave freezingly remarked ; “ You will,
of course, understand, Mrs. Blank, that this is
purely a business visit.” “Oh, don’t mention it,
Mrs. Dash,” said the other; “we are getting
quite used to that sort of thing, now. Why, it
was only this very morning I had another woman-
canvasser here—trying to sell me a medical-work
on female diseases. Just fancy!! Good after-
noon.”
# * •
Bernhardt decidedly did not shine, at the
departure of the Oroya, from Melbourne for
Adelaide. Her beastly big dawg annoyed the
high-toned passengers, and she went about smack-
ing a riding-whip against her gown, and com-
plaining. The sickening thing was the long string
of male sycophants after her, the poodles, while
Government officials vied in their glee over re-
lieving railway porters of her bags. Beauty draws
us by a single hair —-even when it’s put on. You
think, perchance, it is Sara’s hair, but it may be
the hair off a poor red-towheaded French girl,
lying buried in a churchyard of La Bretagne. It
may not be so. There may even be no enamel
in danger of cracking, Peachbloom may grow
naturally on the cheek of budding fifty.

Now that the Gaiety Company is back in
Melbourne, the Government whip always knows
whereto rush in search of the Ministerial majority
when it is wanted in a close division. All he lias
to do is to gallop round in a cab to the theatre,
and grab the entire contents of the six front rows,
and bring them back with him, and the country is
saved once more.
# * •
A keen-looking man had almost completed
all the arrangements for the purchase of a half-
acre block in North Broken Hill, and was just
about to tender a deposit to seal the bargain,
when the would-be seller, who seemed to suddenly
“ suspicionise something,” abruptly asked —“ what
do you propose to use this land for ? ” “To build
a church on.” “ Blash, dank, startler it!” ex-
claimed the negotiating unbeliever, “this bargain’s
off! I ain’t going ter have no church on my
land to ruin the value of the ’jacent property.”

Strained relations exist between the
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners and Mr. Cowdery,
the well-known Government engineer, on the sub-
ject of a free pass for life issued to Mr. Cowdery
on his retirement and now sought to be with-
drawn. The story of the embroglio is said to be
a very pretty little history, indeed.

During the voyaging of a certain burlesque
crowd to these shores, the chief steward had a
pretty rough time with some of the tow-headed
ballet element. One day at dinner he ventured to
request a frowsy frail thing “to try and behave
yourself.” The retort was keen, convincing and
loud —“Oh, go to h , Jones ! ”

The now celebrated Tyson divorce-case is
to go before the N.S.W. Full Court, at the
instance of petitioner, who still contends that the
ceremony which formally joined him to respon-
dent was, in law, no marriage. The case will
probably reach the Privy Council for final adjust-
ment —and Mr. Tyson will, whatever the ultimate
issue, have the pleasure of paying all the costs.
* # #
A commercial traveller complains in a
letter to the press that Bruce Smith tin; N.S.W.
Minister of Works, who, the other day, in an
otherwise crowded train, had an eight-seat com-
partment all to himself, turned out him, the bag-
man, who now enquires whether B.S. aforesaid
had paid, on the occasion, for eight seats, or one,
or none. The Bulletin, having travelled with
and lived at hotels frequented by “commercials,”
rejoices in having at last discovered one man
capable of resisting a “drummer’s ” rush. Ordin-
arily, when a member of the modest and unassum-
ing guild of bagmen sees a pie, he reaches out his
hand therefor, and then that is his pie in one act.
It would do the Commercial Travellers’ Associa-
tion a whole heap of good to collide with a snag
like Bruce Smith every day for a week.
The Melbourne theatres are rather haunted
of late by a buzzing bevy of lady journalists, who
seem to have taken the drama under their protec-
tion, also to be sweetly persuaded that they bring
cultohaw to the dress-circle, and confer an inesti-
mable benefit upon society every time they sweep
tlie landscape with a glance that signifies—“ You
see I have come.” Some of these disturbing
apparitions betray a marked tendency to come it
too thick —as they say at Toorak —and all along of
the Austral Salon. As the representatives of
sundry local productions boasting a combined cir-
culation of next to nothing worth mentioning,
and congratulatresses of the various managements
upon an everlasting sequence of brilliant suc-
cesses, these erudite females have their humorous
side. Nay, the side they put on would be scream-
ingly funny if it were restricted to the front of
the house and disassociated from the pretentious
imbecility of that darned Salon. The Salon is
becoming too ghastly a joke altogether. A skirted
compiler of tasteless journalistic taffyis harmless
enough whilst she merely smears it over the
columns of the Mqon, Kitchen Cackle, Linger-
ing Death, or what not; besides, the wild,
luxuriant adulation of a female whose hairpins
outnumber her readers is apparently soothing to
theatrical managers, but when she forms a
dowdy picnic party for the purpose of swooping
down behind the scenes and congratulating Sara
Bernhardt on behalf of the Salon, and in the
sacred name of Australian literature, it is time
that James Smith, or some responsible person
like that, dropped the silly sisterhood down a
well-hole to cool. Journalism which is journalism
doesn’t make “ cultchaw ” its war-cry, nor
should it allow what cultchaw it possesses
to be misrepresented by the chattering little
magpies who greeted Sara with a floral
tribute addressed “La Grand Bernhardt.” Those
Salon nymphs who get into theatres on the Press
nod certainly require a lot of looking after. The
necessity for chaining them up betvveen the acts is
extremely urgent. When Sara received that his-
torical wreath on her first night at the Princess’
she naturally imagined that the Salon was an
elementary French class, or an asylum for would-
if-they-could-be people of neglected education.
Or, perhaps, she thought it was Dan O’Connor.
When Sara found, however, that the spokeswomen
of that bogus institution were molesting her in a
‘ ‘ Press ” capacity—well, it was mighty rough on
the trade in general. And this comes, as W, S.
Gilbert would say, of women figuring in two
capacities when they have no capacity at all, but
simply a cast-iron cheek. Not that there is
nothing fresh and enticing about many of their
cheeks. Very nice girls, as a rule, fine, handsome
creatures, if they happen to be no longer girls.
Their figures are ever so much better than their
French, and the depth of meaning in their eyes is
past all understanding. In point of fact, it is a
hard matter to find fault with anything they do,
even though one knows that beauty is only skin-
deep, and women are vain things seeking w hat
they can devour. These last few lines ought to
make ’em feel all right again.
# * *
“ Williamson, Garner, and Co.” still
appears in the advts., but Garner, as suggested in
last issue, has no longer any interest in the old
firm. His furniture at the St. Kilda domicile was
auctioned the other day, and on Saturday (18th)
that amiable cynic sails for Europe. The fas-
cinating eyes of Mrs. Garner have been tenderly
fixed upon the northern hemisphere for a long
time past, whilst Arthur, Esq., is a well-to-do
gentleman who takes hemispheres just as they
come and doesn’t mind much.
# # #
A concert was given last" week by the
young ladies of East Melbourne Presbyterian
College, but not a word was spoken about Miss
Mitchell (Madame Melba) having been trained
by that institution :
Oh no, we never mention her, her name is never heard,
Oar lips are now forbid pronounce that once familiar word.
The clergymen’s committee says, in effect, “We
undertake to make the young Miss Tallowfats
musical dabs, but we also guarantee that they
shall not be qualified so highly as to become stars
of the Paris Grand Opera.” What is the use of a
good thing if you are forbidden to aspire to the
best? Melba is taboo at the College. Rev.
Mungo Malagrowther gets up and congratulates
the principal on the musical progress, but says not
a word about black-sheep Melba. From the Mala-
growther view-point, one would say, “ Why
instruct the girls in music with such fearful
danger?” The reply would be —“We only want
them brought up to the exact standard re pared to
snavel a rich husband.”
* * #
Some yet unhanged inventor has offered
to the Victorian Railway department a new and
fiendish invention for closing carriage-doors. In-
stead of the porter rushing along the platform,
and closing each one separately the engine-driver
will turn a handle at the moment of starting, and
bang the whole row simultaneously by steam-
power. And as no one will see him coming it
naturally follows that the infant which is leaning
out will have its fingers squashed, and the fat
woman who is late will be stunned and cast sense-
less on the floor of the carriage, and the last man
will be partially murdered by getting a sGlb door
in his spine just as he is rushing in ; and the train
will start amid the shrieks of the wounded, and
the howls of the dead, and the hysterics of the
survivors, and general ruin, gore, brains and con-
sternation.

Fully one-third of the cases under the new
Victorian Divorce Act are applications from wives
who married under the age of 20—often a good
way under it. There is a woeful sameness about
these cases. The bride was a young lady in a
shop or a factory, with a big bunch of hair dragged
■down over her forehead to make a bang or a fringe
or something, and another big mass of thatch
piled up on the summit of her cranium. The
bridegroom was a leery youth with high heels and
a dog. They met in the street and he whistled at
her. Then they met again and he winked. Then
they got married and had a sticky infant, and a
wooden cottage garnished with time-payment
urniture, and matrimony was a failure.
* * *
Au unfortunate foreigner named Ocki has
decided to do no more good turns for the
Melbourne Public Library. Ocki bought a French
grammar from a man in the Exhibition Gardens,
and subsequently discovered that it bore the
Library stamp. So he promptly took the book
back to the place from whence it came, when the
Library trustees prosecuted him for theft. Asa
matter of course, the foreign gentleman was dis-
charged with the Court’s fullest sympathy, and
the interests of justice were served by both sides
paying their own legal expenses. Ocki didn’t
even get back the trifle he paid for the grammar.

The average church-goer puts something
on the parson’s plate, and calls that “serving
God.”
* * *
The Victorian Undertakers' Association
held their inaugural bean-feast in one of Mel-
bourne’s multifarious town halls last week. The
outside world was represented by a parson, a
lawyer, a doctor and a chemist —(four representa-
tives of trades that assist to send us to the ceme-
tery before our time) and proceedings were of the
usual gloomily hilarious character. Nothing, in
fact, occurred to interrupt the flow of soul, except
the suggestion, made by a journeyman coffin-
maker, that Sunday funerals should be abolished,
and the mute allowed his Day of Rest, on which
to take out the family and prepare his soul to die.
Whereupon the chairman replied that such a
concession was impossible. Corpses had to be
considered, especially in warm weather, and he
need hardly remind the company that it is often
impossible to keep ’em over Sunday. Then every-
body relapsed into a ruminative sniff and mur-
mured, “ That’s very true.”
# # #
An evidently guileless matron wrote to a
London lady’s paper for information on the sub-
ject of soap for the children’s heads, and signed
herself “La Traviata.” Perhaps she, or her
husband, has since discovered the meaning of that
naughty nom-de-plume, which somehow reminds
us of the Sydney C.M.G. who boasted that all his
English relations belonged to the fashionable
demi-monde; also of the Sydney lady-scribe who
signs herself “Faustine.”
* * *
The leading gentleman in a recent high-
toned Melbourne wedding is said to have started
married life with 72 new suits of clothes. If he
should begin to put on flesh as most bridegrooms
do, the chances are against him ever putting on
all the suits of clothes.
* * *
While visiting Nelson (M.L.) one day,
Governor Onslow ran out of matches. Entering a
store he put down a penny and was handed the
usual two boxes. “What, are they only a
ha-penny ?” said the haughty Earl. “Then give
me one box.” And he took his ha-penny change
with the pleased feeling of a man who has nipped
a reckless extravagance in the bud.
* # *
If you want to bow to a lady nowadays
the orthodox thing is to remove the hat with
extreme velocity from the cranium, then carry it
quickly down till the back of the hand strikes the
hip, and the hat itself is wobbling among the
owner’s feet. While taking off the tile the
operator breaks off suddenly at the waist, and the
top half of him bobs abruptly forward in a respect-
ful manner. This internal cataclysm is accom-
panied by a smile of recognition. The sudden
jerk forward brings the patient into concussion
with his hat which is just returning from its
expedition, and if the thing is done properly it
hits him on the stomach. Then he looks at it in
a surprised manner as if he didn’t expect to see it
there. Next he upends the hat and glares re-
proachfully at the inside of it as if he thought he
might find soma kittens in it, and there being no
kittens he plants it firmly on his head again,
returns to the perpendicular, and goes away. By
this time the bewchus woman has got round the
Corner into the next street,

A universal provider in London now lends
out a £lOOO Bank of England note to be exhibited
at weddings as the present of the bride’s loving
parent, and makes a big percentage on it. He
also lends out a bogus man-servant, who looks
like the old family retainer, but whose real
business is to die in the last ditch on top of the
mangled remains of the bride and all her friends
and relatives, before he lets any of them make off
with the property. Possibly the note is a bogus
r one after all, though, and the detective who
x wa,tches it is merely put on to give a look of prob-
ability to the proceedings and to swell the bill.
A serious trouble broke out the other day
in a distinguished-looking mansion down by the
sad sea waves, at Brighton (Vic.). Relations had
been strained for a long time, however, and the
explosion wasn’t altogether unexpected. The
owner of the establishment was a tall, elderly in-
dividual with a wisp of moist and weak grey hair
dabbed down in the centre of his head, and his
wife was a large Roman-figured female of 29, with
an aquiline nose; and they had horrid rows daily.
The cause of the rows was the other man—the one
that the lady was supposed to correspond with, but
no one could ever drop on any actual proofs of the
correspondence, till the other day, when the whole
business blew up like kerosene stuffed with thun-
der. There had been the usual row—only a little
worse than usual, and the enraged capitalist went
out and banged the door behind him, and
wandered about cursing. He had a firm convic-
tion that his wife would immediately write to the
other fellow telling him about the trouble, and
lamenting that she was married to a hog, and he
wanted badly to capture the letter and bowl them
both out. And then a great and brilliant idea
struck him. He loafed around till well on in the
afternoon, and then he went and waited in the
dusk outside the other fellow’s house. The post-
man wasn’t due till 5.30, and by that time it was
very nearly dark. When the official loomed up in
the distance, the watcher opened the gate and
stole softly up the path on to the other fellow’s
verandah, and composed himself in an easy,
familiar attitude against a pillar. He was smok-
ing a cigar and looked quite at home, and when he
held out his hand for the letters, the whole of the
other fellow’s correspondence was shoved into it
without the slightest hesitation. He pocketed
the lot and fled, and just as he was going through
the gate the other fellow sighted him and the post-
man from the window and realised that every-
thing was up. And so it was, for two minutes
later the elderly husband had read his wife’s letter,
and about six seconds after that he had rushed
home and shoved the Roman-looking female down
the front steps.

Shiels’ Divorce Act,
to put the matter
bluntly, permits people
to “ marry on trial” —the
only marr age that is
not a failure. After all,
there was a lot of philosophy in Lord Dundreary’s:
“ Marry me for a fortnight, my dear, and if you
don’t like me you can go back to your ma.”
# # *
As an amorous Rockhampton railway
official was lurching homewards “ under the
influence” a few nights ago, he made the agreeable
discovery that a lady was beckoning to him from
a verandah. Nothing loth, he boldly opened a
front gate and marched on to the premises, where
he started to enact the “ balcony scene ” with the
unknown Juliet. Getting no response from his
idol (literally), he rushed frantically on to the
verandah and clasped in his arms —the wooden
figurehead of a wrecked vessel, which image the
housefather had fixed up on his verandah. The
disgusted Romeo gave Juliet a sling over his
shoulder, and slouched off in search of real
femininity.

# * *
The chief employment going in Brisbane
just now is escaping from bailiffs. You may see
dozens of Brisbane escapees on Sydney streets at
all times in the day. Most have merely fallen out
of the frying-pan into the fire.
It is possible to keep the atmosphere of a
smoking-room perfectly clear by placing a ring of
platinum over a flame; this, when red-hot, con-
sumes smoke as fast as it is made.
In one respect only does The Bulletin
envy Sydney Echo. That fortunate journal rarely
or never says a nasty thing on its own account.
Such things are always said for it by some con-
venient “correspondent.”
London Zoological Society has acquired a
pair of Tasmanian wolves. The male Tasmanian
wolf has a pouch as well as the female. He uses
his for carrying tobacco, and she fills hers with
powder-puffs and hairpins.
The English papers report that “at the
funeral of the late Archbishop of York the police
arrested several thieves dressed as clergymen.”
But they didn’t capture the principal thief dressed
as a clergyman. He was in the coffin, .and got
buried.
The city of Warrnambool (Vic.) is now in
telephonic communication with the far-distant
township of Melbourne. This opens up the
alarming prospect of the local Mayor Hyland
being, sooner or later, on “ Hello ! ” terms with
his friend Queen Victoria.

paper has been visiting England. He declares
THE LATE DR. BEANEY.
that the English Throne is tottering ; that it is a
rare thing to meet an English statesman who is
not bald ; and that the Salvation Army is no
better than it should be,
“John E. Kelly” asks in the S. M,
Herald— Will the squatters turn their backs on
the “ Free labourers ? ” You bet they will when
it suits them to do it—just as the steamship
owners did with the persons who helped them to
fight the Maritime Unions,

late cablegram about the 1400 prisoners found
armed in gaol refers. To the penitentiary of St-
Quentin, France, or to that of St. Quentin, in.
California—the place where the Irish gaoler gave
all his boarders a full holiday on St. Patrick’s
Day ?

The demoralising effects of capital pxmish-
ment are not confined to the party operated upon
and the operator. They also extendi to the
witnesses. The most shocking illustration of this
law which we have seen for a long tinve- appeared
in a Ballarat paper the other day in the shape of a
reminiscence of the hanging of Bourke. Said the
writer I never saw Corney Bourke but during
that minute on the gallows, yet I will never for-
get that minute. I feel as if'l could write a three-
volume novel about him. ” What ahideousthought 1
And if the ghastly villain to whom it came should
carry it into effect! Why, he will dramatise
it, for certain, and then Dampier will produce it
at the Melbourne Alexandra. And the author of
this paragraph will quit the country.
The case against the Melbourne abor-
tionist, Ellen Taylor, possessed some remarkable
features. The evidence against her consisted
entirely of a statement made by-in fact, almost
wrung from —the deceased woman Carter, during
an agony of suffering, her sedative doses of
morphia having been stopped “on purpose to make
her mind clear.” The medical attendant and con-
stable who procured Mrs. Carter’s dying state-
ment, appear to have entered upon their hideous
task con amove. The dying woman was shrieking
for chloroform, and, according to Barrister Purves
(vide Age report):—
The doctor told her she ought to make a statement and
that, if she did, he would give her chloroform.
The possible inference being that, if she
didn’t, she might scream away in vain
for the anaesthetic. From the Age’s re-
port of the cross-examination we quote—
“ Neither of the doctors found any indications
that instruments had been used before the illness
from which Mrs. Carter died came on, and one of
them added that all the appearances were con-
sistent with there having been a natural abortion.”
Mr. Purves, Q.C., very properly objected to the
Court hanging a woman on the uncorroborated
statement of a person who, if that statement were
correct, was an accomplice, and this point of law
was reserved, though Judge A’Beokett meanwhile
sentenced the moaning wretch to be hanged. The
jury acted in keeping with the other eccentricities
of the case. It found Mrs, Taylor guilty of
“ wilful murder,” but recommended her to mercy.
“On what ground ?” asked his Honor. Foreman :
“Because we do not think it was wilful. ”
Round the Shops and Shows.
Trying weather such as we have experi-
enced for the last three weeks induces one to look
out for a kind of clothing that will counterbalance
the effects on the body of the winterly season.
Wonderfully adapted for this purpose are the
various garments produced under Dr. Jaeger’s
sanitary woollen system, which is nothing short of
a perfect reform in clothing, the essentials being
pure wool, and only pure wool, instead of the
commonly adulterated merino garments, and, the
texture being porous, it allows the skin to breathe
and to thoroughly perform its functions. The
representative of the Company, whose head-
quarters for Australasia are in Wynyard-street, is
always ready to explain to ladies and gentlemen
the interesting details of this system, though—
and this should always be kept in mind—the busi-
ness is conducted strictly upon wholesale lines,
and purchases can only be made at the leading
retail houses in the city. From what the
representative says, combinations in every
form and texture are the leading line of
the establishment, and certainly they must be
popular with the ladies at home, considering that
the company sell thousands of dozens every season.
But, for those who still hesitate to adopt the com-
binations, there are other garments in endless
variety. Ladies vests in numberless styles ; a
great variety of drawers and chemises ; there are
nightdresses, amongst which is the State nightdress,
a garment richly embroidered and trimmed with
handsome lace. Surprising is the extensive range
of garments for children —nine different lines of
girls combinations being kept in stock, and every
line in seven different sizes, so that every age can
be fitted. It appears that special attention has
been bestowed on this department and we can
only wish for the sake of our “ little ones” that
this system of clothing will be well patronised
by the mothers, for there cannot be any doubt
that it is far healthier for the children to wear
pure wool next the body than cold, clammy linen.
Until very recently there was a great difficulty in
obtaining pure woollen garments, especially in the
smaller sizes. The genuine make of these goods
is easily identified ; every article bears a diamond-
shaped tradesmark, enclosing the portrait of that
celebrated German savant, Dr, Jaeger.

What lady is there who doesn’t possess a
piece of old-fashioned jewellery which only needs
the magic touch of a first-class re-modeller to make
it quite modern and wearable ? One of the best
places to go to for this kind of work is the estab-
lishment of Mr. Leon Harris, Market-street,
between Hunter’s Boot Palace and Evening
News office. Here, too, one can obtain the
highest price for opals and pearls in the rough.
• • *
It may not be very polite to say so, but in
some ways ladies are extremely like to a flock of
sheep—they follow the leader. Apropos to this a
certain lady who leads society in Sydney was
observed to enter the ladies’ room of the City
Buffet a few weeks ago. Since then this room
has been besieged at mid-day by hungry shoppers,
who by no means fail to do full justice to the
capital fare provided for the price of one shilling
upward !

£lOO,OOO SWEEP.
(TO THE EDITOR OP “ THE AUSTRALIAN STAR.”)
Sir, —Will you, through your widely-circulated
paper, inform me and the general public if it is a
fact that “ Tattersall George Adams ” has disposed
of the whole or part of his interest in the well-
known Tattersall’s sweep, and that it is now
carried on by a syndicate of Sydney people ? If a
syndicate have it in hand will it be conducted in
the fair and square manner that it was supposed
to be when George Adams had it himself.—Yours,
&c., A SUBSCRIBER.
(“The Australian Star,” Sydney, June 3,1891.)
“ TATTERSALL'S ” CONSULTATIONS.
In answer to “A Subscriber,” whose letter we
published in our issue of yesterday, we have to
state that upon inquiry we find there is no truth
whatever in the statement that “ Tattersall’s” con
sultations have been disposed of to a syndicate.
The consultations are still conducted (and
will continue to be as heretofore) under
the direction and sole proprietorship of
“ Tattersall,” and the same principle of
drawing will always be adhered to.
