It is interesting to the photographic world
to know that Messrs. Baker and Rouse, of Mel-
bourne, Adelaide, and Brisbane, have purchased
the business of Messrs. Lichtner and Co., photo-
graphic stock importer. 1 , and, to have greater
facilities at their command to cater to the wants
of the New South Wales professional and amateur
photographic community, they have removed to
more central and commodious premises situated at
373 George-street, next Australian Joint Stock
Bank. It is their intention to combine the pre-
sent business with that of optical lantern goods,
and they are now showing a splendid assortment of
lantern-slides, plain and coloured, optical lanterns,
and all requisites, and the whole stock, consisting,
as it does, of all the latest and most modern
ideas in photography, is displayed to great
advantage, and is rvell worthy a visit of
inspection. It is worthy of note that Messrs.
Baker and Rouse, apart from their regular ship-
ments by every mail steamer, have at their com-
mand the large manufactory of Messrs. T. Baker
and Co., at Abbotsford, Melbourne, where they
are now making dry plates, opal and lantern
plates, bromide, platinotype and sensitised papers,
pure nitrate of silver, chloride of gold, ana all
sundries, including mounts of every description.
The firm, having establishments in all the Austra-
lian colonies, are in a position second to none in
supplying the requirements of the profession, and
have erected on the premises a dark-room for the
convenience of amateurs. Messrs. Baker and
Rouse are just issuing a new and illustrated
catalogue, which will be w T ell worthy of perusal,
and are sparing no pains to make their Sydney
house as complete as possible, and we cordially
wish thenq success in their enterprise.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
About the latest report from Mr. John L. Sulli-
van is that he is going to light Slavic for any money on
ear tl,—as soon as lie gets back from Australia. It is one
of the saddest features of Sullivan’s latter years that ho
Ins always been going to do something tremendous as
soon as he got back from some place a long distance off.
Therefore, in order to bring the big pugilist to the scratch,
t ie writer of this paragraph will whack him himself at
the earliest possible moment after he gets hack from the
source of the Nile ; and if Sullivan declines to meet him
—when he returns from that river—he will bang him
informally in the public thoroughfare. He won’t bang
him before he goes away because he can’t spare the time,
but he would as soon do it as not, only he is too busy
p veking his carpet-bag.
* * *
Tiie cable states that directly Slavin reached
Queenstown ho struck the attitude which Ajax assumed
towards the lightning and announced his anxiety to fight
Sullivan tor all the money on earth. Meanwhile, the
exploded slugger.was peacefully making the best of his
way hither in the capacity of hactor. At present we dre
pmting td hear Sullivan declare* at the wily suggestion
of his business managers, that the dream of his life is to
meet Slavin (who appears to be training in Paris at the
gay Mabille, or somewhere like that) for ten millions
a side and the skite championship.

John L. Sullivan and his wife, arrive in Sydney
On July 27th. We italicise the fact, in consequence of a
par. that appeared in a Sydney Sunday paper.
# # *
The Walhalla miner who brought an action
against a former chum for half the first prize in Adams’
sweep on the Carbine Cup, has obtained a verdict with
costs. Seeing that expensive gentlemen like Purves were
retained in the case, there isn’t likely to be much prize
left for the other miner, whose main point of defence was
the alleged illegality of sweeps. A Victorian judge
decided that the plea didn’t hold good, inasmuch as
sweeps were legal in N.S.W. (Wka-a-t!), and the Victorian
Police Offence? Act could not be infringed outside the
colony. The case has been a large, gratuitous advt. for
Adams.
# # *
When politician J. D. Young ran the pub. at
the corner of King and George streets, Sydney, a man
employed there turned “rusty” one day, when J.D.
waded in and gave him “ the father of a dressing.” Next
day the man went up to Larry Foley’s White Horse and
asked for a job. He was pretty badly marked, and Larry
asked him how he had received his punishment. Upon
hearing the cailse Larry said, in all seriousness, “if you
would turn on a big man like Mr. Young, what would you
do with a little fellow like me ? No use for you to-day,
thanks.”
* * *
By cable we learn that Jack Slavin has defeated
Australian Billy Smith in the States. The man who can
defeat Billy Smith and be able to fight within middle-
weight limits must he something out of the common.
But “ Billy’s ” best days are long gone for ever ; his fight
with McCarthy in Foley’s White Horse was one of the
best.

The Sydney amateur light-weight championship
was won by Edward Burden, who knocked-out James
M’Ginley after 17 terrific rounds. M'Ginley, who fought
very gamely, was badly cut up.
# # #
An article on kangaroo-hunting appears in the
current number of Scribner’s Magazine. There are some
fine things in it The writer mentions that a kangaroo
will sometimes rip up a dog with “a single stroke of his
hind hoof.” We have heard a new-chum speak of a work-
ing-bullock’s fore-paw—a kangaroo’s hoof is, we presume,
about the same style of thing. The article also says that
a kangaroo will sometimes “ leap upon a horse’s haunches,
seize the rider about the neck from behind, and drag him
from his seat, and then woe betide the unfortunate wretch
for his chances are small indeed.” Good iron 1 We would
fike to see a picture of this. One of the illustrations
shows an old-man kangaroo standing under a fern-tree,
but the American editors have evidently reckoned that a
fern-tree is an impossibility, so they have put it “flr"-
tree in the letterpress. The writer records that he let an
old doe kangaroo have both barrels of a breechloader, No.
3 shot, full in the flank at 20 yards’ distance, and she
never even faltered in her course. One feels inclined to
echo Punch’s small boy—“ Yah, never hit it!” At 20
yards, No. 3 shot from a decent gun would admit
sunlight to the innards of a pig, let alone a soft-
skinned animal like a kangaroo. He also says that it
gave four men all they could do to struggle home under
the weight of the skin and tail of one kangaroo—adding
that they managed it by relieving one another occasion-
ally. This man should come out and relieve our champion
liar.

The racehorse Benzon was recently purchased
from Hales by Mr. Tyson, of Hay, N.S.W., for 400 guineas.
The new owner has managed to get the nag in very light
for the two chief events at Hay Show races (which he con-
siders a “ moral ’•), and expects to make a close thing of
the Melbourne and Caulfield Cups.

Gladstone has lost his eldest son. Twenty
years ago he brought this young man into politics,
just when Russell introduced his bright son, Lord
Amberley. Young Gladstone did not hit, and
retired, leaving the field for Herbert, to come
later, and he, too, is sapless. Amberley was clever,
but not political, and died young, after startling
the world with a heterodox book, in which his
able and sympathetic wife co-operated. FeW
statesmen’s sons tiirn out like Pitt after Chatham,
and Burke underwent the same disappointment as
Gladstone and Russell.
That enthusiastic scientist, the late Gerard
Krefft, of the Australian Museum, went to a
berth, which he had reserved on the Newcastle
(N.S.W.) steamer, and found therein a hulking
person stretched at full length. “Excuse me,
sir,’’said Gerard with all politeness, “but you are
occupying, in mistake, the berth which I had
taken.” “You can have it,” growled the other,
a surly brute, “if you are a good enough man to
take it from me.” Krefft, unwilling to engage in
personal combat on British lines, said: “You
will, I am sure, at least do me one favour, sir—
please hand me my bag of snakes, which you will
find at your head.” The bunk was soon vacant.
It is related with much gusto that while
X, an Australian statesman, was recovering from
an accident, some good-natured friends hired for
his use, at a livery-stable, a two-horse barouche at
an expense to themselves of £6 weekly, and placed
same at his disposal. X. continued using the
barouche for months and months, and they,
feeling the expense, endeavoured to diplomatically
substitute for the two-horse vehicle, a one-horse
victoria, which they hired at another stable.
Presently one of them saw X, and remarked that
they hoped he liked the victoria. “No,” replied
the old ’un, “ I much prefer the barouche, and
have continued using it,” “ And what have you
done with the Victoria?” they asked. “Oh,’’
calmly said he, “ I handed that over to Lady X.’’
Henniker Heaton is coming to Australia
before Christmas. By the way, we read in the
Journalist that at the annual dinner of London
Press Club, Mr. T. M‘Rendle proposed “ The
Visitors,” amongst whom was ye Henniker, and
related how, on a visit to Canterbury (Eng.) he
went into a Post-office and asked the postmaster
what sort of a member the town had. The post-
master replied that he had only one objection to
the hon. gentleman—that whenever he sent a
telegram from the office he wanted his name to go
as one word, and he would not send it. From
that day his (the speaker’s) estimation of the
postal-servants had appreciably risen. He thought
that a man who went in cold blood to a district
post-office, and then and there demanded, without
notice, that the postmaster should telegraph a
long, double-barrelled name such as “ Henniker
Heaton ” for one half-penny was, no doubt un-
wittingly, trying to commit a fraud on his native
land.
The N.S.W. Bush Missionary Society
wants funds. Reading this we recall the first
Australian bush-missionary whose acquaintance
it was our honour to make —at Charters Towers,
many years ago. After dinner, one very cold
evening, a white-haired old gentleman—a tremen-
dous knife-and-fork man, who had been our
vis-a-vis at dinner—was sitting with a number of
miners before the hotel fire. One of this old
gentleman’s many harmless peculiarities was
gradually discovered by the company —he
“shouted” for himself only. Said he to the
waiter : “ Patsy, bring me—aw—some of that —
aw—aw —best rum of yours ; ‘Red-heart,’ I think
you call it.” Yes, the Rev, J. J. Westwood
merely “thought” they called it “Red-heart.”
He didn’t know, you see, for certain. Blessed
are the innocent, for they shall be Australian
bush-missionaries.
RHEUMATISM versus MICROBE
KILLER.
294 Castleresgh-street, June 25, 1891.
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co., Gentlemen,—For
forty years I have been a sufferer from rheu-
matism, and latterly in the most aggravated form,
often having acute attacks which made me almost
helpless for days, insomuch that it was with the
greatest pain and difficulty that I could move
about at all, and only able to get up stairs on my
hands and knees. A friend who had tried the
“Microbe Killer” with beneficial results sent me
a jar of the medicine, which I took, and I have
now taken three jars of “Microbe Killer” and
the result has been a great wonder to myself and
my friends, for now I can get about freely, with-
out pain. Igo up and down stairs without any
trouble, and I am greatly improved in health
every way. In fact my suffering from rheumatism
seems a thing of the past, and I can recommend
the “ Microbe Killer ” to anyone with confidence,
knowing what it has done for me. Several have
tried your medicine on my recommendation for
other diseases —and one a cancer patient —with
the same beneficial results. —Yours respectfully,
MRS. WHITAKER.

Queensland Coalition Government will not
introduce one-man-one-vote, which Griffith says
he supports, but which MTlwraith denounces.
The French system of second ballot will probably
be offered as a cure by the Coalition. Without
one-man-one-vote there would be no cure. Out
the Coalition must go.
Parkes has not for twenty years Minister!'
ally occupied so galling a position. Instead of hi s
usual team of servile noodles, he has in what by
courtesy is called “his” Ministry, some strong
men who will not be brow-beaten. And, instead
of being able to carelessly drag along at his heels
by one string 70 or 80 docile dullards, with rings
through their noses, he has to deal with scores of
independent private members having opinions of
their own.
Governor Kintore circularised the pro-
minent Adelaide butchers, asking them to tender
for the honor of Government House meat custom.
At scheduled rates, we suppose ? One butcher
replied by a polite memo., stating that he “ never
tendered for the supply of gentlemen’s houses.”
Another, it is whispered, offered to serve the
vice-regal household gratis, provided “my missis
has the right of private entry to all the swell
parties and 1 e-vees.”
Quong Tart, Sydney’s representative
Chinese, gave a christening-party, and to it were
bidden, among many others, old Sir John Robert-
son and his young friend, Tom Slattery, M.L.A.
When the clergyman began the service, the grey-
haired old Jack reverentially went right down on
his marrow-bones, and, bending his head, remained
in that uncomfortable attitude until he felt
thoroughly tired. Looking up, he observed that
Thomas, in an endeavour to make the best of
both worlds, was “only half kneeling down”—
i.e., he was on one knee and was resting his elbow
on his bent leg while the clergyman got through
his interminable task. Jack hereupon picked up
his big walking-staff, which was lying near, and
brought it down smartly on Mr. Tom Slattery’s
skull. The latter winced severely, rubbed his
head, and said nothing, but when the service was
finished he complained and said : “Sir John, you
hurt me very much indeed.” “Well, I meant
to, you infernal rascal,” snorted old Jack—“ you
came here to a religious ceremony, and if you
haven’t the blankity-blank good grace to san-
guinary well go down on your adjective marrow-
bones you deserve to have your carmine head pro-
perly stove in.”
SEGENHOE,

In an election for Evelyn (Vic.), many
years ago, no less than three candidates were suc-
cessively returned, each of whom became insolvent
ere he had been many weeks in the House. The
next member was Gatty-Jones, a well-known
solicitor, who on the hustings was implored by
the crowd not to “go broke”for.at least a month.
Jones did not become insolvent, but he was thrown
front his buggy, broke his leg, and did not take
his seat for months.

The other day a man, obviously in great
pain and almost incapable of speech, came before
a Sydney S.M. charged with indecent language.
He explained that his jaw had been broken by
the arresting policeman. The magistrate calmly
remanded him to gaol for a week, refusing
tendered bail 1 On that day week he was again
brought up, when the police evidence having been
heard, the charge was at once dismissed by
another stipendiary. What has the Minister for
Justice to say to a functionary who commits to
gaol, under such circumstances, on a trivial charge,
a citizen who offers bail? The judicial eccentrici-
ties of this police-magistrate are beginning to
arouse much attention.
Use BaiHo’s Baking Powder.
The result of the Upper Hunter (N.S.W.)
election is a big joke against that much-advertised
squatter, W. E, Abbott, who must have written
hundreds of letters within the last few months
to the town and country press in his self-
chosen crusade against “wicked agitators.”
These lengthy epistles seemed to pop up
simultaneously everywhere in the city and
country papers; in fact, W.E.A.’s pen was
übiquitous. His yarn was always the same old
yarn—that the heart of the people was dead
against the labour-agitators, and that he was the
impersonation of their real Codlin. A low, com-
mon, vulgar agitator named Williams —a shearer,
a shearer’s cook, a rouseabout, a fencer and
splitter, a horsebreaker, an all-round bushman —
took up Abbott’s challenge, and to almost every
letter of the latter appeared a vigorous reply over
the signature of T. H. Williams. The scorn of
W.E.A. was magnificent; his prophecies of how
the people would “show this man” were
numerous. Now, he, the lordly Mr. Abbott,
has the pleasure of handing over to the
mere worker his free railway-pass. Even at
Abbott’s own door the labour man polled
within 14 votes of the great squatting father to
his people. But Williams had to work hard for
it, and in the late terrible weather and in a dis-
trict veined with creeks, this was no small feat.
Here is a record :—At Stewartbrook on a Satur-
day night addressing a meeting ; away on horse-
back for 40 miles with 44 swollen crossings of
sinuous streams to swim, raining hard, to Scone,
where he rested on Sunday night; on Monday to
Aberdeen to address a meeting, and back to
Scone; on Tuesday to Merriwa (50 miles), and
then on to Cassilis for the polling-day; back after
the close of the poll to Scone—7B miles ! The
country needs men of such grit among its represen-
tatives.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
Dr. Neild, of Melbourne, delivered a lec-
ture recently on the general habit of taking too
much medicine, and, in the course of his remarks,
he admitted that he had probably killed a good
many patients during his career while groping
with a bottle and a blister among their innards in
search of some complaint that probably wasn’t
there. No doubt he has —every doctor has. The
worst feature in the profession is that whenever
the physician meets some new and astonishing
case which he can’t fathom he has, nevertheless,
to look as if he knew all about it, and plunge
wildly at the disease with a quart of evil-smelling
medicine. If he admits that he doesn’t under-
stand it, the patient will go off to some other
doctor who doesn’t understand it either, but who
says he does. Therefore, the only thing to do is
to seize a piece of paper and write the customary
dog-Latin paragraph, which runs something like
this; “Hyd. Car., 11. B.Z. Slush gr. 111. Pot
Gulph X 3 Dash Blast B 3 gr. Hyd. Cuss % & lod.
Slop 3",” and softly clean the patient out of two
pounds two shillings. And then when he drinks
it all, and comes back feeling worse, the correct
caper is to look at the outside of his chest through
a bit of stove-pipe with a frill on it, or to gaze at
his back through an ear-trumpet, and then the
physician looks as if he was staggered by the in-
formation he has acquired, though he has only
seen the patient’s skin and a fly walking on it;
and he feebly writes something in a book and
charges some more. Most doctors hesitate about
telling the patient what is the matter with him,
and this proceeding is described as “ professional
reticence. ’ The cause of it is that in about 20 per
cent, of the cases there is nothing the matter at
all; and in 40 per cent, more the patient has pub
the’doctor off the track by sheer lying; and in
about 20 more the M.D. can’t explain, through
sheer ignorance. And, anyhow, about half the
patients never stump-up, and when the matter
lies between a doctor who doesn’t know and a
patient who doesn’t pay things are about even.

Tales of Old Sydney,
No. IX.— The Skeleton Banquet.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When, in the seventh year of the reign of
His Most Gracious Majesty, that superlative
blackguard, George IV., his bosom friend, the
Most Noble the Marquis of Sligo, sold the bril-
liant racehorse, Skeleton, to Prince Esterhazy—
lie who, according to that veracious chronicler,
Mr. Barney Maguire, was on a certain distin-
guished occasion, “All joo’ls from his jasey to his
di’mond boots,” the aristocratic and riff-raff cir-
cles of the United Kingdom, expressed surprise;
judge, then, of the feelings which were excited
among the same distinguished sections of British
Society when the news spread, within a month or
two, that the Prince had disposed of him to an in-
significant colonist of Botany Bay. Eminent
though Mr. Alexander Riley’s position was in
Sydney as a merchant and a grazier, he was of
little note, indeed, we may say, of no note at all
in his native land ; and that he should have had
the audacity to negotiate and to complete success-
fully the purchase of that noble grey stallion
whose turf career had been of exceptional lustre
was little short of appalling. How dare . a
mere colonist, a huckster of rum and so forth,
rob England of one of her brightest equine
ornaments ? And how amazing was it that Prince
Esterhazy should have been so little considerate
of what was due to the prestige of the nation
which had so hospitably entertained him, in con-
senting to a transaction of the kind ! Neverthe-
less, the remonstrances expressed in Bell’s
Weekly Messenger, the Times, the Morning
Chronicle, and notably by “ Nimrod ” Apperley,
in his turf gossip, were without avail, and Skele-
ton was duly shipped to Port Jackson. Apperley
might fume and fret bitterly over the loss to
English racing stock of this notable stallion (and
that he did so take it to heart we heard from his
son, when he was stationed in Sydney in the early
Fifties buying horses for the Honorable East
India Company’s Service), but the sentiments of
the distinguished sporting writer kicked the beam
when weighed in the balance against Mr. A,
Riley’s golden guineas. Even princes get hard-up.
# * #
To say that horsey and sporting opinion in
the colony reached the pinnacle of excitement on
the announcement being made public that Skele-
ton, the famous Irish performer, was actually
includ d in Botany Bay’s population, is to under-
state the case. Every man who had the least pre-
tensions to equine knowledge went wild over the
news.
Now they were going to have some sport !
Now, the national pastime of England was to
have a chance of becoming properly implanted in
Australian soil ! Now, would the young bloods
of the colony be able to emulate the praiseworthy
examples of the scions of the Home aristocracy
and gentry, and own, and be ruined by, pedigree
stock! There had been, of course, in the forty
years of the colony’s existence, some attempts to
base upon the popular belief that racing improves
the breed of horses, an annual meeting or two.
Sydney, Windsor, and Campbelltown all had their
periodical contest, and matches for purses, more
or less heavy, were by no means infrequent. Still,
these prior attempts at the introduction of the
noble pastime into the new world had not been
characterised by remarkable success. Connoisseurs
had been at no loss to explain the fact by the
absence of any sire of notable qualities and per-
formances —the “ stables” were, in short, possessed
only of weeds.
Now, all this was to be changed. Skeleton was
going to call into existence a very different state
of things, and visions were glowing and hopes ran
high, of close matches, remarkable finishes,
magnificent fields, and all the rest of the accessories
to turfites’ pleasures. From Government House
and the Bent-street Club to the low pot-houses
and convicts’ huts, the intelligence of Skeleton’s
arrival stirred the hearts and imaginations of the
people. The Rileys had done big things for the
country—one of the things was building the Rum
Hospital—but the general consensus of opinion
was that the purchase of Skeleton was about the
biggest. That was in the first flush; then, very
unaccountably, so it seemed, feeling grew less
buoyant.
» * «
The following conversation, if reported,
Would have explained the change :
“ I say, Warphy,” said Flicker, of the Lands
and Survey, in the smoking-room of the club one
day a week after Skeleton’s landing, “have you
seen that new importation of the Rileys ?”
“What, the grey stallion ? Faith, I have, and
I don’t think old Alexander ever made a better
bargain, either for himself or for this portion of
His Majesty’s Dominions, than that same, if all I
hear about his performances is true.”
Mr. Robert Jenkins, a young cattle-breeder and
a famous memoriser of pedigrees and perform-
ances, here entered the room, and caught Warphy’s
response. “Talking about Skeleton? Suppose
you are,” he said. “ Everybody seems to be doing
the same, and I do think there is some reason for
interest being taken. If his owner meets with
proper encouragement, it’ll not be the last of
many such importations, I’ll warrant.”
“ Seen him yet, Jenkins ?” queried Warphy,
eager to get an opinion first-hand of the horse’s
points from so competent a judge as Mr. Jenkins.
“ Yes : just left the stables, walked up with Mr,
Richard Jones and young Bob Campbell. Mr.
Jones, by the way, is very glad Skeleton is not a
partnership property, but I don’t think his judg-
ment on the point is as trustworthy as it would be
with regard to a cargo of sugar or Irish manufac-
tures. He is the only man whom I have heard
express regret that Mr. Riley has laid out his
money on the horse. ”
“What’s your personal opinion of the nag,
Jenkins?”
“Simply this, that I saw few such animals at
home, and certainly none out here, that combined
in the same degree the best qualities of a race-
horse and a stallion. He has got shape, substance
and bone. Short legs from the knees, and good
and large to the fetlock. He is a beautiful horse,
and as powerful as beautiful. He and his stock
will prove a mint of money to the country. Then
look at his performances ”
“That’s what I would like to know something
about,” said Warphy.
“ Well, Hall, of the Monitor,’’replied Jenkins,
“ hang the fellow’s impudence ! sent round to me
for the loan of some Calendars, as he wished, so
his message ran, to prepare a list of Skeleton’s
doings. Of course 1 wasn’t going to take the
trouble to analyse the books for him, and I was
equally disinclined to lend them to him, so I re-
turned answer that if he would compile the list
from other sources, I’d compare it with the
Calenders. I’ve heard nothing of the matter
since.”
“Still,”said Warphy, “you must know some-
thing about the doings of the horse, Jenkins 1
Surely you can give us some information.”
“Well, I’ve suggested to Riley he should issue
a card to owners of stock with full particulars, and
he has promised to do so, and it is probable till
that is printed, I shall have to depend for precise
knowledge of the animal’s career on my general
recollections of what I have read in the home
sporting papers, and—well, to tell you the truth,
1 have jotted a few points down just for my own
satisfaction.” With this Mr. Jenkins took out his
pocket-book and favoured his friends (the group
had been increased by the addition of two or three
other interested gentlemen) with certain particu-
lars of the animal’s turf-career.
“ He was bred;-” he began, “ by the well-known
Mr. Kelly in 1820 out of the famous Walter
Raleigh mare, by Master Robert, whose sire
Buffer was in demand for every stable in the king-
dom. Ho first ran on the Curragh of Kildare
under the name of Chanter, he was then not quite
two years old, and came in second and third to
horses he afterwards beat.
“ In 1823, he was bought by the Marquess of
Sligo, who altered his name to Skeleton ; and in
October of that year he won the Kirwan Stakes of
50 guineas each, beating Mr. Caldwell’s filly,
Mary Anno, three years old —his former success-
ful antagonist; Lord Sligo’s filly, Penitence, four
years old; and Mr. Battensby’s horse, Johnny
Raw.
“ In April, 1824, he won the Wellington Stakes,
of 30 guineas each, on the Curragh, beating Mr,
Kirwan’s bay colt, Prince Hohenlohe ; Mr. Hun-
ter’s chestnut, Rolla; and Mr. Irvin’s chestnut
horse, Petworth. On the following Saturday, the
24th, he also took the King’s Plate, of 100 guineas,
beating Mr. Watt’s colt, Munster; Mr. Dixon’s
colt, Giles; and Mr. Battensby’s Irishman.
“ In 1825, he won, in June, the Sweepstakes on
the Curragh, beating Mr. Caldwell’s horse, Noble;
Major Kirwan’s filly, out of Lissey ; Mr. Doolan’s
gelding, Paddy from Cork; and Capt. Hackett’s
Kate.
“In April, 26, he won his chief victory, beating
the celebrated Miss Foote for the Wellington
Stakes, other horses being Blake’s Byron, the
chestnut colt, Soldier, the colt O’Hara, also owned
by Lord Sligo, and Studdart’s chestnut filly, Fanny.
Now, I don’t think much of the mere winning a
race unless you defeat a powerful opponent, but
when he put down as he did in this race, Miss Foote,
he put down what, according to the best opinion,
was the swiftest thorough-bred in all Ireland. It
was in April, I see, that he won the Wellington of
50 guineas each, beating Mr. Hunter’s Hypocrite,
Mr. Kelly’s Drone, his brother; and Mr. Watt’s
Mount Loftus ; and on the following Wednesday
he ran for the challenge of the Kirwan of 50
guineas each, carrying as the winner of the
Kirwan Stakes, 61b. extra. He came in second
then —the last race he ran. Last year he was only
entered for one or two English races, but Sligo
having no confidence in his trainer, sold him
before competing to Prince Esterhazy, from whom
Riley purchased him.”
“ A very notable performer,” said Flicker, “no
doubt, but ” —Flicker always was a sceptical
wretch—“that makes it the more singular that so
remarkable an animal should have been allowed
to go out of the United Kingdom. I can hardly
believe it is the genuine Skeleton— it is not the first
time by a dozen, so I’ve heard, that horses have
been sold for foreign parts with pedigrees that
were first-class and performances that were bril-
liant, the solitary defect in their history being
that pedigree and performances were alike fic-
titious !”
Flicker’s remark excited a little laughter, but
more dissent. But, without meaning it, he had
thrown a damper on the enthusiasm with which
Skeleton had been welcomed to New South Wales.
The seed-grain of doubt grew into a mighty tree,
and there came a time when, three or four years
later, it overshadowed every one of Skeleton’s
progeny. Once people’s minds had been directed
into that channel it did seem strange that a horse
of his quality should have been permitted to
leave England.
» • •
As generally happens, the persons most
interested in the settlement of a doubt or a verifi-
cation of a matter which is in dispute were the
last to hear of it. Mr. Riley and his manager,
Mr. W. H. Dutton, of Raby Estate, were, till the
infection of disbelief had spread widely, in ignor-
ance of the circumstance that anybody had flip-
pantly or. seriously chosen to cast a doubt as to
the authenticity of the stallion so carefully housed
at Raby. Had they heard, they would, it is to be
presumed, scarcely have then withheld from the
public of the colony this letter :
Mansfield-street, London, 16th July, 1828.
Sir,—l regret that I have not the whole pedigree of
Skeleton at present within my reach, but I will state all I
do recollect; He was the best horse of his year in Ireland,
when Lord Sligo purchased him, and by the calendars you
will see his many successful performances.
Lord Sligo had a horse called Arrogance, of the same
year of Skeleton, who won the King’s Plate at Doncaster
when four years old, and ran over the course in as short a
time as ever it was done before or since; I can assure you
that, from public and private running, I am satisfied that
Skeleton was much faster, and in every way superior to
Arrogance.
His own Brother, Drone, now five years old, is the
fastest in Ireland ; he won the Kirwans twice, and isa very
fine animal. Their sire, Master Robert, was decidedly the
best of his year, having beat Friday who was sent to Eng-
land ; and was there considered so good a horse.
If my opinion is of any advantage to have, I feel confi-
dent in the favourable view in which I look on Skeleton.
Master Robert was got by Buffer, his dam Spinster, by
Shuttle, great dam by Sir Peter, great gt. dam by Bour-
deaux. gt. gt. grand dam Speranza, own sister to Saltrem,
by Eclipse. Buffer was got by Prize Fighter, dam by
Florizel. Skeleton’s dam was got by the celebrated Sir
Walter out of the famed Miss Tooley', &c., for which see
Stud-book. Mi-. Kelly, who bred Skeleton, has the finest
filly in Ireland, own sister to him, who is considered the
best of her year, and has won twice that she started. In
short, I think you very fortunate in having got Skeleton,
his blood is excellent, his performances very superior, and
his shapes speak for themselves.
(Signed), Rohbrt Browne.
To A. Riley, Esq.
Mr. Robert Browne was Captain-ranger of the
Curragh, and a cousin to Lord Sligo, and his testi-
monial to Mr. Riley’s veracity would, if published,
have settled the doubts that had arisen. Unfor-
tunately, the owner of the stallion and his
manager were ignorant of any discredit being
thrust upon Skeleton’s hona-fides.
* # *
Skeleton’s groom was an Irishman named
Bennet—Henry Bennet. He had arrived in the
Countess of Harcourt, Bunn, master, in 1822,
under sentence of life for an offence “ not stated,”
having been found guilty in Dublin City on the
ninth day of January of that year. In height he
was five feet five-and-a-quarter inches, and in
complexion he was fresh and freckled. His hair
was brown, and his eyes hazel; and under the
sub-heading of “general remarks ” his parchment
record stated: “Cast in left eye.” A mason’s
labourer by trade, by that ingenious arrangement
of the System which, in.the majority of cases, put
the square man into a round hole, he was
assigned by the Board to Mr. Riley, of Raby, as a
farm hand, and proved himself a decidedly useful
groom.
It was in '2B that Skeleton was put in his
charge, with many promises of floggings if he
failed in his duty, and with no promise at all of
reward if he more than performed it. Now,
being not altogether brutish, Bennet conceived
for the animal that fine affection which so often
grows up between a horse and his master or
attendant. There was more than a spark of vice
ih the stallion. He was controllable only by two
or three of all who had ever handled him, and he
had killed his man “at home.” Harry Bennet,
after a tussle in his loose-box, mastered him, and
Skeleton thereafter never failed to notice his
presence and permitted the groom to treat him as
though he were but a lady’s palfrey caressed by his
mistress’ hand. The care of the animal gave to
Bennet that living subject of interest for want of
which so many convict natures grew nearly, akin
to the brute. He took a pride in his charge’s
strength, and worried over his occasional ailments
as mothers trouble over their dearest child. Once,
if not twice, during every night he passed from the
loft where he slept to the loose-box to see if
Skeleton was all right. He learnt by rote the
horse’s pedigree, and knew every available detail
of the events in which he had been concerned. It
was his one regret that Skeleton was not to be
raced in the colony and that he could not have the
gratification of preparing him for a victory. On
every possible occasion to Mr. William Riley
(“Angora Goat Riley”), Mr, Dutton, and to
every visitor to the estates, he would converse as
long as they would let him on Skeleton’s temper
and capabilities, and “as fit as a fiddle, as hard as
nails, as sound as a bell, as handsome as a pic-
ture,” and the kindred phrases wtih which the
lover of the horse is accustomed to express his
panegyrics were constantly on his tongue.
Through his belief in Skeleton’s merits, he
became rather a bore indeed, did Bennet, to
everyone of his convict associates. His brain had
taken a twist. The consideration that he was
thereby saved from twists of a more pernicious
kind did not, of course, occur to the men when
they got sick of being dosed with Skeleton as an
ingredient in the morning hominy, in the noon-
day soup, and in the evening damper.
Bennet had been sent into Camden one day
with a message relating to one of the ladies of
Skeleton’s harem, and in the bar of the Camden
Inn (still extant, but then a newly-established
element of civilisation) he was discoursing as
usual on Skeleton when one of James Macarthur’s
men told him that he wished Skeleton was dead.
“ ’Tain’t notbink but Skel’ton, Skel’ton, Skel’ton,
all day long, w’en we sees yer, Bennet. We’re
sick of Skel’ton—he’s only an ’oss, all said an’
done. Wish ’e were dead ! I’ve a good mind to
salt ’im down an’ eat ’im !”
“ There’s some bosses which is better’n some
men !” quoth Bennet, in indignant reply. “Skele-
ton ’e’s worth a hundred times a lag like you !”
“ Lag yerself, an’ if I were Mr. Dutton, I ’udn’t
trust you with an ’qss. Yer’d steal the skin of
’im—an’, ’sides, it ain’t Skel’ton at all: the true
Skel’ton never came ’ere at all, so Mr, Ann’bal
ses. ’Tis an ’oss which is ’ere under false
colours.”
“Wot say?” demanded Bennet; “I’ll tell Mr.
Dutton wot Mr. Annibal ses, but I tell you that
the man which ses that I’d steal an oat from
Skeleton is a liar an’ a peachin’ lag !”
Than the latter term of opprobrium nothing was
more offensive in the convict vocabulary. Many
a transport was proud of having been “sent out,”
but ‘ ‘ a lag ” was a reproach to even a Macquarie
Harbqurite or “Black Norfolker,” and the tender
sensibilities of a representative of either class of
permanent ruffian would resent, with tenfold
bitterness the imputation of being “a lag” that
“ peached ” upon his fellow convicts.
When verbal courtesies of this sort were ex-
changed, there could be, of course, but one sequel
—blows. One of the prettiest fights that one
could desire to see then took place between Riley’s
Bennet and Macarthur’s Barnes then and
there in the barn ; and so spirited was it that con-
stable Higgs—he was afterwards an overseer with
the Blue Mountain Road-gangs—having been
sufficiently entertained by the display of pugilistic
science, acting like an emissary of Imperial jus-
tice, locked both up in the watch-house. Skeleton
was within an ace of kicking the brains out of
Bennet’s locum tenens that night, for he missed the
attentions of his accustomed groom.
Major England, of the Fourth, or King’s Own,
and Mr. Hannibal Hawkins Macarthur were the
presiding magistrates the next court-day. Bonnet
—audacious fellow ! took exception to Mr. Macar-
thur’s presence on the Bench. “Yer ’n interested
party, Sir.”
Mr. Macarthur laughed interrogatively. “ How
so, prisoner?”
“Yer said as Skeleton wor not Skeleton, sir,
an’ that’s wot caused the row. ”
“The charge is creating a disturbance in a
public place, and that you, being pris’ners of the
Grown, were drunk on licensed premises ! ” said
Major England,
“ The row took place over Skeleton, an’ nuthin’
else ! ” doggedly repeated Bennet. “ An’ Barnes
there said as ’ow ’e would like to salt Skeleton
down an’ eat ’im, an’ yer an interested party.
Mr. Macarthur, sir, an’ for he said as ’ow yer said
as Skeleton worn’t Skeleton at all.”
Major England’s head moved towards Mr.
Hannibal Macarthur’s, and Major England’s lips,
a second later, inflicted the punishment. “ Forty
lashes for the record, and 25 for insolence to pre-
siding magistrates, Pris’ner Bennet. Prisoner
Barnes is discharged with a caution that unless he
keeps out of disreputable company he will be
severely dealt with. Next! ”
It seems to be the rule of nature that affection
for a loved object shall bring suffering in its train.
Bennet’s experience was not exceptional. His
fondness for the stallion had won him 65 lashes,
and the chance of getting 25 more from Mr.
Dutton on his return, for being absent without
leave for 24 hours.
* # #
That chance, however, to do Mr. Dutton
justice, was not taken advantage of, for he did not
flog Bennet. He was about to do so when Bennet
told him what he had heard, and Dutton, always
of a litigious disposition—as tenant of a farm, he
once carried on a suit-at-law against himself as
trustee of the farm property—resolved to find
out the reason for Mr. 'Hannibal Macarthur’s
statement that Skeleton was not Skeleton. A
convict’s evidence on oath was, by the majority
of landed proprietors, constantly dismissed as
valueless, yet in every relation of life, and in
every department of business, squatting and com-
mercial magnates were guided by unsworn convict
testimony. As a magistrate, Dutton would have
rejected Barnes’ evidence if it were tendered in a
case before him, but he was none the less prepared
to act upon the mere report of another convict of
what Barnes had said. He sought an early oppor-
tunity of visiting Mr. Hannibal Macarthur at
Brovvnlow Farm, where he was then residing.
“Look here, Mr. Hannibal”—it was “Mr.
Hannibal” and “Mr. James” nearly always;
‘ ‘ Mr. Macarthur ” was generally reserved —the
Hon. John was living at Hambleton Cottage, but
the law had pronounced him mad—for references
to Captain John. “ What’s this your man Barnes
says—”
“I have no man Barnes,” retorted Mr. Mac-
arthur.
“ Well, Mr. James’ man, Barnes. What is this
that he says about Skeleton ?”
“You forget yourself, Dutton, in asking me
what any Gbv’ment man says. Gov’ment men
say anything!”
“ But Barnes says that you declared that
Skeleton is riot what he professes to be.”
“ Once more I decline to be held responsible for
what a Gov’ment man says !”
“But did you or did you not say anything to
justify anyone thinking that you do not believe
that Skeleton is the horse we represent him to
be?”
“ Well, if I did,” rejoined Mr. Hannibal, “it is
only what everybody else is saying.”
“ What ! ”
“Haven’t you heard?” said Mr. Macarthur.
“Why, you don’t mean to say that you don’t
know that people have talked openly for the past
couple of years over the horse ? If I’ve heard a
doubt expressed once, I’ve heard it a hundred
times. People laugh when they see your advertise-
ment. Why is it that Bayly’s Whisker, and
Theorem, even Bennetong, are being patronised
by owners this year and last, when formerly
they only received Skeleton’s leavings ? ”
“ I can't say ! It never occurred to me to ask!”
“ Well, it’s because people doubt whether Skele-
ton is Skeleton ! You must have heard, surely ! ”
“xMo,” said Dutton, staggered. “I haven’t
heard, and I’m sure neither Mr. Riley nor Mr.
Will has. And I don’t think it is a friendly
thing of you, Macarthur, to have heard this state-
ment repeated and not to have put a stop to it! ”
“ Why, how could I interfere? I have nothing
to do with the horse, and I’m certainly not going
to take upon myself to vouch for anybody else’s
bona fides. Mr. Riley himself might have been
deceived. I don’t say that he or Will are purposely
deceiving others, and I don’t say that Skeleton
is not the horse that you say he is ! ”
Dutton turned away muttering that perhaps it
was as well for Hannibal’s own sake that he
did not, or otherwise he would make it hot for
him.
* * »
On his return to Raby, as he was putting
his horse into the stable, Mr. Dutton interrogated
Bennet,
“Bennet, is this the first time that you’ve
heard this report ?”
“ The werry fust, sir ! ’Never ’ad no idea there
was enythin’ ever said agen the an’mal. An’ ”
bursting into spluttering rage—“nobody as ’ud
speak the truth ’ud say he ain’t Skeleton ! Look
at them legs, an’ them shoulders ! An’as a stock-
getter, ain’t he wuth ev’ry guinea paid on ’is
’count? No —he’s Skeleton, sure enough. I’d
stake my life on it!”
“ Well, it’s very annoying. I’ll have to see
Mr. Will, and take steps to publish the documents
which prove the horse’s career. And, look here,
Bennet, as you got into that mess and that
flogging for the horse’s sake, come up to the stores
and get a fig of tobacco. I think you deserve it!”
In all ages and climes there have been alchemists
who have discovered panaceas, but it was reserved
for assigned masters in the convict times of Aus-
tralia to ascertain the cure-all properties of “ colo-
nially-grown ” at 2s. per pound—and twelve figs
to the pound. It could even unjustly-
lacerated 1 acks.
* * •
Nothing was more common (save floggings)
than a fight between two convicts ; and the con-
test, with its Court sequel, would not have been
thought of sufficient importance for a single line of
type in the papers had it not been for its connec-
tion with Skeleton. Skeleton’s first stock were
now rising three years, and as two-year-olds
several victories had been secured by his progeny
at the Windsor, the Campbelltown and Sydney
meetings. Nevertheless, and in spite of this
evidence of the quality of the stallion, the court-
case being mentioned by them, the Australian
and the Monitor—the Gazette had no allusion
to the circumstance—refanned the doubts to a
blaze, which was not lessened by a long advertise-
ment inserted by Mr. W. E. Riley in the first-
mentioned paper. ‘‘ Is Skeleton Skeleton ? ” was
one of the burning questions of the day,
and the discussion, for and against, exceeded
in yirulency even that excited by another great
topic of the time—Secretary Macleay’s right to 54
acres of land at Elizabeth Bay.
“ Tell yer wot, Bennet 1” cried Barnes, a month
after the fight, as the two met again, where the
Great Southern Road crosses the Nepean at the
approach to the township, “yer’ll get no more
mares next year. Yer’ll ’av that Skel’ton
sarved out to yer for rashings yet! Save uz
some o’ ’im, will yer ? ” Bennet shook his fist at
the speaker, but as he had no desire to get another
65 lashes, he confined himself to that impotent
action.
The words, however, rankled. In the evening,
in the men’s hut, he told the other assigned hands
what Barnes had said. The effect was not what
he anticipated. They had no special interest in
Skeleton to bind them towards a loyal support of
the Rileys’ assertion that their stallion was the
great Irish performer; and the sentiment expressed
by one Kerr, a farm labourer, was applauded.
“ If Skeleton was made into rashings’ meat, we’d
ha’ somethin’ juicy ! We never has enythin’ juicy
—our rashings are simply scrag, ain’t they ? ’

66 MARGARET STREET. SYDNEY.
“ ’Ear, ’ear,” cried the majority.
“ An’ for two pins, I’d be one to make ’im into
rashings ! ” continued the orator.
“Not while I’m alive!” impressively declared
Bennet. “ W’ether he’s the true Skeleton, or a
false Skeleton, he’s—he’s — my Skeleton——”
“’Ear ’im, ’ear ’im ! ’is Skeleton ! Bennets
Skeleton will stand this season at Baby Cow-
pastures !’ ” interrupted his tormentors.
“Yes, my Skeleton ! Eny one who touches ’un
’ud ’ave to do so over my body. Take warnin
now! But o’ course ye’re only foolin’. ’Oss
steal in’s deat h ! r ’ .
“Death or no,” exclaimed a sullen, gipsy-look-
ing convict —French. “ If I don’t get no better
rations than this stuff, I’ll do for Skeleton. _I d
sooner iia’ horse-flesh than maggoty beef. Ye d
better look out for your Skeleton now, Bennet,
and if ye don’t want your own skeleton devilled
with his bones, ye’d better not interfere !”
This talk was, Bennet thought, the merest
bravado, and undoubtedly it had originated in
jocularity. But a true word is often spoken in
jest, and light chatter may engender business of
a serious import. The ration-scale at Baby was
the regular Government one as to quantity, but as
to quality there was no official stipulation except
such as l)r. Wardell had intimated in his paper,
“as was implied by the acceptance by the
authorities of tenders which were too cheap by
half.” At the prison and factories and gang-
stations meat was chargeable at 3£d. per lb.;
and this “cat’s-food standard” (as the fighting-
doctor termed it) was naturally higher than that
issued by the average employer to his assigned
man. Baby was an average estate.
Station-cattle or sheep were not killed for the use
of the hands—not when Oowp isture stock brought,
at the Sydney butchers, 4d. or 4Ad. !—and Mr.
Chisholm, the dealer, supplied the establishment
at 6s. for each 12 men’s rations for a week. Taking
the ration at lilb. (the extra half-pound of meat
being granted in lieu of 11b. oaten or maize meal),
the quality of meat furnished in quantities of
12Glbs. for 6s. may be readily inferred. And the
idea of appropriating Skeleton’s carcase as an
assistance to the ration-scale, once implanted in
the minds of the assigned men, crew rapidly.
“I’ll teach old Dutton a lesson, an’ young
master ! They’d find it cheaper in the long run
to give us meat at tuppence a pound, than to
ration us on eight-hundred guinea stallions,” said
Kerr, the fellow' who had been the first to broach
the notion to the Baby hands.
“It’ud mean the nubbling cheat* for all that
wor in it.” .
“Better Jack Ketch than die o’ starvation!
’Sides, the masters would never take it to court
if we went to work properly. They’d ha’ no
evidence !”
* * *
The idea came to a head one Sunday night.
Mr. Riley and Mr. Dutton were both in the city,
and would not return for a day or two, and the
convict-overseer, who also acted as storekeeper,
had, as was usual with him when not watched,
sought to add to his own emoluments by issuing
short rations. There had been discontent in the
men’s huts and the kitchens that would have
been easily enough quenched had the owner or
manager been present, but, in their absence, took
volume and intensity. Ominous whispers went
round. “They’d teach the Rileys a lesson, they
would !”
The hands, some dozen or fourteen, employed in
the vicinity of the house were locked up at eight
nightly. But that night the overseer, knowing he
had offended the men, thought he would, for once,
risk leaving the doors unlocked. He did not
hanker after a thrashing. His decision was fatal
to Skeleton. _ . .
“ It’s long past lock-up time,” said one in the
larger hut. “ Where’s ‘ Bob Turned-up ?’ ” Bob
Turned-up, the overseer, was so called from the
number of times he had been put on his trial and
acquitted. .
“ He’s afeared to come near us to-night !
replied another. “ He’s afeared we’d dollop him.”
“ Then,” said the prime mover in this business,
“ We’ll go for Skeleton to-night?” And he dis-
closed his plan.
• # •
At midnight the hasps retaining the pad-
lock of Skeleton’s loose-box were wrenched from
the wood by the leader of a gang of men whose
faces were masked with pieces of canvas. The
doors were flung open, and the rogues found them-
selves face to face with Bennet. They had gone
noiselessly to work, but the groom had been on
one of his visits to the box from the contiguous
loft, when he had caught the sound of laboured
iureathing and of rending wood, and had at once
guessed the origin of the noise.
“It’s yer, is it?” he said coolly to the startled
'group, as the light of a lantern was thrown upon
his features. “An’ d’yer know it’s a bangin’fer
yer all ? ”
“ Not if yer can’t give evidence agen us ! lap
him, French, tap him ! ” .
French, the gipsy transport, lifted the timber
billet, with which h? had arriled himself, and
felled Bennet to the cobbled floor. He dropped,
groaned for a second, then passed into uncon-
sciousness. The thieves, after all, had fulfilled
his prophecy. They had only secured access to
the stallion over his body.
# # #
. One of the hands who had sometimes helped
Bennet in the care of Skeleton had been told oft
to get the horse but. Their scheme was to lead
the animal to the parting of the ways by the
Four Mile hut, on the banks of the river, and then
to kill him and cut him up on the atrip of
sandy margin. The tracks of the horse, if followed
up, would be lost at the stream-side, and all
signs of the butchering operation could be readily
removed. The opinion would be created that the
stallion had been stolen, and if the carcase were
salted down, it would be the easiest of matters to
supplement their ordinary rations:by this special
delicacy by the connivance of the shepherd at the
out-station. The principal difficulty was the
sißncing of Bennet. As for themselves, they
were all incriminated, and though one or two
might turn King’s Evidence if suspicion fell upon
the gang, yet they would do so under a penalty
more awful to the convict-mind than hanging
itself." Few Crown witnesses lived lottg after
testifying. ' . , , ,
Bennet was not dead* he was simply beaten
into insensibility. When the horse was brought
out, not without difficulty and danger to his
temporary attendant, the problem arose as to what
was to be done with the groom. Should they kill
him ? That would remove danger of detection by
Ins evidence ! And some were for killing him.
Men had been killed before for the sake of a ration
worth 3i’d. Why should they not kill him when
it was the case of a ration worth eight hundred
guineas ? But
“No ! ” declared the leader. “We’ll make the
horse carry him to the Four Mile. Shep’erd Dick
’ll keep ’im close for a fortnight, an’ we’ll dose ’im
■in the time so that ’is wits won’t be worth nothin’
—at least, not as evidence.”
Skeleton, not without inflicting some damage
with his heels on his guards, was laden with
Bennet, and the gang set forth. The stallion
must have known his rider, for no one but Bennet
had ever mounted him during his Raby career,
for he went quietly to the Four Mile and his
doom.
* # *
The overseer roused the hands out at s'oo
on the Monday morning, and though delighted to
find that they had apparently not known they had
not been locked in, was surprised at the general
disinclination to lap, with the customary eager-
mss, the breakfast hominy. Necessarily, lie was
not informed that only a couple of hours before
the assigned-men had banqueted off hastily-
roasted steaks from Skeleton’s flanks. Never had
convict a richer meal, and never one more satis-
factory at the price. To the assigned-man
generally “fed” on ration-* 1 mag,” a luxurious
horse-steak was the daintiest of morsels.
The remembrance of their feast, all hurried
though it had been, remained with the gang
during the day, and sustained them during the
demand made upon their energies by the discovery
“that Skeleton had been stolen by Bennet.” That
was the news which was despatched into Camden
and Campbell town and thence into Sydney. The
overseer could swear he had locked Bennet in
Bennet must have had an accessory outside, per-
haps a principal, for otherwise how could the
fastenings of the horse-box have been wrenched
away ?—and, and the horse was gone! For, in
the scrub by the Nepean, Bennet was kept care-
fully by Shepherd Dick, and dosed daily on
11 colonially-grown at two shillings the pound,”
and could afford no testimony either for himself or
against others. And, night after night, and,
morning after morning, did the gang and Shep-
herd Dick banquet off Skeleton. After the first
three servings the meat was salt. Still, it was
tasty and filling. For Shepherd Dick knew how
to mix a proper brine, and the meat lost none of
its savour through the circumstance that both it
and the salt, and the cask sunk in the Nepean
sand, were all stolen.
# * *
Dutton, tracing the horse’s tracks to the
banks of the Nepean, there lost them. He searched
west; he searched east; he sent Mr. W. E. Riley
to the north, and himself went to the south.
On his return he met Mr. William standing
with a mutual friend at the homestead door. 11 No
news? ’ was the muttered interrogatory and reply
from each as they faced each other.
“I’ve an idea!” whispered the mutual friend
when they reached the inner room. “Give out
that Bennet, misunderstanding instructions, has
removed the horse to Cavan.” Cavan was the
Rileys’ station in the Murrumbidgee areas.
“ That won’t find us the horse !” testily rejoined
Mr. William and Dutton, simultaneously.
“No, but I’ve heard—and you have heard, too
—that there’s a horse the exact spit of Skeleton,
shape and build, at Launceston. He’s to be had
for a hundred-and-fifty currency. I suggest you
buy him, and then give out that Bennet died at
Cavan. Bring the new stallion here ”
11 It won’t do!” declared Mr. Riley emphati-
cally.
“‘Well,” retorted the other, “ you have now the
credit of doing the same thing ! You may as well
do it, as be supposed to do it !”
* * *
We cannot say whether the Rileys acted
on their friend’s hint. But we do know that the
season after the date of the banquet, the pro-
prietor of Raby issued a circular distinctly in-
tended to set at rest the doubts as to the true
character of Skeleton’s loose-box. And we know,
also, that when an idiotic wretch, who strongly
resembled Bennet in facial aspect, was found
wandering in the bush near Camden, the Rileys
locked him up in the homestead for a time, and
then perceiving him to be quite witless, returned
him to the Assignment Board. As a robber of
men’s brains, “colonially-grown ” in combination
with overproof rum, could be backed against every
other invention of the devil. _ About the only in-
telligible utterance of the imbecile was “S-s-s!
Get over, Skeleton, get over ! ”
* * *.
Three years ago, the host at a small
friendly dinner-party in the Bathurst district,
pressed another slice of the sirloin upon the writer.
“Take another cut 1 ’Tis Blaokdown beef —you
have not often tasted a Juicier morsel! ”
There was an emphasis on the pronoun that in-
duced us to reply ; “Why, have you?”
“ Lord, yes ! When I banqueted off Skeleton !
Did I never tell you that story ? ”

THE STRONGEST ARGUMENTS.
A recent issue of the Wolverhampton Chron-
icle contains the following : “ We have for a long
time heard various reports of the wonderful effects
produced by the use of St. Jacobs oil in chronic
cases which have been given up as incurable, but
had always supposed these were enlarged upon
for advertising purposes, like all other patent ot
proprietary medicines, but the reports from all
our leading chemists must be considered one of
the strongest arguments which Can be advanced
respecting the value of any leading article of trade
in their line. Th'e uniformity of these reports
will be noted at once. All speak in the highest
terms of the value of St. .Jacobs oil as an outward
application for pain, 'idle oil is only advertised
to cure rheumatism, neuralgia, gout, sciatica, and
those bodily pains where an outward application
is used. The proprietors of this popular remedy
never advertise the oil to cure those diseases
which it will not actually cure. By so doing lliey
have gained the confidence of the people. We see
by the Liverpool papers that St. Jacobs oil is used
largely on board of nearly every steamship that
sails from that port. Many companies, including
the Cunard, White Star, and some of the South
American lines, do not e ven Consider their ships
ready for sea unless a sufficient supply of this
popular remedy is on board to last the voyage.
Latterly we are informed that all lI.M. troopships
leaving Portsmouth take a large supply of the oil
on board. The oil in yellow wrappers for veteri-
nary purposes is being used with equal y good
results by the leading minibus and cab companies
of London and provincial cities for use on horses
for sprains, sore shoulders, and general ailments
Where an embrocation is required.
“ A leading article in a recent issue of the
Chemist and Drcggist stated that St. Jacobs
oil had the largest sale of any medicine in the
world ; and when an article possesses the actual
merit which St. Jacobs oil undoubtedly does
possess, the proprietors are entitled to receive the
benefit of the enormous sale which this popular
remedy has.”
AN EXPERT’S OPINION.
A gentleman writes to the Editor of the Water-
bury as follows : “I called on M. J. E. Tucket,
jun., an expert watchmaker and engraver, who is
now with R. C. Richmond, of Grlendive, Montana,
whom I had met before in St. Louis. Knowing
his hatred for the Waterbury Watch, I was
surprised to see one of your sighs, Waterbury
Watches ’ hung in the window. Inquiry as to
this brought this in response : ‘ ¥es, I have been
down on the Waterbury watch all my life, and i
have never sold one half dozen of them until t.U.ing
the last month. My reasons for this change are
that I consider both the series ‘ J ’ and ‘ Ladies
Waterbury 5 to be reliable watches. I have tested
them carefully, honestly, and with all the exact-
ness that I, as a practical Watchmaker, could, and
I believe I do my customers Justice by selling
them the Waterbury watches. I believe the
company to-day to be trustworthy friends of the
legitimate watchmaker ; and I feel it to be doing
them a right, my customers a just act, and my
country a blessing, by discountenancing Swiss
watches, and instead selling the Waterbury.”
“I have in constant use on the railroad heri
nine Series ‘J’ besides thirty-two Rockford,
Elgin, and Waltham watches. Last month the
nine Waterburys made an average of 13b seconds,
not one being over 34 seconds off. The other
watches averaged 43 to 12 seconds. The Water-
burys were at a cost of sdol each, the other
watches from 25d0l to 125d01. We carry all kinds
of watches, but ‘ honesty is the best policy
always. I like the Waterburys ; they have been
weighed and not found wanting. I have decided
to recommend them, and to sell all of them i
can.”

HUDSON’S EXTRACT OF SOAP reduces the Hours of Labour.
Some Goblins of Sydney.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There is no one who could write this
sketch but ourselves. There is no one but our-
selves who, for years, have arisen at early dawn
watching the coming sun, as yet a golden glow
afar off, and listening to the gentle throbbing of
the great city’s heart. [We can run this kind of
copy off by the yard, and shall be happy to give
lessons in the art. N. B. —No previous knowledge
of literature required.— Ed. B.] Five o’clock
used to be our usual hour for getting up -
“ rising ” is, we believe, the correct term at Potts’
Point, Sydney,—occasionally, however, we have
been about earlier; but that, we think, was when
a sudden influx of the English nobility had
augumented, more than was quite compatible
with comfort, the company in our boiler.
# * *
Oh, but listen ! —Talking of boilers, —once
we, and the son of an English baronet, had a tank
on Gowper’s Wharf (a regular goblin haunt) be-
tween us. He had been recently staying at
Government House; but some venial diffi-
culty having arisen over five silver egg-
spoons and a soup-ladle, he was politely
“biffed out ” by the under-butler (previously
under suspicion), and straightway adjourned
to the Royal Hotel! Here he flourished
in great splendour, for a time; but after a
while appeared to have had a fierce wrestle
with an electro cruet-stand (which he took for
silver) and was defeated with great slaughter. No
proceedings, however, were taken, out of con-
sideration for the social rank his father held in
society (and the Marines); the management even
courteously informing him that he was at liberty
to take away his luggage. As this had, some few
nights before, been lowered down into a friendly
van with the aid of an hotel sheet and a clothes-
line, he raised his hat politely and, shouldering
his tooth-brush, left the premises with a proud
and supercilious air. He came to grief, however,
at his next place of residence. He went to reside
with a widow lady, who had two daughters and a
ouokoo-olook. He made fierce love immediately to
both fair daughters, and eloped with—the cuckoo-
clock. . . But there! Three months ! What
was that ? Not much to him, apparently, for he
came out as good as new, and when he went to
call on an officer at Dawes’ Point to borrow a
little silver, he assured him that he met “some very
pleasant fellahs there.”
« # *
To return to the tank, however. We
would impress upon the mind of the reader that,
sleeping in one is not always half so unpleasant
as the aesthetic mind might imagine. Once inside,
with the lid half on, and a pipe of glowing cigar-
ends in your mouth, there is a delightful feeling of
being out of the world ; of being beyond the reach
of the ladies who ask you the right time when
you have only a schnapper-lead at the end of your
watch-chain; and of the miscreants who arrest
your progress as you are walking along, cursing
the world, and entreat you to come into the
“little oi-l.” To us, indeed, these are quite plea-
sant reminiscences ; though now, of course, we
recline on a chair of buhl, work at a table of
ormolu, write with a pen of marqueterie, and put
on a new pair of yellow kid gloves after each
paragraph. The humours connected with that
tank were of the most delicious character. Times
without number, other decayed noblemen used to
take a fancy to it as a place of residence; upon
whose approach, however, we would imme-
diately put the bung in, and the would-be
tenant, seeing the hole plugged, would then
crawl away discomfited. But, one night, having
grown over-confident, we neglected to close
the aperture, and, in the small hours, we
were awakened by the sound of someone
scrambling up to the top. We (personally) were
considerably alarmed, and suggested uncondi-
tional surrender; but the baronet’s son (he said he
was descended from the Conqueror) was equal to
the occasion. Inserting his head through the hole,
he said, with dignity: “Look heah ! If you
attempt to force your way into these apartments,
I shall —ah—be compelled to give you in charge.”
* * *
Seriously, though, no one could write this
but ourselves. We have taken, like Mr. Silas
Wegg, “a powerful sight of notice” and know
the City of Sydney as well as we do our alphabet,
and a thundering sight better than we do our
multiplication-table. As some funny book says,
when we had got as far as 6 times 9 are 47, we
thought we had gone quite deeply enough, into
mathematics for all useful (and even ornamental)
purposes. We have a taste, you must know, for
the weird and wiclied, and are egregiously “gone”
on the grotesque. While Woolloomooloo makes
us merry, we are depressed by Darling Point and
Macleay-street. Oft at early dawn have we
paused on the pavement in front of Alberto
Terrace, and listened to the snoring of the
Oriental financiers. Golden goblins, some of these
Aaronshes and Sholomons, with eyes of softness
and hearts of stone ! It warms the cockles of our
heart to think, however, that not long ago we
“ bit the ear ” of one of them for a tenner. Dull
figures, these ; but we have a luxuriance of others.
Hold on till we wind them up, and you’ll see
how they’ll go it.
* # #
Cold, muggy and dark, with a damp pave-
ment, and the long, dim vistas of gas-lamps burn-
ing with a surly sluggishness. Dreary, too, you
must know, with an angry grey-black sky, and a
moist, unpleasant wind. We are in Forbes-street
now, and there is not a soul stirring. Stay,
though. A rumble of wheels. Now, mark well
the man in the cart that’s passing. That is the
goblin milkman of Woolloomooloo, We don’t
knovv what country he hails from, but his name is
Patrick MacDermody. We knew him once when
he was a fair and apple-cheeked milkman, who
used to drive in a blue necktie, and serve all
the young ladies’ schools and French laundresses.
In an evil hour he obtained a large connexion
amongst the local hostelries, and then he fell—not
only out of his cart every trip—but morally.
Whenever he serves at an hotel he always has a
drink —sometimes two ; so after attending to the
wants of five customers he is generally “ on his
ear ” at the bottom of the cart, with his face
covered over witli the nose-bag, while his little
nephew drives the rest of the round with his feet
on uncle’s stomach. But come lower down the
stree". Down the lane, nearly opposite the Fish-
market, we knock at a side-door. It is almost
immediately opened by a dapper little fellow in
his shirt-sleeves. This is “ Geordie.” Geordieisa
good-looking little man, but he labours under the
trifling facial disadvantage of only having one eye.
We asked him one day why he did not wear the
other one, and he said that he lost it at the battle
of Salamanca, fighting against the Sardines. We
traverse the bar, and pass through a good old
melodramatic sliding-dqor and enter a small par-
lour furnished with a sofa and a couple of chairs.
There are seven or eight people here, and
they are all goblins !
« » #
Look at the end of the sofa now.
-it- .i . ... , , o ii
You see that man with a frock-coat all
over mud, and a comically crushed bell-
topper, whose grimy hands, though he holds his
glass with both, are powerless to prevent the
spilling of the precious rum Well that s Henry
Darlon. Who s Henry Darlon. T i •
don tcherknow ? Henry Darlon, you must
learn then, is an English lawyer, who before
coming to Sydney, used to practise with great
success at the various Melbourne courts. He was
noted for his good looks, his talent, .
gentlemanly demeanour. One day he got a letter
sslS sffiti’Tura
Mr. H. D. always had lodgings somewhere, but is
never able to find them, so camps in the Domain
or the watchhouse—whichever comes the most
handy. He’s getting so mixed, now, that we
shan’t be surprised soon to see him give himself
up for drunkenness, defend himself with great
ability at the Central, and sue himself in the
District Court for his fees afterwards. That tall,
thin fellow next him with a barked “ ponum ” and
a glazed eye, whose clothes are vandyked and
arabesqued with the secrets of the culvert, is the
son of aburlyJ.P., and has, or had, a pub. in
New Zealand. He came over, no end of a dandy
after the Melbourne Cup, and for some days
the city was hardly large enough for him.
He stayed at the Domino Club first, tackled
dominoes, and was ingeniously outflanked at the
first onset. Rounding up his forces, he then
attacked icarU on a diet of absinthe and water-
biscuits and was repulsed with severe loss in the
first 48 hours. Beating a hasty retreat to another
hostehae he confronted billiards, but was speedily
cut up by the enemy s cannon. Then he sat
down to think. The loadstone rock was ahead,
He remembered him a friend in Woolloomooloo
Geordie. He sought him out, and at once
commenced to charge the rum with exceeding
fury. Then he became a goblin. Seated on a
chair facing.the door is a hard-faced little man,
with a glass of Old lom in his hand and a fish-
bag on his knee. This is an ex-champion light-
weight pugilist, who is now a bookmaker. He tells
us that he laid something or other to the extentof
s>*ty quid, as . bust him. He has
sought, therefore, consolation in the seductive
fumes of Old Top* and has become a goblin in
consequence. Three other rum-jars in more or
less a condition of dilapidation are jammed
together on the sofa, each one digesting his last
nun, and gloating over the prospect of another.
They have a curious, drawn, wizened look—these
dram-drinking goblins. They are silent, reserved,
and extremely dirty. In their eyes is an unholy
brightness, and if they breathe straight at you,
you die. # # #
Half-past five. Things are livelier now.
Phew ! what a crowd in the shed in which the
fish is sold. Towering above all is Inspector
Seymour, “ one of the most remarkable men of the
country, sir.” He gets up at 3, a human being,
He musters the scavengers at 4, a degenerated
mortal. He enters the Fishmarket at 5 and be-
comes a goblin. What • a sight! Though he
keeps them off with a six-foot wand, the dealer
goblins surround him, shriek at, dispute with
him, but he ip quite immovable. The fish are
knocked down to Nebuchadnezzar, Ginger,
Dutchy, and Goose Egg with stern impartiality.
The goblins are on the whole orderly, though
there is not unfrequently a dispute between
“Goose Egg,” Kate, and “Pair o’Soles”; and
Jf®, certainly cannot commend the elegance of
Frog s ” language. All these are dealing gob-
iins a 6 s are al ° 0 «* FlaBh in the Pan » •«Clipper ,”
and “Bruiser No. 2”; and all are much addicted to
re treshment-especially “ Mossmonger,” “Fiji,”
and .. Water Caskd - << Red Comforter,” “Mor-
ley>M and « Graball) » however) are reputed teeto-
tallers, though we wish we could say as much for
„ Little Bir |» and » OJd K ate.” Crossing the
market pro per, we encounter a hump-backed little
t leman of courteous aspect, quite a Beau
uaghnodo) and come acroSS) ' by the gate, a goblin
who had been beguiled of a prominent facial
feat babl a *f so at the bat ft e of Salamanca,
Have you had enough of goblins, or will
you see some more? Very well. Thanks. Yes.
We know our style is attractive. And after all,
it’s a mere trick. Avoid, as far as possible, the
use of semicolons, take plenty of run onwardly,
and there you are! Why, we met a man the
other day who told us that he had only been
practising writing sketches like these for a month
or so, and now he can make ’em fine. Fancy!
But to return to the goblins,
# * *
It i s (say) 11 o’clock, and we are stumbling
down a cobble-stone passage, leading out of
George-street, on our way to a cellar. Coming
- n f rom t be br i gb t sunshine the cellar is very dark ;
j n a f ew moments the sight gets used to the
g l o om, and there is revealed to the gaze quite an
array of goblins. You see that thin one who is
seated on three wine .cases piled one on the other,
and writing on a cask . That is a literary goblin,
He ia <• S cri bb lin g off a little thing or two,” and
chuckles to himself as his pen ploughs its way

so fast as could be desired, for first the top of his

b ] ows o ff the ashes, his pen drops amongst the

t be cask, he has a long search for matches, and
having at last found one, the head comes off as he
strikes it. This necessitates his proceeding to
tbe coun t e r for more, and then he has to cross

\Vhen he returns, he discovers that one of the

ouG and screwing up his goblin face in the

the rei of Nero which (here the top of his pipe
fe u o ff)lam localising for the Echo.” He then
wept, and told us he had a wife and seventeen chil-
dren, and all he earned last week was Ss. 9d.
We left him burrowing in the sawdust for his
pencil, and bedewing the Bodega with his tears,
Whisht! Mark this man with the fierce
moustaches and the bristling beard. Not long
ago he was the mildest of mild assistants in a
library. He was the pink of politeness to ladies
who had forgotten the “number of their folios,”
and it was generally considered that he had
settled down to a tea-and-toast existence. All of
a sudden — v'lan! he pitches his situation to
blazes, casts off his reserve as a garment,

Mr. Frank Isidore Power.
PRESIDENT OF THE GYMPIE TURF CLUB.
In the bustling community of Gympie there is
no face or figure better known than that of Mr.
Frank Isidore Power. Professionally, he ranks as
the most popular solicitor on the G-ympie gold-
fields, socially he takes the cake as principal
leader of the Race Club, as also other sports and
pastimes associated with the district. Mr. F. I.
Power is a native of Brisbane, and brother to Mr.
Virgil Power, the well-known barrister. In addi-
Electric Photo [?] SL SYONEY.
tion to his large practie?, Mr. Frank Power is
considerably interested in the mining and sugar-
growing industries of Queensland. He is one of
the two Australian representatives of the Great
Eastern mine Gympie, now owned by a large
and wealthy English Company. The Great East-
ern G.M. Co. has proved one of the few pheno-
menally rich gold-bearing properties at Gympie.
Mr. Frank Power, in his professional capacity, is
a commissioner of affidavits for Queensland and
New South Wales. Mr. Power scored a popular
win with Wanderer for the Town Plate at the
recent meeting of the Gympie Turf CbT),
It has been observed c.u. worst
feature about the boastful “self-mademan”is that
he looks so dreadfully like the sort of man that a
man like him would be likely to make.
The prospectus of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, whose director is Mr. T. S. Richardson, lies
before us. This institution has introduced a
novelty for public use in the shape of deposit-
notes, which can be purchased by anyone without
giving name or address, while trans‘errable with-
out endorsement, therefore can be paid away or
giv.en as security, while bearing interest at a high
rate during the time of currency. These notes are
issued from £lO upwards for any term at the fol-
lowing rates : 12 months, 7 per cent. ; 6 months, 6
par cent.; 3 months, 5 percent. But special rates
can be procured for longer or shorter periods.
The Bank on fixed deposits allows for 3 years, 9.V
per cent. ; 12 months, 8J per cent. ; 6 months, 7S
percent. ; 3 months, per cent., and during the
last four years a bonus of 1 per cent, has been paid
to all depositors in addition to the current rates on
their deposits. This is the only Bank in Austra-
lasia that thus divides its profits.
Dr. William Mathews, in the North
American Review, lays down the novel theory
that the true index of civilisation is—Suicide.
The more suicides the more civilisation. Just now
c.vilisation is moving onwards, and as a natural
consequence suicide is increasing with tremendous
rapidity. It is increasing, in fact, much faster
than population—so much faster that if the pre-
sent ratios be maintained the people of Europe
and America will one day cease to die natural
deaths, and one-half the population will be kept
busy fishing the remains of the other half out of
tanks and rivers and reservoirs. Germany,
France, and England are the most suicidal coun-
tries on the European continent; Spain, Ireland,
Portugal, Russia, and Turkey the least. The
highest pressure of self-destruction to the square
inch is in Saxony, where education is probably in
the highest state of perfection, and everywhere
the wealthy and cultured classes furnish, in pro-
portion to their number, about six times as many
suicides as the poor and ignorant. In other words, I
the predisposing cause is not want and hunger *
and misery, but weariness, champagne, rich 1
victuals, boredom, laziness, and the absolute iin- I
possibility of raising any new amusement to save '
a used-up rake from utter exhaustion. Know- '
ledge, money, fat, and weariness form the com-
bination which promises to decimate the world.

BETWEEN HELEN STEWART MULHOL

VICTORIA, by the Grace of Coil of the
(us.) United Kingdom of Great Britain and
Ireland, Queen, Defender of the Faith.
TO FREDERICK ALFRED MULHOLLAND,
of Sydney, in the Colony of New Soutli Wale?,
Commercial Traveller.
WE Command yon that within One Calendar
Month after service of this on you, inclusive of the
day of such service, yon do cause an appearance to
be entered for you in Our Supreme Court of
Queensland, then and Gere to make answer to a
Petition wherein HELEN STEWART MUL-
HOLLAND, claiming to have been married to
you, is the Petit oner, and a copy whereof, sealed
with the seal of our said Court, is herewith served
upon you. AND TAKE NOTICE that in default
.of your so doing the hearing of the charges thereof
proved in due course of law and the pronouncing
of sentence therein will be proceeded with in your
■absence notwithstanding.
WITNESS, The Honorable Sir Charles
Lilley, Knight, the Chief Justice of
s Queensland, this thirteenth day of April,
. in the fifty-fourth year of our Reign, and
in the year of Our Lord One Thousand
Eight Hundred and Ninety-one.
(1.5.) ALFRED DOWN.
Deputy-Registrar.

adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large —from 100 up to ISO feet frontages, w-ith long depths
thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
