The election of nine aldermen is chronicled
by a Moruya (N.S.W.) print as “ The peoples’
triumph.” That editor evidently thinks the
people are a cook.
Pear Bulletin, —I picked up at a book-
stall the other day a volume of “Selections from
the Poets ’’—American poets, mostly—published
in Boston in 1853. I found in it the following
depressing instance of the irony—one might
almost say the deliberately malignant irony—of
Fate: —
Writing on tub Sands.
I paused at early morn to trace
My name upon the sand,
Nor oared to think how soon the race
Of leaping waters would efface
The record of my hand.
And now—alas, that human pride
So slight a thing may quell !
With yonder works beneath the tide,
I feel that all I’ve wrought beside
May disappear as well.
And dare I deem that all this strife
Of thoughts within my soul,
These hopes with which my heart is rife,
These longings for a glorious life,
Will find a better goal?
The writer of the foregoing laudable lines may or
may not —these things depend upon taste —have
found the “ better goal ” he yearned for in 1853,
but he certainly came close to finding a very good
and substantial goal lately. What is his name?
Well, you would hardly guess that the same hand
which wrote the edifying stanzas quoted above
could have produced the scriptui’y satyriasis (see
dictionary passim) attributed to that most obscene
of “ ghosts,” Wilfred Murray. And, yet, most
likely you would—Satanic cynicism being (I
speak with all respect) a weakness of yours. Yes,
the author of “Writing on the Sands ” was W.
H. Hurlbert —he was innocent then, and hud no
occasion to spell his .name Ilurlbut in order to
throw an aristocratic English glamour over his
quaint and curious vices— whose brilliantly
horrible letters caused such a sensation in a Lon-
don court not long since, and had the effect of
making him vanish abruptly from London society
to enter on a wild-ghost chase for Wilfred Murray.
And now the authorities are on his track for per-
jury ! How ho must have wished, when the letters
were brought forth, that he had always confined
himself to “Writing on the Sands!” Young
men about to enter on the pursuit of poetry should
take warning from the fate of Hurlbut and begin
by being wicked —as Swinburne did. The chances
are that they, if they do so, will end, like Swin-
burne, by producing poetry warranted not to
bring a blush even to the cheek of mock-modesty.
Yours, &c., Creeve Roe.

Hero, the Frima Donna.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
VVhafc a fuss there was, to be sure, over
Hero, the prima donna Hero will do as
well as any other name—when that clever
young woman made her first appearance in
Barataria ever so many years ago! Every-
body said she was charming, and no two
people could agree upon the exact reason
why. The critics found fault with her sing-
ing, which indeed was not of a very high
order, but declared that her arch manner and
quaint oddities took them captive. The women
declared that she was a heap of affectation, but a
genius in matters of costume, and the men ran
after her like boys after a circus simply because it
was noised abi’oad that she was unconquerable.
Hero understood them all and played with them
all, and advertised herself by them and through
them to her heart’s content. She got up a boat’s
crew of girls and rowed stroke-oar herself, and did
it very well. She swam and dived in a very be-
witching and perfectly decent costume; she drove
the quaintest little ponies that ever were seen
with a free hand and a dashing style that com-
pelled applause, and, in short, she enjoyed a year
of unbounded popularity, dining which time she
bagged quite a nice little sum of money. Finally,
she sailed for her native land amid a chorus of
good wishes and invitations to return as quickly
as possible.
“Well,” said the popular Bill Bluster, best,
shrewdest, and kindest of operatic managers, but
vociferous withal and given to the use of violent
language, “ she’s all fake, by sheol, and nothing
but fake, but she’s deuced smart and a blanked
sight cleverer than those blooming idiots that
carry their crimson noses so high in the air. She
can just come back when she likes, by Ashtaroth,
and I’ll give her a dashed fine show by blankety
and dashety and destruction, I will.”
But Hero did not come back for half a-d zen
years, and when she did return poor Bill Bluster’s
kindly heart and noisy tongue had been still in
death for many a long day.
But that must have been a small matter to
Hero, who was not given to vex herself about
other people’s troubles. She came back to find
her name still remembered and her welcome still
warm for her. The change was in herself, and it
was a complete and startling transformation. The
Hero who had left was a slim, fragile little
creature with fair hair and big, mild eyes which
she invariably managed to cast down at the
moment when you were trying to make up your
mind as to their colour. The Hero that came
back was a short, stout, podgy woman much too
tightly laced, with bold eyes that all the world
could see were green as to their pupils and blood-
shot as to their whites, with hair the colour of tow
and a brazen, aggressive manner that extorted
compliment while it destroyed admiration. Worse
than all, the unapproachable Hero, who had
never been seen without her duenna at her first
visit, now travelled in company with a very
caddish low-comedian who was introduced as the
stage-manager of her troupe.
But her altered personality after all only affected
the few persons who were compelled to come into
immediate contact with her. The great question
as to what the public would say was in no way
affected by any diminution of the charm «he had
once exercised over her associates. The managers
might growlingly allege that the pleasant little
woman had turned into a greedy little shrew, but
they were quite prepared to put up with that if
she would draw full houses. This, however, after
the first curiosity of the playgoing crowd was
a?suaged, she proved unable to do. She required
more consideration than ever from all around her,
and gave less in return. She quarrelled with
everybody she came near, alienated all her old
friends and made no new ones, and, finally, in
default of any manager who would endure her
exactions, had to assume the direction of her own
affairs and the risk of her own productions.
A year or so of her own management led her to
bankruptcy, and bankruptcy led to various un-
comfortable disclosures. The very low comedian
had died, and had been succeeded by a young
squatter, by a not very young solicitor, by a
white - headed young tailor, and by divers
persons of more or less notoriety, and the
queerest of queer stories began to be circulated
concerning Hero’s domestic arrangements. For
a time it seemed as if she were quite out of the
race, but presently a sharp, speculative agent
tried his hand at her affairs, and succeeded in
steering her through a fairly profitable season.
He appeased her creditors with a composition,
rescued her wardrobe from the assignees, concluded
an engagement for her with a manager abroad,
and, finally, shipped her off to the East in com-
parative comfort.
Hero, however, had not learnt wisdom by
misfortune. She had not subdued any of the
propensities which had brought her to grief, and
her Eastern career only intensified her downfall.
Her fancy settled upon Gamboge, the basso, who
sad been for quite a long time, six months at
least, a faithful husband to a much-enduring wife,
and she did not leave the gentleman in doubt as
to the effect of his fascinations. She sent down
one night to the hotel where he was staying to
say that a native was slinking about near her
bungalow and that she was frightened. The gal-
lant Gamboge, who had already retired to rest,
arose and left his reluctant wife, who lias since
been blessed with but one fleeting glimpse of her
runaway spouse.
Mrs. Gamboge’s plight was a sad one. Her
private fortune, which had been large, had long
been dissipated by her husband’s extravagances,
and no money reached her after his disap-
pearance. When the hotel-keeper dunned her fer
lier bill she applied to Hero, as the nominal
head of the company, for some part of her
husband’s salary. Hero sneered at her. “ I
have no money,” she said, “I have assigned
everything to Eurly, the baritone. Go to him and
he will take you on in the chorus. You have a
good figure, go and show it on the stage and you
will be worth a salary.”
A day or two after, Mrs. Gamboge, bereft of hus-
band and home, stood shelterless in a public street,
her infant in her arms, without a penny, a crust
or a friend. Her trunks were detained for her
hotel-bill. She knew not where to get her next
meal nor where to shelter her child’s head, and as
she leaned crying against a lamp-post a laden car-
riage rolled rapidly by bearing her husband and
Hero who—yes, Hero, smiled and nodded as
she passed ! The lamp-light shone on the tears of
the deserted wife, and a gentleman stopped in
sympathy and astonishment.
“ Are you not Mrs. Gamboge? What are you
doing here? Is anything wrong! Don’t you
know that your husband’s going with the com-
pany to China! You will miss the boat. Can I
help you ?”
tt • •
The poor woman broke down and told her
sad story, and her new friend telegraphed a brief
abstract of the case to a great Baratarian theatrical
firm who had been not a little troubled in their
time by the peculiarities both of Hero and her
companion. The junior member of the firm re-
ceived the telegram, and was as prompt to aid as
though the runaway pair had left the happiest
memories behind them. Ho did not even await
the arrival of his partners before committing him-
self to a heavy responsibility. His return message
ran—“ Supply all Mrs. Gamboge’s necessities,
pay her passage to Barataria, America, or Europe
and charge this firm.” The poor woman decided
to come to Barataria, the firm adopted and
developed the action of their partner and presently
by their liberal aid Mrs. Gamboge was restored
to her friends in the land of the Stars and Stripes
from which she, like her husband and his frail
companion, had origiuallv hailed.
• *' •
Hero was not particularly lucky in the
rest of her Eastern tour, and a third smash re-
sulted from her operations. Those who followed
her movements wondered what she would do next.
She could not return to America, where there was
an injured husband, a deserted family, and an in-
different public to face. She could hardly expect
to get any lucrative engagement in England where
her recent failures would be widely known, and
she decided to return to Barataria, where she
evidently thought she could live down all
enmity and achieve her old renown. It was
a delusive hope. She succeeded in getting
a couple of new operas, but she was
plainly incompetent to bear any responsible
part in either, and after vainly trying her luck as
a comedienne, she relapsed into poverty and
penury. Gamboge, whose voice had lost its
beauty and whose singing was woefully impaired,
found himself at the time excluded from the stage,
and the whole, sad story ended, so far as Bara-
taria is concerned, by the departure of the couple
for Africa, their passages being paid by means of
a charitable subscription among the bookmakers.
It needs no prophet to foretell the fate of Gam-
boge and Hero. They are both in the prime of
life, but their physical powers are exhausted and
their accomplishments impaired. Indolence and
alcohol, vice and conceit, have between them
robbed the guilty pair of all claims to admiration,
and in all probability the tale so far truly told
will have a terrible and tragical termination.
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and du
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is fouqil
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin fs an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin wai
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
p''.a has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the pi-oduction of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushraan of the Far Nor h, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-she ler of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently reniailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and mqst
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kcuklmann, Esi(.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted,
m&re than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, blit I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectoratingevery day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in "March last. At the
end of three-months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, 1 am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
